The Mu'allil's Options: Changing or Transitioning the Proof
Author defines and distinguishes between taghayyur and intiqal for the mu'allil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between taghayyur and intiqal and apply the correct option when a premise is objected in a dialectical argument.

Core Idea
When a premise is objected, the mu'allil must either change the proof (taghayyur) or transition to a new one (intiqal).

In dialectical argument, the mu'allil (prover) does not simply give up when a premise is objected. He has two options: taghayyur (changing the proof) or intiqal (transitioning to a new proof). This lesson explains the technical distinction between them, based on the logical relationship between the old and new proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objected premise
In a jadal exchange, the mu'tarid objects to a premise of the mu'allil's proof. Recognize that the mu'allil has the option to provide another proof for the same claim.
2
Understand the general rule
The new proof must differ from the first in form, middle term, or mode (if categorical), or in the repeated part (if conditional). The difference can also be in the conclusion itself.
3
Distinguish taghayyur from intiqal
Taghayyur occurs when the new middle term or repeated part is necessarily implied by the old one (equality or absolute generality). Intiqal occurs when there is no such necessary implication (opposition, partial generality, or the new being more specific).
4
Apply the distinction
Given a scenario, determine whether a mu'allil's new proof constitutes a taghayyur (modification) or intiqal (complete replacement).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الممنوع لو كان مقدمة دليل المعلل فللمعلل وظيفة أخرى للتخلص عنه وهو إثبات المدعى بدليل آخر أى مغاير للدليل الأول وتغاير الدليلين المثبتين لشيء واحد يكون في الصورة أو في الحد الأوسط أو في الجهة إن كانا اقترانيين حمليين أو شرطيين واتحدا في النتيجة. وأما إن اختلفا فيها فيجوز أن يكون التغاير فيها وفي الحد الأوسط أو الأكبر. وأما إذا كانا اتصاليين أو انفصاليين فان اتحدا في الصورة والنتيجة ففي الجزء المكرر نفيا أو إثباتا وإلا فيكون في أي جزء كان. وأما إذا كان أحدهما اقترانيا حمليا والآخر شرطيا أو اتصاليا أو كان أحدهما شرطيا والآخر اتصاليا أو انفصاليا فلا بد في معرفة تغايرهما من معرفة رد كل منهما إلى الآخر والطالب الذكي يعرفه فلا حاجة إلى الإطناب ولا يساعده هذا الكتاب. لا يقال إن الدليلين المثبتين شيئا واحدا لا يكونان إلا متحدين في النتيجة لأنا نقول المثبت بدليل قد يكون عين النتيجة وقد يكون مساويا لها وقد يكون أعم منها فيجوز أن يكون المثبت بأحد الدليلين أحد هذه الثلاثة وبالآخر أحد الآخرين من الثلاثة. وذا إثبات ذلك المدعى بدليل آخر إفحام من وجه أما باعتبار أنه ليس باثبات الممنوع وإظهار صواب من وجه باعتبار أنه إثبات لما سبق له المنع. فان قلت: ما الفرق بين التغيير والانتقال مع أن كلامهم يشعر بعدم الفرق. قلت: لا فرق بينهما بحسب اللغة لكن بينهما فرق بحسب الاصطلاح وهو أن التغيير يكون في موضع كان فيه ما يتضمنه الثاني من الحد الأوسط في الاقترانى أو الجزء المتكرر في الاستثنائى لازما تحققه عند تحقق ما يتضمنه الأول وذلك بأن يكون بينهما مساواة أو يكون ما يتضمنه الثانى أعم مطلقا مما يتضمنه الأول والانتقال يكون في موضع لم يكن فيه ما يتضمنه الثاني من الحد الأوسط أو الجزء المتكرر لازما تحققه عند تحقق ما يتضمنه الأول وذلك بأن يكون بينهما تباين أو عموم من وجه أو كان ما يتضمنه الثاني أخص مما يتضمنه الأول. وبالجملة التغيير يشعر بكون الأول غير متروك بالكلية والانتقال يشعر بكونه متروكا بالكلية كما هو الظاهر من تتبع مواردها. فاعرف
And know that if the objected premise is a premise of the mu'allil's proof, then the mu'allil has another function to get rid of it, which is to establish the claimed matter with another proof, i.e., different from the first proof. The difference between two proofs that establish one thing is in the form, or in the middle term, or in the mode if they are both conjunctive categorical or conjunctive conditional and they agree in the conclusion. If they differ in the conclusion, then the difference may be in the conclusion and in the middle term or the major term. If they are both disjunctive conditional or disjunctive disjunctive, then if they agree in form and conclusion, the difference is in the repeated part negatively or affirmatively; otherwise, it is in whatever part it is. If one is a conjunctive categorical and the other a conditional conjunctive or disjunctive, or one is conditional and the other conjunctive or disjunctive, then to know their difference one must know how to reduce each to the other, and the intelligent student knows that, so there is no need for verbosity, and this book does not support it. Do not say that two proofs that establish one thing must be identical in the conclusion, because we say: what is established by one proof may be the very conclusion, or equivalent to it, or more general than it; so it is possible that what is established by one of the two proofs is one of these three, and by the other it is another of the three. And that establishing the claimed matter with another proof is an invalidation of the opponent from one aspect—namely, that it is not an establishment of the objected premise—and a showing of correctness from another aspect—namely, that it is an establishment of what had been objected to before. If you say: What is the difference between taghayyur (change) and intiqal (transition), when their statements suggest no difference? I say: There is no difference between them linguistically, but there is a difference convention: taghayyur is when what is implied by the second proof (the middle term in categorical or the repeated part in conditional) is necessarily realized upon the realization of what is implied by the first, such that there is equality between them, or what is implied by the second is absolutely more general than what is implied by the first. Intiqal is when what is implied by the second is not necessarily realized upon the realization of what is implied by the first, such that there is opposition between them, or generality in some respect, or what is implied by the second is more specific than what is implied by the first. In summary, taghayyur indicates that the first is not entirely abandoned, while intiqal indicates that it is entirely abandoned, as is apparent from investigating their instances. So understand.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage defines the options available to the mu'allil (the prover) when his premise is objected. It begins by stating that if the objected premise is part of his proof, he has another function: to prove the claim with a different proof. The author then explains how two proofs that establish the same thing can differ: in their form, middle term, or mode (if they are conjunctive categorical or conditional). If they differ in the conclusion, then the difference may also involve the middle or major term. For disjunctive proofs, the difference is in the repeated part. If the proofs are of different types, one must know how to reduce them to each other. The author then addresses an objection: two proofs for the same thing do not necessarily have the same conclusion; what is established may be the conclusion itself, its equivalent, or something more general. Then he introduces the key distinction between taghayyur (changing the proof) and intiqal (transitioning to a new proof). He notes that linguistically they are similar, but technically they differ: taghayyur occurs when the new middle term or repeated part is necessarily implied by the old one (either being equal or absolutely more general), while intiqal occurs when there is no such necessary implication (e.g., opposition, generality in some respect, or the new being more specific). The first indicates that the old proof is not entirely abandoned, while the second indicates complete abandonment.

تغيير
انتقال
معلل
إفحام

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what indicates that the mu'allil has performed a taghayyur rather than an intiqal?
A) The new proof's middle term is more specific than the old one.
B) The new proof's middle term is absolutely more general than the old one.
C) The new proof's middle term is in opposition to the old one.
D) The new proof's middle term is partially overlapping with the old one.

Key Takeaway
Taghayyur is a modification that retains the core of the old proof; intiqal is a complete shift to a new proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a debate, a mu'allil claims that 'Wine is forbidden because it is an intoxicant.' The mu'tarid objects that the 'illah of intoxication does not apply to nabidh because it is not as strong. Construct a taghayyur response (modify the 'illah) and an intiqal response (provide a different proof, e.g., a hadith).
?
Given two proofs: (1) All intoxicants are haram; nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore nabidh is haram. (2) All wines are haram; nabidh is not wine; therefore this proof fails. Determine whether replacing proof (1) with a new proof like 'All fermented drinks are haram' constitutes taghayyur or intiqal, and explain why.

Logical Relations in Proof Replacement: Equality, Generality, Specificity
Author defines the technical difference between taghayyur and intiqal based on logical relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the logical relationship between two proofs (equality, absolute generality, opposition, partial generality, etc.) as either taghayyur or intiqal.

Core Idea
The boundary between taghayyur and intiqal is drawn by necessity of implication: taghayyur when the second is necessarily entailed, intiqal otherwise.

The author provides a precise technical condition for when a new proof counts as a taghayyur (change) vs intiqal (transition). The key lies in whether the new middle term or repeated part is necessarily realized when the old one is. This lesson explores the five logical relations: equality, absolute generality (on the side of the new), opposition (tabayun), generality in some respect (umum min wajh), and the new being more specific.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two categories
Taghayyur requires that the new middle term or repeated part is necessarily entailed by the old one; intiqal does not.
2
Understand the relations for taghayyur
Either equality (e.g., 'intoxicant' and 'muskar') or absolute generality of the second over the first (e.g., 'drink' is more general than 'wine').
3
Understand the relations for intiqal
Opposition (tabayun: e.g., 'cat' and 'plant'), partial overlap (umum min wajh: e.g., 'teacher' and 'sick'), or the second being more specific (e.g., 'wine' is more specific than 'drink').
4
Apply to a real example
Determine whether replacing the middle term 'intoxicant' with 'fermented drink' in a proof about nabidh is taghayyur or intiqal.

8 min
Distinction Between Taghayyur and Intiqal in Dialectical Proofs
Author distinguishes between taghayyur and intiqal in dialectical proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between taghayyur and intiqal in jadal by analyzing the logical relationship between the middle terms of two proofs.

Core Idea
Taghayyur modifies a proof; intiqal abandons it entirely.

In dialectical debate, when a speaker offers two proofs for the same claim, we must determine whether the second proof is a modification of the first or an entirely new argument. The terms taghayyur and intiqal capture this distinction. This passage clarifies the technical conditions that differentiate them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle terms
In a categorical syllogism, focus on the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat) of each proof. In a conditional syllogism, focus on the repeated part (al-juz' al-mutakarir). These are the key elements that must be compared.
2
Determine the logical relationship
Check the relationship between the two middle terms: are they equal? Is one absolutely more general? Are they opposed (tabayun)? Do they share generality from one aspect ('umum min wajh)? Or is one more specific than the other?
3
Apply the rule
If the second middle term is equal to or absolutely more general than the first, then the second is implied by the first: this is taghayyur (change). Otherwise (opposition, one-aspect generality, or more specific), it is intiqal (transition).
4
Interpret the consequence
Taghayyur means the first proof is not entirely abandoned; it is modified or refined. Intiqal means the first proof is completely set aside for a new argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال إن الدليلين المثبتين شيئا واحدا لا يكونان إلا متحدين في النتيجة. لأنا نقول المثبت بدليل قد يكون عين النتيجة وقد يكون مساويا لها وقد يكون أعم منها، فيجوز أن يكون المثبت بأحد الدليلين أحد هذه الثلاثة وبالآخر أحد الآخرين من الثلاثة. فان قلت: ما الفرق بين التغيير والانتقال مع أن كلامهم يشعر بعدم الفرق؟ قلت: لا فرق بينهما بحسب اللغة لكن بينهما فرق بحسب الاصطلاح وهو أن التغيير يكون في موضع كان فيه ما يتضمنه الثاني من الحد الأوسط في الاقتراني أو الجزء المتكرر في الاستثنائي لازما تحققه عند تحقق ما يتضمنه الأول، وذلك بأن يكون بينهما مساواة أو يكون ما يتضمنه الثاني أعم مطلقا مما يتضمنه الأول. والانتقال يكون في موضع لم يكن فيه ما يتضمنه الثاني من الحد الأوسط أو الجزء المتكرر لازما تحققه عند تحقق ما يتضمنه الأول، وذلك بأن يكون بينهما تباين أو عموم من وجه أو كان ما يتضمنه الثاني أخص مما يتضمنه الأول. وبالجملة التغيير يشعر بكون الأول غير متروك بالكلية، والانتقال يشعر بكونه متروكا بالكلية.
It cannot be said that two proofs establishing one thing must be identical in the conclusion. For we say: what is established by a proof may be identical to the conclusion, or equivalent to it, or more general than it. So it is permissible that what is established by one proof be one of these three, and by the other proof be another of the three. If you ask: What is the difference between taghayyur and intiqal, given that their speech suggests no difference? I say: There is no difference in language, but there is a difference in terminology: taghayyur occurs where what is included in the second proof (the middle term in a categorical syllogism or the repeated part in a conditional syllogism) is necessarily realized when what is included in the first is realized; either by equality or by the second being absolutely more general. Intiqal occurs where what is included in the second is not necessarily realized when what is included in the first is realized; either by opposition, or by generality from one aspect, or by the second being more specific than the first. In summary, taghayyur indicates that the first is not entirely abandoned, while intiqal indicates that it is entirely abandoned.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exposition clarifying the difference between two technical terms: taghayyur (change) and intiqal (transition) in the context of two proofs for the same claim. The author first addresses an objection: it might be thought that two proofs for the same thing must have the same conclusion. He refutes this by noting that what is proven by a proof (the middle term or the minor premise) can be identical to the conclusion, equivalent to it, or more general. Thus, two proofs can have different focuses. Then he answers the question about the difference between taghayyur and intiqal. In language they are synonyms, but in the terminology of jadal they differ: taghayyur is when the second proof's middle term (in categorical syllogism) or repeated part (in conditional) is implied by the first proof's corresponding element, meaning the first proof is not completely discarded; it is merely modified. Intiqal is when the second proof's element is not implied by the first, so the first is abandoned entirely. For example, in a categorical syllogism, if the middle term of the second proof is equivalent to or more general than that of the first, it is taghayyur. If it is opposed or partially general or more specific, it is intiqal. This distinction is crucial for the etiquette of dialectical debate, as it determines whether the speaker is simply refining his proof or moving to an entirely new argument.

Key Terms
التغيير
الانتقال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a categorical syllogism, if the middle term of the second proof is equivalent to that of the first, is this taghayyur or intiqal?
A) Taghayyur
B) Intiqal
C) Neither
D) Both

Key Takeaway
The distinction between taghayyur and intiqal hinges on whether the second proof's key element is logically implied by the first, determining if the original proof is refined or abandoned.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given a scenario: In a debate, a scholar first argues that the Quran is mu'jiz (miraculous) because of its eloquence (balagha). Then he argues it is mu'jiz because of its prophecies (ikhbar). Determine whether this is taghayyur or intiqal, given that eloquence and prophecies are not generally related.
?
Construct your own example from fiqh: two proofs for the permissibility of ijtihad and identify if it is taghayyur or intiqal.

When may the questioner object?
Author explains the questioner's role after the proponent proves his claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule for when a questioner may object to a premise in a dialectical debate.

Core Idea
Not all premises are open to attack; only the disputable ones may be challenged.

In a dialectical debate, not every move is allowed. Once the proponent has presented his proof, the questioner has a right to object, but that right is not unlimited. This passage from the matn clarifies exactly when the questioner may intervene. Understanding this rule is crucial for anyone engaging in or analysing a jadal exchange – it prevents pointless objections and keeps the debate focused on real points of dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the roles
Find the Arabic terms for the questioner (السائل) and the proponent (المعلل) in the passage. Note that the proponent establishes a claim or premise using a proof or by invalidating a connection.
2
Locate the condition for objection
The passage says the questioner may object to a premise of the proof or of the invalidation 'مالم تكن تلك المقدمات بديهية جلية أو مسلمة' – as long as those premises are not self-evident or conceded. This is the key condition.
Self-evident (بديهية جلية) means the premise is obviously true without proof; conceded (مسلمة) means it is accepted by both parties.
3
Apply the condition to concrete cases
Consider two premises: 'The world is temporally originated' (disputable) and 'A thing cannot be both existent and non-existent' (self-evident). The questioner may object to the first but not to the second, because the second is self-evident.
4
Connect to earlier rules
The passage ends by stating that if the questioner does object to a permissible premise, the earlier detailed rules apply. These would include the proponent's obligation to defend the premise or show that its denial leads to contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيان وظيفة السائل بعد الاثبات. وعند إثبات المعلل مدعاة أو مقدمته بدليل أو بابطال السند السائل أن يمنع شيئا من مقدمات الدليل أو من مقدمات الإبطال مالم تكن تلك المقدمات بديهية جلية أو مسلمة. فإذا منع السائل شيئا من مقدماتهما يأتي فيه التفصيل السابق.
Chapter on explaining the function of the questioner after proof. When the proponent establishes his claim or his premise by a proof or by invalidating the connection, the questioner may object to something from the premises of the proof or from the premises of the invalidation as long as those premises are not self-evident or conceded. So if the questioner objects to something from their premises, the previous detailed rules apply.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is a chapter heading and explanation of the questioner's duties in the debate after the proponent has presented his proof. The author specifies that the questioner (السائل) has the right to object (يمنع) to any premise of the proof or of the invalidation, provided that the premise is not self-evident (بديهية جلية) or conceded (مسلمة). This limits the questioner's objections to only those premises that are disputable. If the questioner does object, then the rules detailed earlier (in the text) apply, such as the proponent's obligation to defend the objection by proving the objected premise, or by showing that the denial leads to contradiction, etc. This is a key procedural rule in jadal: not all premises can be challenged; only those that are not obvious or not agreed upon. This ensures the debate does not degenerate into endless trivial objections.

Key Takeaway
The questioner may only object to premises that are neither self-evident nor conceded; otherwise the objection is invalid under jadal rules.

Propriety of Switching Proofs in Debate
Discusses when a prover may switch to a different proof if unable to answer an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when a prover is justified in transitioning to a different proof during a dialectical exchange.

Core Idea
Switching proofs is justified only if the prover can still establish the claim.

In a dialectical debate, the prover (mu'allil) must respond to objections. Sometimes, he may consider abandoning his current proof and moving to another. Is this permissible? The passage explores this question, presenting two opposing views and settling on a nuanced rule: it depends on whether he is capable of defending the original proof or not. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opposing views
Locate the two contrasting positions: (1) Some say the purpose is only to establish the ruling, so any proof suffices. (2) Others say the purpose includes both the ruling and the soundness of the proof, so transitioning due to inability loses one purpose.
2
Find the correct view's condition
The author asserts that the correct view is conditional: if the prover is able to establish the prevented proposition (i.e., the objection does not undermine his core position), he may transition for a valid purpose. But if he is unable, transitioning is not justified.
The key phrase: 'إن كان قادرا على إثبات الممنوع مع ذلك انتقل إلى دليل آخر لفرض من الأغراض'.
3
Understand the distinction between conventional and real termination
Al-Talwīh explains that switching due to inability is merely a conventional end to avoid lengthy discussion, not a real termination. Real termination occurs only if the prover moves to an irrelevant proof to avoid the appearance of being silenced.
4
Apply the rule to the example of Prophet Ibrahim
The passage mentions Prophet Ibrahim (al-Khalil) as an example: he transitioned proofs for a higher purpose (to guide his people). Since he was capable of establishing his claim, his transition was justified.
This shows the practical application in Islamic prophetic debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصلاة والسلام مع نمروذ بأن الغرض إثبات الحكم فلا يبالي بأي دليل كان ، وعند البعض ليس ، واستدل عليه بأن الغرض إثبات الحكم مع صحة الدليل لا إثبات الحكم وحده فلو انتقل بموجه إلى دليل آخر لعجزه عن دفع الاعتراض فقد فات أحد الغرضين فلزم الافحام . والحق أن المعلل إن كان قادرا على إثبات الممنوع مع ذلك انتقل إلى دليل آخر لفرض من الأغراض كما في حاجة الخليل عليه السلام فهو موجه وإن لم يكن قادرا على إثباته وانتقل فليس بموجه لأنه مجز عن إثبات ما منعه السائل . قال فى التلويح : إن كون الانتقال إلى دليل آخر للعجز عن دفع الاعتراض من قبيل انتقال البحث مجرد اصطلاح من المناظرين لئلا يطول الكلام بالانتقال إلى دلیل آخر ، وليس بانقطاع البحث في الحقيقة لأن الغرض لما كان إظهار الصواب لزم جواز الانتقال إلى دليل آخر لأن المقصود ظهور الحق بأي دليل كان . نعم لو انتقل في معرض الانتقال إلى مالا يناسب للمطلوب أصلا دفعا لظهور إفخامه فهو يكون انقطاعا في الحقيقة انتهى ، ويحتمل أن يكون إشارة الى السؤال والجواب . أما السؤال فهو أن ههنا وظيفتين أخريين تحرير المدعى وتحرير أجزاء الدليل . وأما الجواب فهو أنهما داخلان في الاثبات ، وأما مقابلتهما في بعض المكتب بالاثبات فمن قبيل مقابلة العام بالخاص
Peace and blessings [may be] with Nimrod that the purpose is to establish the ruling, so one does not care by any proof it was. And according to some it is not. And they argued that the purpose is to establish the ruling with the soundness of the proof, not the ruling alone. For if he transitions to another proof due to his inability to repel the objection, then one of the two purposes has been missed, so being silenced becomes necessary. And the correct [view] is that the prover, if he is able to establish the prevented [proposition] despite that, transitions to another proof for some purpose, as in the need of al-Khalil, peace be upon him, so he is justified. And if he is not able to establish it and he transitions, then he is not justified because he is unable to establish what the questioner prevented. He said in al-Talwīh: Indeed, the transitioning to another proof due to inability to repel the objection is of the kind of transitioning of the investigation, merely a convention of the disputants so that speech is not prolonged by transitioning to another proof, and it is not a termination of the investigation in reality, because since the purpose is to manifest correctness, the permissibility of transitioning to another proof is necessary, because the intention is the appearance of truth by any proof. Yes, if he transitions in the context of transitioning to something that does not suit the required [claim] at all, to repel the appearance of his being silenced, then it is a termination in reality. End. And it is possible that it is an indication to the question and answer. As for the question, it is that here there are two other functions: editing the claim and editing the parts of the proof. As for the answer, it is that they are included in the proof. And as for their being contrasted with proof in some book, it is of the type of contrasting the general with the specific.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical commentary on the rules of debate. It opens by citing a disagreement: some hold that the sole purpose of debate is to establish the ruling, so any proof suffices; others insist the proof itself must be sound. The author clarifies that the correct view is that switching proofs is permissible if the prover is capable of establishing the prevented proposition but chooses another proof for a valid reason (like the prophet Ibrahim's need to argue differently). However, if he is unable and switches just to avoid being silenced, that is not justified. A quote from al-Talwīh confirms that transitioning due to inability is merely a conventional end to avoid lengthy discussion, not a true termination. The real criterion is that the truth must appear by any proof. The author then adds that there are two other duties: editing the claim and editing the parts of the proof, which are included under 'proof' and are sometimes contrasted as general vs. specific.

The Questioner's Objection and Prover's Responses in Jadal
Defines the questioner's options and the prover's counter-strategies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rules of objection and response in a dialectical exchange by identifying valid objections and choosing appropriate prover responses.

Core Idea
The questioner can block any non-self-evident premise; the prover must break the deadlock without circularity.

In the Islamic dialectical tradition (adab al-baḥth), after the prover has presented his proof, the questioner has specific rights and limitations. This passage from the Waladiyya explains exactly how the questioner may object and what strategies the prover may use to respond. Learning these rules ensures that theological and legal debates remain structured and productive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prover's claim and premise
Determine what the prover has established. The pronoun references in the Arabic clarify whether the prover has proven his claim or his premise.
The passage begins by parsing the grammar of the phrase about the prover.
2
Check if the premise is self-evident
If the premise is self-evident (either truly or by ruling), the questioner is prohibited from objecting to it. Identify such premises.
Self-evident premises are those that need no proof or are accepted by convention.
3
Select the appropriate prover response
When the questioner objects to a non-self-evident premise, the prover may choose from five strategies: re-proving the denied point, invalidating the necessary opposite, editing the objection, clarifying the school, or editing the claim/proof.
The passage lists these as the 'previous detail' referenced.
4
Recognize the termination condition
The exchange must end with one party incapacitated; infinite regress (tasalsul) or circularity (dawr) is impossible. Identify which party is silenced.
This ensures the debate reaches a conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل فى بيان وظيفة السائل بعد الاثبات وعند إثبات المعلل مدعاه أو مقدمته : الضمير الثاني إما راجع إلى المعلل كالأول أو راجع إلى الدعي ، والإضافة في كلا التقديرين لأدنى ملابسة بدليل أو بابطال السند السائل أن يمنع شيئا معينا من مقدمات ذلك الدليل أو من مقدمات ذلك الابطال مالم تكن المقدمات أو الشيء ، والتأنيث باعتبار كونه مقدمة بديهية جلية حقيقية أو حكما فاذا منع السائل شيئا معينا من مقدماتهما يأتى فيه التفصيل السابق من إثبات الممنوع باقامة الدليل أو بابطال اللازم لنقيض الممنوع فقط أو بتحرير الممنوع بحيث يسقط المنع أو بيان للذهب الذي يبنى عليه الممنوع أو بتحرير المدعى أو الدليل عند كون الممنوع تقريبا ، وأيضا للسائل أن يمنع لزوم السند للنقيض عند والتغيير والتحريرات ، فاما أن يعجز الملل فيفحم أو يعجز السائل فيلزم إذ لا يمكن جريان البحث إلى غير النهاية حتى يتسلسل أو يدور
Chapter on explaining the duty of the questioner after proof. And upon the prover establishing his claim or his premise: the second pronoun either refers back to the prover like the first or refers back to the claim, and the annexation in both estimates is for the slightest connection. By proof or by invalidating the evidence chain, the questioner may object to a specific thing from the premises of that proof or from the premises of that invalidation, unless those premises or that thing — and the feminine being due to its being a premise — are self-evident, truly or by ruling. So when the questioner objects to a specific thing from their premises, the previous detail applies: establishing the prevented by presenting a proof, or by invalidating the necessary from the opposite of the prevented only, or by editing the prevented such that the objection falls, or by clarifying the school upon which the prevented is built, or by editing the claim or the proof when the prevented is an approximation. Also, the questioner may object to the implication of the evidence chain to the opposite at the time of change and edits. Then either the prover is incapacitated and is silenced, or the questioner is incapacitated and is forced [to accept], because it is not possible for the investigation to proceed indefinitely such that it chains or circles.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exposition of the questioner's role after the prover has established his claim or premise. It begins by parsing the grammar of the phrase 'وعند إثبات المعلل مدعاه أو مقدمته', explaining the pronoun references. Then it states the questioner's options: he may object to a specific premise of the proof or of the invalidation, provided the premise is not self-evident (either truly or by ruling). If such an objection is made, the prover must respond using one of several strategies: re-proving the denied point, invalidating the necessary consequence of its opposite, clarifying the denied point to remove the objection, explaining the school underlying it, or editing the claim or proof. Additionally, the questioner may object to the implication of the evidence chain to the opposite. The passage concludes that the exchange must end with one party being silenced; infinite regress is impossible.

Key Terms
إبطال السند (ibṭāl al-sanad): Invalidating the evidence chain; showing that the proof's connecting link fails to support the claim. [Note: Standard in adab al-baḥth; similar to 'naqd al-dalīl'.]
بديهية جلية (badīhiyya jalīyya): Self-evident, either truly (needs no proof) or by ruling (accepted by convention). [Note: No significant shift.]
الممنوع (al-mamnū'): The proposition that the questioner denies or objects to. [Note: Standard term.]
تقريبا (taqrīban): An approximation; a premise not exactly stated but close to the intended meaning. [Note: May also mean 'for the sake of argument'.]

Key Takeaway
The dialectical exchange has rules: the questioner's objection must be to a non-self-evident premise, and the prover must respond appropriately; the debate must eventually end with one party's acceptance.

Invalidating the Sanad: Dialectical Moves and Proper Procedure
Shows the syllogistic form of invalidating the sanad and the questioner's counter-objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the dialectical exchange when a prover's evidence chain is challenged, identify the logical form of the sanad invalidation, and assess whether the prover's response adheres to qānūn al-tawjīh.

Core Idea
Invalidating the sanad only refutes the claim if the sanad is equivalent; otherwise the prover needs new proof.

In Islamic dialectic (adab al-baḥth), a common strategy is to attack the opponent's evidence chain (sanad). This passage explains the logical structure of such an attack and the proper counter-response. The commentator al-Mas'ūd critiques a particular response as outside the law of direction, showing the disciplined nature of dialectical exchange.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial syllogism
The passage presents a conditional chain: if the sanad is invalid, then the opposite is invalid; if the opposite is invalid, then the claim is established. Therefore, if the sanad is invalid, the claim is established. This is a valid hypothetical syllogism.
This syllogism is the core logical tool the prover uses to connect sanad invalidation to claim establishment.
2
Spot the questioner's objection
The questioner denies the minor premise: he does not concede that invalidity of this particular sanad leads to invalidity of the opposite, because the sanad may not be equivalent (ghayr musāwī). In other words, the sanad might not be a necessary condition for the opposite.
The objection targets the implication, not the truth of the premises.
3
Analyze the prover's options
The prover can either prove that the sanad is equivalent (i.e., prove the minor premise) or invalidate the sanad itself (showing it is not a legitimate chain). Both options require additional argumentation.
4
Follow the questioner's counter-objection
The questioner argues that any speech about the sanad is useless, because the issue is about the claim itself. This is a meta-objection: talking about the sanad does not directly refute the original claim.
The questioner uses a universal claim: 'Every speech about the sanad is not useful.'
5
Evaluate the prover's disjunctive response and al-Mas'ūd's critique
The prover tries a disjunctive refutation: 'If you mean speech about a non-equivalent sanad, it is prevented; if you mean absolute sanad, the major premise is prevented.' Al-Mas'ūd says this alternation is outside proper procedure (qānūn al-tawjīh) because it does not address the questioner's core point: that the prover's speech about the sanad does not refute the objection. The only valid responses are to bring a new proof or prove that the sanad is a necessary consequence.
The commentator's verdict shows the strict discipline of dialectical argumentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه اعلم أن تصوير إبطال السند هكذا إذا بطل هذا السند بطل النقيض، وإذا بطل النقيض ثبت المدعى ينتج إذا بطل هذا السند ثبت المدعى ، لكن المقدم حق والتالى مثله فللسائل أن يمنع الصغرى ثانيا، بأن يقول لا نسلم استلزام بطلان هذا السند لبطلان النقيض الجواز أن يكون السند المذكور غير مساو فللمعلل حينئذ إما إثبات الصغرى المذكورة أو إبطال هذا السند وللسائل هنا كلام آخر هو أن هذا كلام على السند ، وكل كلام على السند غير مفيد فهذا الكلام غير مفيد فللمعلل أن يردد ويقول إن أردت أنه كلام على السند الغير المساوى فهو ممنوع ، وأن أردت أنه كلام على مطلق السند فالكبرى ممنوعة ، وقد ردد فى الكبرى ويقول إن أردت أن الكلام على السند المطلق غير مفيد فممنوع ، وإن أردت أن الكلام على السند الغير المساوى غير مفيد فلا يتكرر الأوسط قال الشارح المسعود هذا الترديد ما لا يفيد المعلل أصلا لأن حاصل قول السائل أن كلامكم متعلق بالسند أنه رد عليه ، ولا يلزم من رد هذا رد المنع لأنه يحتمل أن لا يكون المستند المذكور من لوازمه ، فبقى على المعلل أما إثبات المعلل بدليل آخر أو إثبات كون السند لازما لمنعها : فظهر أن الترديد المذكور من طرف المعلل خارج عن قانون التوجيه انتهى
Notification: Know that the depiction of invalidating the evidence chain is like this: If this evidence chain is invalid, then the opposite is invalid; and if the opposite is invalid, then the claim is established. So it produces: If this evidence chain is invalid, then the claim is established. But the premise is true and the consequent is like it. So for the questioner [he may] deny the minor premise a second time, by saying: We do not concede the implication of the invalidity of this evidence chain for the invalidity of the opposite, because it is possible that the mentioned evidence chain is not equivalent. So for the prover then either to establish the mentioned minor premise or to invalidate this evidence chain. And for the questioner here another statement: This is speech about the evidence chain, and every speech about the evidence chain is not useful; so this speech is not useful. So for the prover he may alternate and say: If you mean that it is speech about the non-equivalent evidence chain, it is prevented; and if you mean that it is speech about the absolute evidence chain, then the major premise is prevented. And he alternated in the major premise and says: If you mean that speech about the absolute evidence chain is not useful, it is prevented; and if you mean that speech about the non-equivalent evidence chain is not useful, then the middle term is not repeated. Al-Shāriḥ al-Mas'ūd said: This alternation does not benefit the prover at all, because the gist of the questioner's statement is that your speech is about the evidence chain and it is a refutation against him, and it does not follow from this refutation the refutation of the objection, because it is possible that the mentioned evidence chain is not among its necessary consequences. So it remains for the prover either to establish [the claim] by another proof or to establish that the evidence chain is a necessary consequence for its prevention. Thus it appears that the mentioned alternation from the prover's side is outside the law of direction. End.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This is a note ('tanbīh') that explains the logical form of invalidating the evidence chain. It presents a syllogism: if the sanad is invalid, the opposite is invalid; if the opposite is invalid, the claim is established; therefore, if the sanad is invalid, the claim is established. The questioner can object to the minor premise (the implication that invalidity of the sanad leads to invalidity of the opposite) because the sanad may not be equivalent. The prover then can either prove that implication or invalidate the sanad. The questioner counters with a general objection: that talking about the sanad is useless. The prover tries to respond with a disjunctive refutation, but the commentator al-Mas'ūd argues that this refutation is ineffective because the questioner's point is that the prover's speech is about the sanad and does not refute the original objection. The prover's only valid options are to bring a new proof or prove that the sanad is a necessary consequence. Thus the prover's disjunctive response is considered outside the proper procedure (khārij 'an qānūn al-tawjīh).

Key Terms
السند
المقدم
التالى
قانون التوجيه

C) To deny the questioner's premise
D) To end the debate

Key Takeaway
The prover's disjunctive alternation is invalid; the only proper moves are to prove the sanad's equivalence or bring a new proof.

The questioner objects that temporally originated things are not necessarily created (sanad not equivalent). Identify why this response is outside proper procedure, using al-Mas'ūd's reasoning that the prover's speech about the sanad does not refute the original objection unless it proves necessity.

Recognizing Beneficial Objections in Dialectic
Explains when an objection to a premise helps the prover by admitting the claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an objection to a premise implicitly admits the prover's claim and thus benefits the prover.

Core Idea
An objection that admits the claim helps the prover.

In dialectical disputation (jadal), not every objection is harmful to the prover. This passage from the text explains a subtle but powerful case: when the objector's challenge to a premise actually contains an admission of the very conclusion the prover is trying to prove. Understanding this allows you to spot when an objection can be turned into a confirmation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and the premise under attack
Determine what the prover is trying to prove (mudda'a) and which premise the objector is challenging. This sets the stage for analyzing the objection's content.
2
Check for implicit admission of the claim
Examine whether the objection includes an admission of the claim in one of three forms: the claim is subsumed under the evidence chain, the evidence chain details the claim, or the evidence chain with another premise yields the claim. If so, the objection is beneficial.
The text identifies these three forms explicitly.
3
Apply the test to the example
Consider the example: 'The world is originated because it is changing.' If the objector challenges the premise 'it is changing,' does that implicitly admit that the world is originated? Since change implies temporality and thus origination, the objection indeed admits the claim.
This confirms the objection benefits the prover.
4
Determine the outcome for the prover
If the objection admits the claim, the prover gains an advantage: he can use the objection as support rather than defending against it. The debate proceeds with the claim reinforced.
This is the key practical takeaway for dialectical strategy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيان المنع الذى ينفع المعلل ( منع السائل مقدمة دليل المحلل قد لا يضر المعلل ) بل ينفعه (وذلك) أى عدم كون المنع مضرا ( إذا ذكر المانع منذا يشتمل الاعتراف بدعوى للطفل ) المستدل عليها بتلك المقدمة، وأما اشتماله على الاعتراف بتلك المقدمة فلا يوجد إلا عندكون السند ميانة وقد عرفت أنه لم يوجد بالاستقراء وكذا منع المدعى إستند يشمل الاعتراف به ، وإما منعه بسند يشمل الاعتراف بالمقدمة فلا يوجد لعدم جواز منع المدعى المدلل وذلك الاعتراف إما بأن تتدرج تلك الدعوى في ذلك السند أو بأن يكون ذلك السند تفصيل تلك الدعوى أو بأن يتقوم بذلك السند مع المقدمة الأخرى دليل ينتج لتلك الدعوى، والأول ) كما إذا قال المؤمن العالم (حادث أى مسبوق بالعدم لأنه متغير)
Chapter on explaining the objection that benefits the prover. The questioner's objection to a premise of the prover's proof may not harm the prover, rather it benefits him. That is, the fact that the objection is not harmful [occurs] when the objector mentions [it] in a way that includes an admission of the prover's claim that was sought to be proved by that premise. As for its inclusion of admission of that premise, it only occurs when the evidence chain is discordant, and you have known that it does not exist by induction. Likewise, objecting to the claim without a evidence chain that includes admission of it; and as for objecting with an evidence chain that includes admission of the premise, it does not exist because it is not permissible to object to the prover's claim [when] it is proved. And that admission is either that the claim is subsumed under that evidence chain, or that the evidence chain is a detail of that claim, or that the evidence chain together with another premise constitutes a proof that produces that claim. The first is like when the believer says: The world is originated, i.e., preceded by non-existence, because it is changing.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage is a chapter heading explaining when an objection from the questioner actually helps the prover. It specifies that an objection that does not harm the prover is one that implicitly admits the prover's claim. The author explains that such admission only occurs when the evidence chain is 'discordant' (perhaps meaning not equivalent), and notes that this is not found by induction. Three forms of admission are identified: the claim is included under the evidence chain, the evidence chain details the claim, or the evidence chain combined with another premise yields the claim. The example given: if a believer says 'The world is originated (preceded by non-existence) because it is changing,' the objection to the premise (changing) might admit the claim of origination because change implies temporality. Thus the prover is benefited because the objection confirms the core claim.

When an Objection Helps: The Support Acknowledges the Claim
Author explains when a questioner's objection (manع) benefits the arguer because the support (saned) acknowledges the claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an objector's support inadvertently acknowledges the arguer's claim and explain how the arguer can use this to his advantage.

Core Idea
An objector's support can turn an objection into a confirmation.

In dialectical reasoning (jadal), not every objection harms the arguer. As this passage shows, when the objector provides a support (saned) that includes an implicit acknowledgment of the arguer's claim, the objection backfires. We will explore the rule and its application through two classic examples from the kalam debate on the temporality of the world (huduth al-alam).

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection (manع)
Locate the premise being objected to by the questioner. In the first example, the objector challenges the minor premise 'the world is not free from motion and rest.'
2
Find the support (saned) for the objection
The support is the reasoning given to justify the objection. Here, it is 'as in the instant of its origination.' Recognize that this is the justification for why the world might be free from motion and rest.
3
Check if the support acknowledges the claim
Analyze the support's content. Does it implicitly or explicitly contain the arguer's claim? In the example, the support mentions 'the instant of origination,' which implies that the world has an instant of origination—i.e., it is preceded by non-existence, exactly the claim of huduth.
The acknowledgment can be by inclusion, detailed explanation, or being part of a proof for the claim.
4
Determine how the arguer benefits
Since the support affirms the claim, the arguer can either prove the objected premise directly or prove the support's equivalence to the negation of the objection. In either case, the sought proposition (huduth al-alam) is established.
This turns a potential defeat into a victory.

Section on explaining the objection that benefits the arguer (The questioner's objection to a premise of the arguer's proof may not harm the arguer) but rather benefits him (and that is) i.e., the objection not being harmful (when the objector mentions a support that includes acknowledgment of the arguer's claim) which he is using that premise to prove. As for the support including acknowledgment of that premise, it does not occur except when the support is incompatible [with the claim] and you have known that it has not been found by induction. Likewise, the objection to the claim using a support that includes acknowledgment of the claim, but as for objecting with a support that includes acknowledgment of the premise, it does not occur because it is not permissible to object to a proven claim. And that acknowledgment is either by that claim being included in that support, or by that support being a detailed explanation of that claim, or by that support together with another premise forming a proof that produces that claim. The first (is like when the believer says: The world is originated, i.e., preceded by non-existence, because it is changing: and everything changing is originated) and he proved the minor premise by (the world (with all its parts) is not free from motion) i.e., from existence in two instants in two places (and rest) i.e., from existence in two instants in one place, and everything not free from motion and rest is changing. So the Philosopher said, objecting to the second minor premise, and he specified it with the Philosopher to indicate that whoever holds the sought proposition as self-evident in reality or by ruling (i.e., it is from the necessities of his school or accepted in another way) it is not proper for him to object to a premise from the premises of the proof of that sought proposition. (We do not confirm its being free from them. Why may it not be free (the world) from them as in the instant of its origination?) i.e., in the instant of the world's origination; for the instant of origination is a single instant in which motion and rest cannot occur because each of motion and rest can only be realized in two instants, for motion is the body's existence in two instants in two places, and rest is the body's existence in two instants in one place as previously mentioned. I say: his statement 'Why may it not be free etc.' is a depiction of the objection without support, and the support in reality is the content of his statement 'as in the instant of origination' because it is equivalent to: 'Why may it not be that the world is preceded by non-existence in the instant of origination?' so it is free from them. And this support is equivalent to his claim, so there is no objection to it that in its conventional application as a support there is a problem, nor is there need to answer it by saying it is a comparison not an example, or that what is meant by support is what is added to the objection, whether it is a support or an illumination or a depiction. If he had said: 'Why may it not be that no part of the world is preceded by another existence?' it would be an example of an equivalent support. End. With the consideration that there is also a problem in the support being equivalent, so reflect deeply. (So this support contains) i.e., in this support there is acknowledgment of the world's origination because the world's origination is included in it, and it is the arguer's sought proposition. He also said: The second is like when that believer says to prove that minor premise: Every part of the world is definitely existing in a place. So by virtue of its existence in that place, either it is preceded by another existence in that place, so it is at rest, or preceded by another existence in another place, so it is in motion. So the questioner said: We do not confirm that exhaustive division. Why may it not be that it is not preceded by any other existence at all, just as the originated thing at the instant of its origination exists in a place and is not preceded by another existence at all? (And in this support there is acknowledgment of the world's origination because it is a detailed explanation of the world's origination.) In these two forms, the arguer alternates and says: Either prove the premises in question or prove this support for its equivalence to the negation of the objected proposition, and whichever it is, it proves the sought proposition, namely that the world is originated.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage defines a special category of objection (manع) in dialectical reasoning (jadal) that actually helps the arguer (mu'allil) rather than harming him. It begins by stating the rule: when the questioner provides a support (saned) for his objection that includes an implicit acknowledgment of the arguer's claim, the objection is not harmful. Three ways this acknowledgment occurs are listed: (1) the claim is directly included in the support, (2) the support is a detailed explanation of the claim, or (3) the support together with another premise yields the claim. The passage then illustrates with two examples from the classic kalam proof for the temporality of the world (huduth al-alam). In the first example, the believer argues: 'The world is originated (preceded by non-existence) because it is changing. Everything changing is originated. The world is not free from motion and rest, therefore it is changing.' The Philosopher objects to the minor premise (the world is not free from motion and rest) by saying: 'Why may it not be free from both at the instant of its origination?' However, the support for this objection — 'as in the instant of its origination' — contains an acknowledgment that the world has an instant of origination, which means it is preceded by non-existence. Thus the objection inadvertently confirms the claim. In the second example, the believer argues: 'Every part of the world is in a place. If it is preceded by existence in the same place, it is at rest; if preceded in a different place, it is in motion.' The objector says: 'Why can it not be that a part is not preceded by any existence at all, like the originated thing at its instant of origination?' Again, the support (the analogue of the originated thing) treats the world as having an instant of origination, thereby acknowledging huduth. In both cases, the arguer can either prove the objected premise directly or prove that the support is equivalent to the negation of the objection, and thus establish his claim. This passage teaches the student to recognize when an opponent's objection can be turned around as a confirmation of one's own thesis.

المنع
السند
المعلل
حدوث العالم

Using the Disputant's Objection as Supporting Proof (al-Sund)
Author presents two examples of sund in the debate on the world's temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a sund in a dialectical exchange and explain how it turns the disputant's objection into a proof for the opponent's claim.

Core Idea
A sund turns the disputant's own objection into a proof for the claim he tried to deny.

In dialectical debates, a skilled proponent can seize the disputant's objection and show that it actually supports the proponent's thesis. This is called a sund (supporting proof). The classic debate on the world's temporality provides vivid examples. The commentator reveals how the proponent uses the disputant's admission of origination as evidence for the world's createdness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proponent's thesis and the disputed premise
In each example, first find what the proponent aims to prove (the world is originated) and which premise the disputant rejects (the exhaustive division or the minor premise).
The disputed premise is called al-mamnu'.
2
Locate the disputant's objection
The disputant offers an alternative scenario that he thinks refutes the proponent's premise. In the second type: 'Why not say the part is not preceded at all?' In the third: 'Why not say change is by removal instead of acquisition?'
3
Analyze how the objection implicitly concedes the thesis
Examine the objection and see that it contains an admission of origination. For the second: describing 'the moment of origination' admits that the world has a beginning. For the third: removal is still an event (like ignorance after knowledge), so change implies an event.
The commentator stresses that even non-existential removal is an event.
4
Recognize the proponent's repurposing as a sund
The proponent accepts the objection but treats it as a supporting proof because it proves the desired conclusion. He then offers a choice: either accept the original premise or accept this sund, which is equivalent to the contradictory of the disputed premise.
The sund is called naqid al-mamnu'.
5
Verify that the sund establishes the conclusion
In both examples, either way the proponent wins: if the original premise is accepted, the conclusion follows; if the sund is accepted, the conclusion follows as well.
This shows the dialectical power of the sund.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني كما إذا قال ذلك المعلل لاثبات تلك الصغرى إن كل جزء من أجزاء العالم كائن في مكان ألبتة، فذلك باعتبار كونه في ذلك المكان إما مسبوق بكون آخر في ذلك المكان فهو ساكن، وإما مسبوق بكون آخر في مكان آخر فهو متحرك. فقال السائل لا نسلم ذلك الانحصار لم لا يجوز أن لا يكون مسبوقا بكون آخر أصلا كما أن الحادث في آن حدوثه كائن في مكان، وليس مسبوقا في ذلك الآن بكون آخر أصلا؟ ففي هذا السند اعتراف بحدوث العالم لأنه تفصيل حدوث العالم. وفي هاتين الصورتين ردد المعلل ويقول إما أن تثبت المقدمة الممنوعة أو تثبت هذا السند لمساواته لنقيض الممنوع، وأيا ما كان ثبت المطلوب، وهو أن العالم حادث. والثالث كما إذا قال المعلل لاثبات الكبرى الأولى لأن كل متغير محل للحادث، وكل ما هو محل للحادث فهو حادث، وأثبت الصغرى بأن كل متغير محل لأمر حاصل بعد أن لم يكن، وذلك الأمر حادث. فقال السائل لا نسلم الصغرى لم لا يجوز أن يكون تغير المتغير بزوال أمر كائن فيه؟ فيردد المعلل بين المقدمة الممنوعة وبين ذلك السند، فيضم لكل منهما مقدمة فيثبت المطلوب بأن يقول إن كل متغير إما محل لأمر حاصل بعد أن لم يكن أو محل لزوال أمر كائن فيه. والأول حادث بلا شك والثاني حادث أيضا فالمتغير محل للحادث. وبيان الكبرى الثانية أن كون الزوال أمرا عدميا لا ينا في كونه حادثا، ولا كونه صفة لشيء كالجهل بعد العلم.
The second [type] is when the proponent, to establish that minor premise, says: 'Every part of the world is necessarily in a place; so by virtue of being in that place, either it is preceded by another occurrence in that place—then it is stationary, or it is preceded by another occurrence in another place—then it is moving.' The disputant says: 'We do not accept that exhaustive division. Why is it not permissible that it is not preceded by another occurrence at all, just as the originated thing at the moment of its origination is in a place and is not preceded in that moment by another occurrence at all?' This supporting proof contains an admission of the world's temporality because it details the origination of the world. In these two cases, the proponent gives an alternative: either you accept the disputed premise, or you accept this supporting proof because it is equivalent to the contradictory of what you denied, and in either case the desired conclusion—that the world is originated—is established. The third [type] is when the proponent, to establish the major premise 'Everything changing is a locus of an event, and everything that is a locus of an event is originated,' establishes the minor premise by saying: 'Every changing thing is a locus for something that comes to be after it was not, and that thing is an event.' The disputant says: 'We do not accept the minor premise. Why is it not permissible that the change of the changing thing is by the removal of something that existed in it?' So the proponent offers an alternative between the disputed premise and that supporting proof, attaching a premise to each, and establishes the desired conclusion by saying: 'Every changing thing is either a locus for something that comes to be after it was not, or a locus for the removal of something that existed in it. The first is undoubtedly originated, and the second is also originated. Therefore the changing thing is a locus of an event.' And the explanation of the second major premise is that the removal, even though it is a non-existence, does not prevent it from being an event, nor from being an attribute of something, like ignorance after knowledge.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) explaining two types of 'sund' (supporting proof) in dialectical debate. It follows the enumeration style of the original matn. The author first presents the second type: the proponent argues that every part of the world must occupy a place, and either it is preceded by another occurrence in the same place (stationary) or in a different place (moving). The disputant objects that the part might not be preceded at all, giving the example of an originated thing at the very moment of its origination. The commentator notes that this very objection serves as a 'sund' because it implicitly acknowledges that the world is originated—it describes a moment of origination, thus conceding the conclusion. The proponent then offers an alternative: either accept the original premise or accept this supporting proof, which is equivalent to the contradictory of what was denied. In either case, the conclusion that the world is originated holds. The third type involves a different proof: all changing things are loci of events, and all loci of events are originated. The proponent's minor premise states that change is the acquisition of something new. The disputant objects that change might be by removal of an existing quality. The proponent then uses a compound argument: every change is either acquisition or removal; both are originated (removal is an event, as ignorance after knowledge is an attribute); thus all changes are loci of events. The commentary emphasizes that even non-existential removal can be an 'event' in logical terms (like ignorance resulting from a cause). This illustrates how a skilled debater turns a seemingly damaging objection into a proof that strengthens his position. The examples are rooted in debates about the world's temporality, a classic theological (kalam) argument.

Key Terms
السند
الممنوع
نقيض الممنوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the second type of sund, the disputant's objection about 'the originated thing at the moment of its origination' actually proves which claim?
A) The world is eternal.
B) The world is originated.
C) Change is impossible.
D) The disputant accepts the proponent's premise.

Key Takeaway
In dialectic, a sund is the disputant's own objection that inadvertently proves the opposite of what he intended, and the proponent uses it to win the debate.

Identifying Ghasb in Dialectical Refutation
Author defines ghasb and distinguishes it from other objection types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether an opponent's objection qualifies as 'ghasb' by assessing its timing and whether it targets an unproven claim with a claim of falsity.

Core Idea
Ghasb is a premature refutation of an unproven claim before the proponent defends it.

In Islamic dialectic (jadal), not every objection is permissible. The term 'ghasb' (usurpation) refers to a specific invalid objection where the opponent refutes a claim or premise that the proponent has not yet proven. Understanding this rule is crucial for conducting fair debates and for the proponent to know how to respond.

Guided Path
1
Locate the proponent's assertion
Identify what the proponent (mu'allil) is trying to prove. Often it is a claim (mad'ā) or a premise of a syllogism.
In the given passage, the proponent asserts that 'every changing thing is a locus for an event'.
2
Determine if the assertion is proven or unproven
Check whether the proponent has already provided evidence for that specific claim at the time of the opponent's objection. If the proponent has not yet argued for it, it is unproven.
In the example, the proponent states the premise but has not yet argued for it when the opponent objects.
3
Check the opponent's response
See if the opponent (sā'il) explicitly claims falsity (ibṭāl) of the claim and provides proof or a reminder. If the opponent simply requests clarification or asks a question without claiming falsity, it is not ghasb.
The opponent says 'We do not concede the minor premise' and gives a reason, constituting a claim of falsity.
4
Assess the timing
The objection must occur before the proponent has provided evidence for the targeted claim. If it occurs after, it is mu'āraḍa (counter-argument), not ghasb.
The passage explicitly states that ghasb occurs before the proponent argues for the premise.
5
Classify the objection
If all conditions are met (target unproven claim, claim of falsity, before proof), the objection is ghasb. Otherwise, it is a different type of dialectical move.
The author also notes that if falsity follows necessarily from a definitive chain without an explicit claim of falsity, it is not ghasb.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(فصل) في بيان حكم المنع الذي في صورة الإبطال: لو أبطل السائل بالدليل أو بالتنبيه المدعى غير المعلل (الذي لم يكن بديهيا ولم يكن مسلما عند الخصم) أو مقدمة دليل المعلل (كذلك) قبل أن يستدل المعلل على تلك المقدمة، أي ادعى بطلان أحدهما من ذلك المدعى والمقدمة وأثبته بدليل، فذا (أي ذلك الإبطال والاستدلال) يسمى غصبا في عرفهم سواء صدره بطلب الدليل أولا. وأما إذا لم يدع بطلان أحدهما بل لزم لما ذكر للإسناد كما في المنع بالسند القطعي المستلزم لنقيض الممنوع فهذا لا يسمى غصبا. وكذا إذا ادعى بطلان أحدهما بعد استدلال المعلل عليها لا يسمى غصبا بل معارضة. مثال: كما إذا قال المعلل لإثبات الكبرى الأولى: لأن كل متغير محل للحادث، وكل محل للحادث فهو حادث، وأثبت الصغرى بأن كل متغير محل لأمر حاصل بعد أن لم يكن، وذلك الأمر حادث، فقال السائل: لا نسلم الصغرى، لم لا يجوز أن يكون تغير المتغير بزوال أمر كان فيه؟ فيردد المعلل بين المقدمة الممنوعة وبين ذلك السند، فيضم لكل منهما مقدمة فيثبت المطلوب بأن يقول: إن كل متغير إما محل لأمر حاصل بعد أن لم يكن أو محل لزوال أمر كائن فيه، والأول حادث بلا شك والثاني حادث أيضا، فالمتغير محل للحادث. وبيان الكبرى الثانية أن كون الزوال أمرا عدميا لا يمنع كونه حادثا، ولا كونه صفة لشيء كالجهل بعد العلم. فذلك الإبطال يسمى غصبا.
A section on explaining the ruling of the objection (man') that occurs in the form of refutation (ibtal): If the opponent refutes, by proof or reminder, the unproven claim (which was not self-evident and not conceded by the adversary) or a premise of the proponent's proof (similarly) before the proponent has argued for that premise, i.e., he claims the falsity of one of them, either the claim or the premise, and establishes it by proof, then this (i.e., that refutation and reasoning) is called 'ghasb' (usurpation) in their terminology, whether he preceded it by requesting proof or not. But if he does not claim the falsity of either, rather it follows necessarily from what was mentioned for the chain, as in the objection by a definitive chain that entails the contradictory of the prohibited (premise), then this is not called ghasb. Likewise, if he claims the falsity of either after the proponent has argued for it, it is not called ghasb but rather a counter-argument (mu'arada). Example: As when the proponent says to establish the major premise: 'Because every changing thing is a locus for an event, and every locus for an event is an event,' and he establishes the minor premise: 'Every changing thing is a locus for something that occurs after it was not, and that something is an event.' Then the opponent says: 'We do not concede the minor premise; why may it not be that the change of the changing thing is by the removal of something that was in it?' So the proponent alternates between the prohibited premise and that chain, and attaches a premise to each, thereby establishing the required conclusion by saying: 'Every changing thing is either a locus for something that occurs after it was not, or a locus for the removal of something that was in it. The first is undoubtedly an event, and the second is also an event. Thus the changing thing is a locus for an event.' And the explanation of the second major premise is that the removal being a non-existent does not prevent it from being an event, nor from being a quality of something, like ignorance after knowledge. That refutation is called ghasb.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is a definitional section from a work on jadal (dialectical logic). It defines 'ghasb' (usurpation), a technical term in dialectical disputation. The author explains that ghasb occurs when the opponent (sā'il) refutes either the unproven claim (mad'ā ghayr mu'allal) or a premise of the proponent's (mu'allil) proof before the proponent has provided evidence for that premise. The refutation can be by explicit proof (dalīl) or by a reminder (tanbīh). The opponent must claim falsity (ibṭāl) and substantiate it. The term 'ghasb' is used whether the opponent first asked for proof or not. The author then distinguishes ghasb from other cases: if the opponent does not claim falsity but the falsity follows necessarily from a definitive chain (sanad qaṭ'ī) that entails the contrary of the denied premise, it is not ghasb. Similarly, if the opponent claims falsity after the proponent has argued for the premise, it is not ghasb but mu'āraḍa (counter-argument). To illustrate, the author provides an example from a syllogism about change (taghayyur) and events (ḥādith). The proponent argues that every changing thing is a locus for an event, and thus itself an event. The opponent objects to the minor premise by suggesting a different explanation: change could be by removal of a pre-existing thing, not by acquisition of a new event. The proponent then refines his argument by a disjunctive syllogism, showing that both possibilities lead to the same conclusion. The opponent's refutation here is ghasb because it was directed at an unproven premise before the proponent had fully defended it. The commentary emphasizes that ghasb is a specific dialectical move, not any objection, and the text's example clarifies its application in Aristotelian-style syllogistic reasoning within Islamic dialectic.

Key Terms
غصب
منع
إبطال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Islamic dialectic, when is an opponent's refutation (ibṭāl) considered 'ghasb' (usurpation)?
A) When the opponent refutes a premise after the proponent has proven it.
B) When the opponent refutes an unproven claim or premise before the proponent provides evidence for it.
C) When the opponent refutes using a definitive chain (sanad qat'i).
D) When the opponent requests proof before refuting.

Key Takeaway
Ghasb is a dialectical offense: refuting an unproven claim before proof is given; the proponent may respond by strengthening the argument, for example via a disjunctive syllogism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Read the example in the passage. Identify: (a) the proponent's unproven claim, (b) the opponent's objection that constitutes ghasb, and (c) explain why it fits the definition.
?

The Proponent's Disjunctive Response to Ghasb
Example of ghasb and the proponent's disjunctive response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proponent's disjunctive syllogism from the example and explain how it counteracts the ghasb objection.

Core Idea
A disjunctive syllogism neutralises a premature objection by showing the conclusion follows from all alternatives.

In the example from the source, the proponent faces a ghasb objection on the premise that 'every changing thing is a locus for an event'. The opponent suggests an alternative explanation. The proponent does not simply repeat the premise but uses a disjunctive argument to cover both possibilities, thereby reinforcing the conclusion. This lesson dissects that logical move.

Guided Path
1
Extract the original syllogism
The proponent originally aimed to prove that every changing thing is an event. The syllogism: minor premise: every changing thing is a locus for an event; major premise: every locus for an event is an event; conclusion: every changing thing is an event. The opponent objects to the minor premise.
The minor premise is unproven at this stage.
2
Identify the ghasb objection
The opponent says: 'We do not concede the minor premise; why may it not be that change is by removal of something?' This is a claim of falsity with an alternative, before the proponent proved the premise.
This is ghasb because it targets an unproven claim.
3
Analyze the proponent's response
Instead of directly defending the original premise, the proponent expands the argument into a disjunction: either change is by acquisition of a new event, or by removal of a pre-existing thing. He then shows that both possibilities lead to the conclusion that the changing thing is a locus for an event.
The proponent uses a disjunctive syllogism: (A or B), if A then C, if B then C, therefore C.
4
Verification of validity
The disjunction is exhaustive (change must be either acquisition or removal). Both disjuncts lead to the conclusion that the changing thing undergoes an event (acquisition or removal). Therefore, the conclusion necessarily follows.
The proponent also explains that removal, though a non-existence, qualifies as an event.

Source Text
