Dispute on the Permissibility of Ghasb in Dialectic
Author presents the disagreement on whether ghasb is admissible (masmū') in debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three positions on ghasb in jadal and reconstruct the key objections and responses that determine its admissibility.

Core Idea
Ghasb's admissibility turns on whether the mu'allil's goal is self-knowledge or truth-manifestation.

In this passage, the author lays out the disagreement over ghasb—the questioner's act of using the proponent's own reasoning to refute him. Three positions are reported, and the arguments for and against its admissibility are critically examined. Understanding this debate is key to mastering the procedural rules of Islamic dialectic (jadal).

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions on ghasb
The passage opens with three views: some say ghasb is admissible (masmū') and must be answered; others say it is not admissible (ghayr masmū'); the majority of investigators (muḥaqqiqūn) side with the latter.
Note the technical term masmū' for procedural admissibility.
2
Extract the two reasons for prohibition
Al-Qāzābādī gives two reasons: (1) the mu'allil's purpose is to know the correctness or falsity of his own proof, and ghasb disrupts that; (2) permitting ghasb for the questioner could lead the mu'allil to also usurp, derailing the debate.
These reasons correspond to the two 'aspects' (wajh) mentioned.
3
Analyze the objections to those reasons
The author refutes each: the mu'allil's true goal is manifesting truth, not self-knowledge; even if his goal were as stated, ghasb does not necessarily frustrate it. The second reason is rebutted by arguing that permission for one side does not imply permission for the other, and any deviation is not inherently harmful.
The objections are detailed and multi-layered; follow the author's line-by-line refutation.
4
Understand the alternative justifying admissibility
Al-ʿAmīdī and his followers argue that when the questioner says 'I only made a refusal with evidence, not commitment to falsity,' the resulting ghasb is not real ghasb but a legitimate objection. Therefore, such ghasb deserves an answer.
This shifts the definition of ghasb to exclude refusals with evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلف في أنه مسموع يجب على المعلل أن يجيب عنه أى عن الغصب. قال بعضهم إنه مسموع يجب أن يجيب عنه. وقال بعضهم غير مسموع لا يجب أن يجيب عنه والمحققون قالوا إنه غير مسموع. قال الأستاذ العلامة القازآبادى وإنما منعوه لأن المعلل مادام معللا يكون التعليل حقه ليعلم حقية دليله أو بطلانه وليس للسائل هناك إلا مطالبة ذلك، فاذا غصب فقد فات غرضه ولأنه إذا جوز في جانب السائل فالمعلل أيضا قد يغصب فيلزم بعدها عما كانه فيه وضلالها عن طريق التوجيه، وكلا الوجهين منظور فيه. أما الأول فلانا لا نسلم أن غرض المعلل أن يعلم حقية دليله أو بطلانه، بل غرضه إظهار الصواب بأى وجه كان، وإظهار الصواب يحصل من غير أن يعلم حقية دليله أو بطلانه بأن يمنع السائل فيعجز المعلل عن دفعه، ولو سلم فلا يلزم منه فوات غرضه على تقدير الغصب لجواز أن يعلم حقية دليله بأن يدفع الغصب وبطلانه بأن يعجز عن دفع الغصب، وأيضا لا محذور في فوات غرضه، إذ لا يخل غرض المناظرة، وأيضا يكفى في إثبات المدعى قوله ليعلم حقية دليلة أو بطلانه : وقوله فاذا غصب فقد فات غرضه، وباقى مقدماته مستدركة : وأما الثانى فلأنه لا يلزم من تجويزه في جانب السائل تجويزه في جانب المعلل ولو سلم فلا يلزم أن يغصب المعلل أيضا لجواز أن يترك وظيفته تلك ولو سلم فان أراد بعدهما عن أصل الدليل فلا محذور فيه، وإن أراد حصول غرض إظهار الصواب بعد طول الكلام فلا محذور فيه أيضا، وإن أراد عدم حصوله أصلا فهو ممنوع. انتهى كلامه. ومن قال وهو مولانا ركن الدين العميدي ومن تبعه إنه مسموع يقول إن للسائل أن يقول لم التزم البطلان بل أردت المنع مع السند مطلقا بما ذكرته في صورة الاستدلال والإبطال لكن لزمه البطلان، ولو كان المنع باستلزام البطلان غصبا لكان المنع مع السند القطعي غصبا وهو بين البطلان، وإنما أورده في صورة الاستدلال تنبيها على قوة الاعتراض ومتانته أو ترويجا لمنعه، وإذا كان للسائل أن يقول كذلك فيستحق الغصب الجواب بأحد الوجوه السابقة حينئذ أى حين كونه منعا ألبتة. قال في التوضيح ما ملخصه
And there is disagreement about whether it (i.e., ghasb) is admissible (masmū') such that the mu'allil is obligated to answer it—i.e., to answer the ghasb. Some said it is admissible and he must answer it. Others said it is not admissible and he need not answer it, and the investigators said it is not admissible. Al-Qāzābādī said: They prevented it because the mu'allil, as long as he is giving proof, the right to give proof belongs to him so that he knows the correctness of his proof or its falsity, and the questioner has no role except to demand that. So if he usurps, his purpose is lost. And because if it is permitted on the side of the questioner, then the mu'allil may also usurp, and thus they would deviate from what they were in and go astray from the path of direction. Both aspects are open to objection. As for the first: we do not accept that the purpose of the mu'allil is to know the correctness of his proof or its falsity; rather, his purpose is to manifest the truth in any way possible, and the manifestation of truth occurs without his knowing the correctness or falsity of his proof, e.g., by the questioner's refusal making the mu'allil unable to defend it. Even if granted, it does not entail the loss of his purpose if usurpation occurs, because it is possible that he knows the correctness of his proof by rebutting the ghasb, and its falsity by being unable to rebut it. Also, there is no harm in losing his purpose, because the purpose of the debate is not undermined. Also, his statement 'so that he knows the correctness of his proof or its falsity' is sufficient to prove the claim, and his statement 'so if he usurps, his purpose is lost' is followed by other premises that are redundant. As for the second: it does not follow from permitting it on the side of the questioner that it is permitted on the side of the mu'allil; even if granted, it does not entail that the mu'allil would also usurp, because it is possible that he refrains from that function. Even if granted, if he intended deviation from the original proof, there is no harm; if he intended to achieve the purpose of manifesting the truth after lengthy discussion, there is no harm either; and if he intended that it should not happen at all, it is denied. End of his words. And those who said—namely our master Rukn al-Dīn al-ʿAmīdī and those who followed him—that it is admissible, they say: The questioner may say: 'I did not commit to the falsity; rather, I intended refusal with evidence absolutely, as I mentioned in the form of reasoning and invalidation, but falsity necessarily followed.' If refusal that entails falsity were ghasb, then refusal with certain evidence would be ghasb, which is obviously false. He only presented it in the form of reasoning as a hint to the strength and solidity of the objection, or to promote his refusal. And if the questioner can say that, then the ghasb deserves an answer with one of the previous ways at that time, i.e., when it is a refusal, absolutely. He said in al-Tawḍīḥ, summarizing it.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage examines the dispute over whether ghasb (the questioner's usurpation of the mu'allil's reasoning) is permissible (masmū') in dialectic. Three positions are reported: some say it is admissible and must be answered; others say it is not admissible and no answer is required; the investigators side with the latter. Al-Qāzābādī provides two reasons for prohibiting it: first, the mu'allil's purpose is to know the correctness or falsity of his own proof, and ghasb disrupts that; second, permitting ghasb for the questioner would allow the mu'allil to retaliate, leading to mutual deviation from the issue. The author then presents objections to these reasons. Regarding the first: the mu'allil's true goal is to manifest truth, not necessarily to know the status of his own proof—truth can be manifested even if the mu'allil fails. Moreover, even if his purpose were as claimed, ghasb does not necessarily frustrate it; he might still learn correct or falsity through his ability or inability to rebut. The second reason is also refuted: permission for the questioner does not imply permission for the mu'allil, and even if it did, the mu'allil might choose not to usurp. Allowing extended debate is not harmful. After refuting al-Qāzābādī's arguments, the passage presents the position of al-ʿAmīdī and his followers who deem ghasb admissible. They argue that when the questioner says 'I did not commit to falsity; I only made a refusal with evidence', this is not ghasb but legitimate argument. If such refusal entails falsity, it does not automatically make it ghasb, as that would absurdly include all refusals with certain evidence. The questioner's presentation in the form of reasoning is merely to emphasize the strength of the objection. Therefore, ghasb deserves an answer.

Key Terms
مسموع
غصب
معلل
منع

Key Terms from Source Passage

The admissibility of ghasb hinges on whether the mu'allil's purpose is to know his proof's status or to manifest truth, and whether ghasb serves or hinders that purpose.

The Ruling on Ghasb in Jadal: Is It Always Acceptable?
Author presents the dispute over whether ghasb must be answered, with arguments from both sides.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the positions on ghasb's acceptability in jadal and evaluate the arguments and rebuttals presented in this passage.

Core Idea
Ghasb's acceptability hinges on whether it aids truth-seeking, not on formal permission.

In the science of jadal (dialectical debate), procedural rules govern which objections the defender must answer. One such rule concerns ghasb—a type of argumentative interference. The verifiers (muhaqqiqun) argued that ghasb is not acceptable, but their opponents offered counterarguments. In this lesson, we dissect this debate to understand how jadal refines its own procedures.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions on ghasb
The passage states that some say ghasb is acceptable (masmu'), some say it is not acceptable, and the muhaqqiqun say it is not acceptable. Note that the muhaqqiqun's position aligns with the second group but they are singled out as verifiers.
The three positions are: acceptable, not acceptable, and the verifiers' position (not acceptable).
2
Extract the two proofs of the muhaqqiqun
The muhaqqiqun adduce two proofs. First: the defender's purpose in reasoning is to know the truth of his proof or its falsehood; if the opponent commits ghasb, this purpose is lost. Second: if ghasb is permitted for the opponent, it would also be permitted for the defender, leading to distance from revealing the truth.
Proof 1 focuses on the defender's epistemic goal. Proof 2 uses a parity argument.
3
Analyze the rebuttals to each proof
Against the first proof, the reply is that the defender's purpose is to reveal truth in any way possible, which can happen even if the opponent contests; the purpose is not necessarily lost. Against the second, the reply is that permitting ghasb for the opponent does not necessitate permitting it for the defender, and even if it did, the defender might not commit it, and if he does, there is no harm.
The rebuttals challenge the necessity underlying each proof.
4
Evaluate which side has the stronger argument based on jadal principles
Consider the goals of jadal: seeking truth and maintaining orderly debate. The muhaqqiqun's proofs seem strong but the rebuttals show that ghasb might still serve truth. Decide which position better preserves the purpose of jadal.
The passage does not declare a winner; it leaves room for further discussion.

Source Text

واختلف في كون الغصب مسموعاً يجب على المعلل الجواب عنه. فقال بعضهم إنه مسموع، وقال بعضهم غير مسموع. والمحققون قالوا إنه غير مسموع. واحتجوا بأمرين: الأول: أن غرض المعلل من التعليل هو معرفة حقية دليله أو بطلانه، فإذا غصب السائل فات هذا الغرض. الثاني: أنه لو جوز الغصب للسائل لجوز للمعلل أيضاً، فيؤدي إلى البعد عن إظهار الصواب. وأجيب عن الأول بأن غرض المعلل إظهار الصواب بأي وجه كان، وذلك يحصل ولو بمنع السائل، ولا يلزم فوات الغرض بالغصب، إذ قد يعلم حقية الدليل بدفع الغصب أو بطلانه بالعجز عن دفعه. وأجيب عن الثاني بأنه لا يلزم من تجويز الغصب للسائل تجويزه للمعلل، ولو سلم فلا يلزم أن يغصب المعلل، وإن غصب فلا محذور في البعد عن أصل الدليل أو طول الكلام.
And they differed concerning whether ghasb is acceptable such that the defender must answer it. Some said it is acceptable, and some said it is not acceptable. And the verifiers said it is not acceptable. They adduced two proofs: First, the defender's purpose in reasoning is to know the truth of his proof or its falsehood, so if the opponent commits ghasb, this purpose is lost. Second, if ghasb is permitted for the opponent, it would also be permitted for the defender, leading to distance from revealing the truth. And it was answered concerning the first that the defender's purpose is to reveal the truth in any way possible, and that is achieved even if the opponent contests, and the purpose is not necessarily lost by ghasb, since he may know the truth of his proof by refuting the ghasb or its falsehood by being unable to refute it. And it was answered concerning the second that permitting ghasb for the opponent does not necessitate permitting it for the defender, and even if it did, it does not necessitate that the defender will actually commit ghasb, and if he does, there is no harm in distance from the original proof or lengthy discourse.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage addresses a procedural question in jadal: is ghasb 'acceptable' (masmu') — meaning must the defender answer it? The author reports three positions: some say it is acceptable, some say it is not, and the muhaqqiqun (verifiers) hold it is not acceptable. Their proofs are given. The first proof focuses on the defender's purpose: his goal in presenting his argument (ta'lil) is to ascertain whether his proof is true or false. If the opponent engages in ghasb — i.e., argues for the invalidity of a mere contested premise — then this purpose is thwarted. The second proof is a parity argument: if ghasb is allowed for the opponent, it should also be allowed for the defender, leading to a futile exchange. The passage then presents rebuttals to each proof. Against the first: the defender's actual aim is to manifest truth, not merely to test his proof. Manifesting truth can happen even if the opponent contests, and the defender might still learn the status of his proof from the encounter with ghasb. Against the second: parity does not hold; the defender's role is different, and even if it did, the defender might not resort to ghasb. The discussion shows the nuanced debate within the tradition about the boundaries of legitimate argumentation.

Key Takeaway
Ghasb's status in jadal is determined by its effect on the defender's goal of manifesting truth, not by formalistic considerations.

Defining Ghasb: The Opponent's Argument for Invalidity
Author defines ghasb as the opponent's argument for invalidity of a legitimately contested premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus and differentia in the definition of ghasb and distinguish it from other dialectical moves like mere denial or the defender's argument.

Core Idea
Ghasb is not mere denial but a structured argument by the opponent targeting unproven claims.

In dialectical disputation (jadal), precise terminology separates legitimate argument from sophistry. The concept of ghasb is central: it defines the opponent's right to challenge unproven premises. This lesson unpacks the author's definition and its implications, enabling you to recognize and use this tool in real debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined. In the Arabic, it is 'الغصب' (ghasb). The author states 'الغصب في عرفهم...' meaning 'Ghasb in their terminology is...'.
2
Extract the genus
What broader category does ghasb belong to? The author says it is 'استدلال' (argumentation/act of reasoning). So the genus is 'argumentation'.
This is not just any argument; it's a specific type within the genus of argumentation.
3
Identify the differentia
What makes ghasb specific? It is 'استدلال السائل على بطلان ما صح منعه' – the opponent's argumentation for the invalidity of something that it is legitimate to contest. The differentia has two parts: (1) it is by the opponent (السائل), and (2) it targets the invalidity (بطلان) of a claim that is legitimately contestable (ما صح منعه).
This excludes the defender's argumentation (استدلال المعلل) and also excludes contesting something that is not legitimately contestable.
4
Note inclusions and exclusions
The passage explicitly says it includes refuting an unproven claim (إبطال المدعى غير المدلل) and an unproven premise, and according to some, even a claim proven with a defective proof. It excludes the defender's own argumentation. This helps define the scope of ghasb.
5
Parse the linguistic nuance
The author discusses wording: if the opponent uses the word 'man'' (contestation) or its derivatives, it is metaphorical (مجاز) because it merely requests proof. If using 'لا نسلم' (we do not accept), it is not metaphorical. This clarifies that the form of expression does not change the logical nature of the move.
This nuance prevents confusion between the literal meaning of 'man'' and its technical use in jadal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الغصب في عرفهم استدلال السائل على بطلان ما صح منعه. خرج به استدلال المعلل. ويدخل فيه إبطال المدعى غير المدلل وإبطال المقدمة غير المدللة، وكذا المدللين بدليل فاسد عند البعض. ومنع المدعى غير المدلل إن كان بلفظ المنع ومشتقاته فهو مجاز، وإن كان بلفظ لا نسلم فلا مجاز. فالمعارضة التحقيقية هي هذا الغصب، بخلاف التقديرية.
Ghasb in their terminology is the opponent's argumentation for the invalidity of what it is legitimate to contest. By it is excluded the defender's argumentation. It includes the refutation of the unproven claim and the unproven premise, and likewise what is proven by a defective proof according to some. The contestation of the unproven claim if it is with the wording of contestation and its derivatives is metaphorical, but if with another wording like 'we do not accept' then it is not metaphorical. So the real counter-argument is this ghasb, unlike the hypothetical one.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage defines a key term in jadal (dialectical disputation): al-ghasb. The author begins by stating that ghasb is the opponent's argumentation for the invalidity of something that it is valid to contest (i.e., a premise or claim that has not been proven and is thus open to being challenged). This definition excludes the defender's own argumentation. The author then explains that ghasb includes refuting an unproven claim or an unproven premise, and also, according to some, even a claim or premise that has been proven but with a defective proof. Next, the author clarifies a linguistic point: if the opponent contests an unproven claim using the actual word 'contestation' (man') or its derivatives, that is a metaphorical use meaning merely a request for proof; but if the opponent uses a different phrase like 'we do not accept' (la nusallim), there is no metaphor. Finally, the real counter-argument (mu'arada tahqiqiyya) is identified as this ghasb, as opposed to hypothetical counter-argument (mu'arada taqdiriyya). The passage thus gives precise boundaries to the concept, distinguishing it from other dialectical moves.

Key Terms
الغصب (al-ghasb)
المعارضة (al-mu'arada)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Ghasb is the opponent's argument-driven challenge to an unproven claim, distinct from both the defender's argument and mere rejection; it is the real counter-argument in dialectic.

Proper Objection Technique in Jadal: Man' vs. Ibṭāl
Author advises on proper objection phrasing in dialectics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between man' and ibṭāl in a dialectical exchange and apply the proper objection technique to avoid ghasb.

Core Idea
Conceal your knowledge and request proof to avoid being dismissed as a ghasib.

In dialectical debates (jadal), how you present an objection can determine whether it is accepted or dismissed. This lesson unpacks the strategic advice from our passage: frame objections as requests for evidence (man') rather than claims of falsity (ibṭāl), so the opponent cannot evade by accusing you of ghasb. You will learn to implement this technique and understand its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target to object
Locate the unproven premise or claim (ghayr mudallal) in the opponent's argument. This is the statement that lacks evidence.
An unproven premise is one that the defender has not yet supported with proof.
2
Choose man' over ibṭāl
Instead of saying 'This premise is invalid,' which is ibṭāl (invalidation), say 'I contest this premise; please provide proof.' This is man' (contestation), a procedural request.
Man' is considered more respectful and avoids the accusation of ghasb.
3
Handle a counter-accusation of ghasb
If the opponent calls your objection ghasb (unacceptable intrusion), clarify that you never asserted invalidity—you only requested proof. You can refer to your phrasing as support.
This aligns with the author's advice to 'conceal knowledge of invalidity'.
4
Recognize the exceptional view
Some later scholars allow ghasb itself to be accepted without converting to man', if one party is utterly unable to argue. Understand this as a minority opinion.
The passage mentions this to show that the rule is not absolute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ينبغي لمن حكم بفساد مقدمة معينة غير مدللة أن يخفي علمه بفسادها ويطلب عليها دليلاً، وكذا من حكم بفساد مدعى غير مدلل، وأن يورد اعتراضه على سبيل المنع لا على سبيل الإبطال، لئلا يقول الخصم الجدلي إنه غصب غير مسموع، فيحتاج إلى العناية بأن يقول لم ألتزم البطلان بل أردت المنع مع السند بما ذكرته في صورة الإبطال والاستدلال. وهذا تعليم ينفع في المناظرات. وجوز بعضهم أن الغصب مسموع بدون إرجاعه إلى المنع، لأن أحد الطرفين يعجز ألبتة عن الاستدلال فيلزم الإحجام والإلزام ويثبت المطلوب. ولهذا قال بعض الفضلاء إن إبطال المدعى والمقدمة غير المدللين إذا كان بتقدير الدليل يسمى معارضة تقديرية وإلا فيسمى نقضاً إجمالياً شبيهاً.
It behooves whoever judges that a particular unproven premise is invalid to conceal his knowledge of its invalidity and to request proof for it, and likewise whoever judges that an unproven claim is invalid, and to present his objection in the manner of contestation not invalidation, so that the dialectical opponent does not say it is ghasb which is not acceptable, and thus need the effort of saying 'I did not commit to invalidity, but I intended contestation with support by what I mentioned in the form of invalidation and argumentation.' And this is instruction that benefits in disputations. And some allowed that ghasb is acceptable without returning it to contestation, because one of the two parties absolutely cannot argue, so reluctance and imposition follow and the desired is established. For this reason some of the excellents said that the refutation of the unproven claim and the unproven premise, if it is with supposition of proof, is called hypothetical counter-argument, otherwise it is called a summary refutation resembling it.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage provides practical guidance for a participant in a jadal session who wishes to object to an unproven premise or claim. The author advises that one should not bluntly declare the premise invalid, but instead conceal one's knowledge of its invalidity and simply request proof from the defender. This way, the objection is presented as a contestation (man') — a request for evidence — rather than a claim of invalidity (ibṭāl). The reason for this strategy is to prevent the opponent from dismissing the objection as ghasb, which according to the muhaqqiqun is not acceptable (ghayr masmu') and thus does not require a response. If one were to present it as an invalidation, the defender could object that it is ghasb and refuse to answer. To avoid that, one should phrase it as a contestation and, if challenged, explain that it was only a request for proof, not a commitment to invalidity. This is a clever rhetorical move. The passage further notes that some scholars do allow ghasb to be accepted without converting it back to contestation, because in practice one side might be completely unable to defend, so the result (iḥjām and ilzām) still proves the point. This is linked to the distinction between hypothetical counter-argument (mu'arada taqdiriyya) and summary refutation (naqḍ ijmālī).

Key Terms
المنع (al-man'): Contestation; a request for proof without claiming invalidity. In this passage, it is the recommended form of objection. [Note: In other jadal texts, man' may also mean outright denial; here it is softened to a procedural request.]
الإبطال (al-ibṭāl): Invalidation; claiming that a premise or claim is false, which may be considered ghasb if the target is not yet proven. [Note: No significant shift, but its contrast with man' is emphasized in this context.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Conceal one's knowledge and request proof.
C) Accuse the defender of ghasb.
D) Withdraw from the debate.

Key Takeaway
In dialectical debate, object to unproven claims via man' (contestation) rather than ibṭāl (invalidation) to avoid dismissal as ghasb.

Mu'arada and Naqd Are Not Ghasb in Jadal
Author proves that mu'arada and naqd are not usurpation in dialectic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the syllogistic proof that mu'arada and naqd are not ghasb in jadal, and explain why the opponent's prevention (man') is not valid against a proof.

Core Idea
In dialectic, invalidating a claim or a proof with a valid counter-argument is not usurpation; it is legitimate opposition.

In classical Islamic dialectic (jadal), two critical moves—mu'arada (opposition to a claim) and naqd (refutation of a proof)—are sometimes mistaken for usurpation (ghasb). This passage from a commentary on al-Waladiyya provides a rigorous syllogistic defense of their legitimacy. By following the author's logical steps, you will see how the composition of a proof renders prevention invalid, and why these moves are essential for structured debate.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish mu'arada and naqd
Identify the two dialectical moves being defended: mu'arada targets the opponent's claim by bringing a counter-proof after the opponent's argument; naqd targets the opponent's proof by invalidating it with another proof. Both are under suspicion of being ghasb (usurpation).
Ghasb here means an illegitimate objection that steals the opponent's right to argue.
2
Extract the syllogism for mu'arada
Find the major premise: 'Every invalidation whose prevention is not valid is not usurpation.' The minor premise: 'Mu'arada is an invalidation whose prevention is not valid' (because prevention of a claim after evidence is invalid). The conclusion: 'Mu'arada is not usurpation.' Note the author also mentions an alternative compound form.
The author uses the phrase 'كل إبطال ليس منعه صحيحا ليس بغصب' as the key major premise.
3
Extract the syllogism for naqd
Identify the premises: (1) 'Naqd is invalidating a proof with a proof.' (2) 'Every invalidation of a proof by a proof is not usurpation.' But the author further justifies why prevention of a proof is invalid: prevention only applies to things that can be argued upon, and a proof (being a syllogism composed of premises) cannot be argued upon. Thus the conclusion: 'Naqd is not usurpation.' The author also gives a first-figure alternative.
The argument for naqd is more complex because it involves the nature of proof itself. The author uses the second figure (or first) to show that proof cannot be prevented.
4
Analyze the research (bahth) on the composition of proof
Understand the subtle objection: if a proof produces only one premise, can it be considered simple? The author replies that even if considered as one premise, it is still composite in reality, and prevention does not apply. This protects the naqd argument from a possible counter.
The bahth shows the depth of jadal: even apparent objections are addressed with logical precision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ليست بغصب لأنه (أي المعارضة) إبطال الدعوى بدليل بعد استدلال المعلل عليه (أي على الدعوى) وأي التذكير في الموضعين سهل، وكل إبطال شأنه ذلك ليس بغصب لأن منع المدعى بعد الاستدلال عليه ليس بصحيح فظهر أن قوله (وليس منع الدعوة) للذكورة (بعد الاستدلال عليه صحيحا) من قبيل عطف الملة على المعلول فهو علة للكبرى المطوية، ومن قال هذا قياس متعارف ذكرت كلنا مقدمتيه ونتيجته قال إن المعارضة إبطال ما ليس منعه صحيحا ونضم إليها قولنا: وكل ما هو إبطال ليس منعه صحيحا فهو ليس بغصب فينتج المطلوب، فلا يخفى تكلفه بل فساده فتأمل حق التأمل) وكذا (أي مثل المعارضة) النقض الإجمالي الحقيقي، ومن عمم بقوله حقيقيا أو شبهها فقد غفل عن سابق كلامه ولاحقه) ليس بغصب لأنه (أي النقض) إبطال الدليل بدليل وكل إبطال الدليل بدليل ليس بغصب ولا يصح منع الدليل) فهو أيضا دليل للكبرى المطلوبة) لأن المنع إنما يصح على ما يمكن الاستدلال عليه والدليل (أي كل واحد من الدليل) لا يمكن الاستدلال عليه) فينتج من الشكل الثاني أن المنع لا يصح وروده على الدليل فلا يصح منع الدليل، ويمكن أن يكون دليلا من الشكل الأول بتقديم الكبرى، وتصويره كل واحد من الدليل لا يمكن الاستدلال عليه، وكل ما لا يمكن الاستدلال عليه لا يصح منعه، فالدليل لا يصح منعه فلا يصح منع الدليل (لأنه) أي الدليل الأول) مركب من مقدمتين (أي الصغرى والكبرى في الاقتراني حمليا أو شرطيا والمقدمة الشرطية مع الواضحة أو الرافعة في الاستثنائي وكل مركب من مقدمتين لا يمكن الاستدلال عليه، فالدليل الأول لا يمكن الاستدلال عليه، إنما اختار المقدمتين ولم يقل من مقدمتين أو أكثر مع أنهم قسموا القياس إلى البسيط والمركب إشارة إلى أن التحقيق أن الدليل في الحقيقة لا يتركب إلا من مقدمتين لا من مقدمات وتقسيم القياس إلى البسيط والمركب إنما هو بحسب الظاهر ولذا قالوا إن القياس والمركب في الحقيقة أقيسة كذا فتح الباب أو اكتفاء بالأقل والدليل) المطلوب من طرف السائل ليست بغصب لأنه إبطال الدعوى بدليل بعد استدلال المعلل عليه ) والتذكير في الضميرين للزوم التأنيث أو باعتبار الخبر في الأول، والمقدمة المدللة من حيث هي مدللة داخلة في الدعوى المدللة فلا حاجة إلى ذكرها ( وليس منع الدعوى بعد الاستدلال عليه صحيحا وإبطال ما لا يصح منعه ليس بغصب كما عرفت ( وكذا النقض الإجمالي ليس بغصب لأنه إبطال لدليل بدليل، يصح منع الدليل ( فلا يكون الاستدلال على بطلانهما ) لأن المنع إنما يصح ) وروده ) على ما يمكن الاستدلال عليه . والدليل لا يمكن الاستدلال عليه ) بدليل آخر (لأنه) أى الدليل الأول ( مركب من مقدمتين ، والدليل ( الآخر لا ينتج المركب من مقدمتين . وهنا بحث وهو أن يقال إن أريد بالمقدمة الواحد المقدمة التي لا تنحل الى المقدمتين فلا تسلم الصغرى ، الدليل الواحد قد ينتج مقدمة واحدة تنحل إلى مقدمات كثيرة وإن أريد بها المقدمة الواحدة ولو اعتبارا فلا تسلم الكبرى إذ المركب من المقدمتين يمكن أن يعتبر مقدمة واحدة ويثبت بدليل بأن يقال هذا الدليل صحيح لأنه دليل ثابتة مقدماته، وكل دليل شأنه كذا فصحيح لا يقال يمكن أن يجاب بأن يقال إنا نختار الشق الثاني . لكن قيد الحيثية معتبر فى الكبرى : فان ما لا ينتج إلا مقدمة واحدة) وهو دليل للكبرى المطوية فهو من قبيل عطف الملة على المعلول ( وههنا (بحث) وسيأتى بيان هذا البحث . قيل وهو أن يقال إن أريد بالمقدمة الواحدة المقدمة التي لا تنحل إلى المقدمتين فلا نسلم الصغرى . إذ الدليل الواحد قد ينتج مقدمة واحدة تنحل إلى مقدمات كثيرة . وإن أريد بها المقدمة الواحدة ولو اعتبارا فلا نسلم الكبرى. إذ المركب من المقدمتين مكن أن يعتبر مقدمة ويثبت بدليل بأن يقال هذا الدليل صحيح لأنه ثابت مقدماته وكل دليل شأنه كذا فصحيح ولا تخفى مافيه فتأمل فيه ) وستعرف المعارضة ) في المقالة الثانية ( والنقض في المقالة الثالثة)
It is not usurpation because it (i.e., mu'arada) is invalidating the claim with a proof after the arguer has adduced evidence for it (i.e., the claim). And the masculine pronoun in both places is easy. Every invalidation of that sort is not usurpation because preventing the claimant after evidence is not valid. So it appears that his statement (and preventing the claim... after evidence on it is valid) is of the type of attaching the cause to the effect, so it is a cause for the implied major premise. Whoever says this is a familiar syllogism, we mentioned both its premises and its conclusion, says: Mu'arada is invalidating what its prevention is not valid, and we add to it our statement: Everything that is an invalidation whose prevention is not valid is not usurpation, so the desired result follows. Thus the affectation, rather its corruption, is not hidden, so reflect truly. And likewise (i.e., like mu'arada) the real summary refutation (al-naqd al-ijmali al-haqiqi), and whoever generalizes by saying 'real or similar' has neglected his earlier and later speech, is not usurpation because it (i.e., refutation) is invalidating the proof with a proof, and every invalidation of proof by proof is not usurpation. And prevention of the proof is not valid. So it is also a proof for the desired major premise, because prevention is only valid on what can be argued, and the proof (i.e., each one of the proofs) cannot be argued. So from the second figure it follows that prevention does not validly occur on the proof, so prevention of the proof is not valid. And it can be a proof from the first figure by advancing the major premise, and its form: Each one of the proofs cannot be argued, and everything that cannot be argued cannot be prevented, so the proof cannot be prevented, so prevention of the proof is not valid. Because (i.e., the first proof) is composed of two premises (i.e., the minor and major in categorical conditional, and the conditional premise with the explicit or the rafi' in exceptional, and everything composed of two premises cannot be argued, so the first proof cannot be argued. He only chose two premises and did not say from two or more, even though they divided syllogism into simple and compound, indicating that the reality is that the proof in truth does not consist except of two premises, not of multiple premises, and the division of syllogism into simple and compound is only apparent. Hence they said that the compound syllogism is in reality multiple syllogisms. So open the door or suffice with the minimum. And the proof demanded by the side of the questioner is not usurpation because it is invalidating the claim with a proof after the arguer has adduced evidence on it, and the masculine pronoun in both pronouns is due to feminine correspondence or considering the predicate in the first. And the evidenced premise, inasmuch as it is evidenced, is included in the evidenced claim, so there is no need to mention it. And preventing the claim after evidence on it is not valid, and invalidating what its prevention is not valid is not usurpation, as you know. And likewise the summary refutation is not usurpation because it is invalidating a proof by a proof, and prevention of the proof is valid, so the argument for their falsity is not valid, because prevention is only valid on what can be argued, and the proof cannot be argued with another proof because the first proof is composed of two premises, and the other proof does not produce the compound from two premises. And here there is a research: It is said that if by 'one premise' is meant the premise that does not resolve into two premises, then we do not grant the minor, for one proof may produce one premise that resolves into many premises. And if by it is meant one premise even if considered, then we do not grant the major, for the compound from two premises can be considered one premise and be proven by a proof, by saying: This proof is correct because its premises are certain, and every proof of such is correct. It cannot be said that it can be answered by saying we choose the second branch. But the aspect qualification is considered in the major: for what produces only one premise, and it is a proof for the implied major premise, so it is of the type of attaching the cause to the effect. And here there is a research, and its explanation will come. It is said: And it is that if by 'one premise' is meant the premise that does not resolve into two premises, we do not grant the minor, for one proof may produce one premise that resolves into many premises. And if by it is meant the one premise even if considered, we do not grant the major, for the compound from two premises can be considered a premise and be proven by a proof, by saying: This proof is correct because its premises are certain, and every proof of such is correct. And what is in it is not hidden, so reflect on it. And you will know mu'arada in the second article, and the refutation in the third article.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a detailed dialectical analysis proving that two specific moves in jadal—mu'arada (opposition) and naqd (summary refutation)—are not acts of ghasb (usurpation), contrary to a mistaken view. The author follows a syllogistic method. First, for mu'arada: it is defined as invalidating the claim with a proof after the opponent's argument. Since preventing a claim after evidence is invalid, invalidating that which cannot be validly prevented is not usurpation. This is presented as a compound syllogism. Then for naqd: it is invalidating the proof with another proof. The author argues that a proof cannot be prevented because it is composed of two premises, and prevention applies only to what can be argued upon. Using the second figure, he shows that the proof cannot be prevented, so naqd is not usurpation. He also shows an alternative first-figure form. A key subtlety: the proof itself (a syllogism) cannot be argued upon because it is a composite; only its premises can be. The author discusses a research (bahth) about whether a proof that produces one premise can be considered simple, and whether that premise can be further analyzed. This guards against misunderstanding that naqd might be invalid because it targets the proof itself. All examples are within the Islamic logical tradition of jadal.

Key Terms
المعارضة
النقض الإجمالي
الغصب

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is naqd not considered ghasb?
A) Because it invalidates the claim, not the proof.
B) Because the proof cannot be prevented since it is composed of two premises and prevention applies only to what can be argued upon.
C) Because the proof is always certain and cannot be refuted.
D) Because the opponent has already finished his argument.

Key Takeaway
In jadal, opposing a claim (mu'arada) or refuting a proof (naqd) with valid counter-arguments is not usurpation; it is a legitimate use of syllogistic reasoning.

When Estimated Opposition Becomes Usurpation in Jadal
Author notes that estimated opposition and total similar refutation are considered usurpation when entailment is preventible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an estimated opposition or total similar refutation is considered usurpation (ghasb) based on whether the entailment is preventible.

Core Idea
Not all objections are allowed: if the entailment can be prevented, opposing it is usurpation.

In dialectical debate, not every objection is legitimate. This passage from the Waladiyya commentary introduces a subtle rule: when the entailment (istilzam) that links a claim to its basis can be prevented by the respondent, certain objections – the estimated opposition and the total similar refutation – become acts of usurpation (ghasb). The author explains why he omitted them from the main text and notes a difference of opinion on their permissibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key claim
The passage states that since entailment (istilzam) can be prevented, the estimated opposition and total similar refutation are considered usurpations according to the author. Find the exact phrase that states this condition.
Look for the phrase 'لما كان الاستلزام مما يصح منعه'.
2
Understand the definition of usurpation (ghasb)
The author defines ghasb as 'the questioner's inference of the falsity of what can be prevented'. This means the questioner oversteps by arguing against something that the respondent is allowed to block.
Usurpation here is a procedural violation, not a logical fallacy.
3
Recognize the basis for prevention
The passage notes that 'there is no doubt about the validity of preventing taqrib' – the 'taqrib' is the part of the argument that is subject to prevention. Because prevention is legitimate, the two types of objections are out of bounds.
Taqrib is likely the immediate move that brings the entailment into play.
4
Note the exception (some scholars differ)
Although the author considers these objections usurpation, some scholars permitted using them against an entailment. This shows there was debate within the tradition about the boundaries of legitimate objection.
The phrase 'وإن جوز البعض' signals the minority view.
5
Apply the rule to a dialectical example
Consider a debate where the respondent claims A entails B. If the respondent can prevent the entailment itself (by blocking one of its conditions), then the questioner's estimated opposition (saying 'but what about such-and-such case') is usurpation because it attacks what can be prevented.
The key is whether the respondent can legitimately block the entailment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[فائدة] ولما كان الاستلزام مما يصح منعه كانت المعارضة التقديرية والنقض الإجمالي الشبيهي باعتبار الدعوى الضمنية غصبين عنده لأن الغصب استدلال السائل على بطلان ما صح منعه كما مر ولا شك في صحة منع التقريب ولذا لم يتعرض لهما وإن جوز البعض أن يكون الاعتراض الوارد على الاستلزام معارضة تقديرية أو نقضا إجماليا شبهيا.
[Benefit] Since entailment is something that can be prevented, the estimated opposition and the total similar refutation considering the implicit claim are considered two usurpations according to him, because usurpation is the questioner's inference of the falsity of what can be prevented, as has passed. And there is no doubt about the validity of preventing taqrib, so he did not mention them, although some permitted that the objection to the entailment could be an estimated opposition or a total similar refutation.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This short note (fā'ida) addresses a subtle point in dialectical procedure. The author states that since entailment (istilzām) is something that can be prevented (man'), two types of objections—the 'estimated opposition' (mu'āraḍa taqdīriyya) and the 'total similar refutation' (naqḍ ijmalī shabīhī)—are considered 'usurpations' (ghaṣb) in the opinion of the author (presumably Qadi Adud al-Din). The term ghaṣb here means that the questioner is overstepping by arguing for the falsity of something that is actually allowed to be prevented. Because it is perfectly valid to prevent (man') the taqrib itself, the author did not discuss these two types of objections in the main text. However, he notes that some scholars permitted using these objections against an entailment. The passage highlights the boundaries of legitimate objections in jadal.

Key Terms
استلزام
معارضة تقديرية
نقض إجمالي شبهي
غصب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because they are invalid forms of argument
B) Because they attack the entailment when it can be prevented
C) Because they are too vague
D) Because the questioner is not allowed to speak

Key Takeaway
In jadal, an estimated opposition or total similar refutation is usurpation when the entailment it attacks can be prevented by the respondent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Real and Metaphorical Meaning of Man' in Debate
Author explains that the term 'man'' is used metaphorically when applied to claims and reports in debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the literal and metaphorical usage of 'man' (prevention) in jadal, and identify the correct object of request when this term is used.

Core Idea
Man' in debate is always metaphorical when applied to reports or claims; the real object is the evidence for a premise.

In jadal (dialectical debate), precise terminology is crucial. The term 'man' carries a specific technical meaning that differs from everyday usage. This section clarifies that when a debater says 'this report is prevented' or 'this claim is prevented', they are speaking metaphorically. Understanding this distinction prevents confusion and ensures that requests for evidence are properly directed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term in context
Locate 'man' in the passage and note that it is defined as a technical term in jadal, not as ordinary forbidding.
2
Extract the literal meaning
Find the definition: 'طلب الدليل على مقدمة الدليل من حيث هي مقدمة' – it is requesting evidence for a premise of the evidence.
This is the real, literal meaning in the debaters' terminology.
3
Recognize metaphorical application
The author states that applying 'man' to reports (naql) or claims (mad'aa) is metaphorical because they are not premises themselves.
The metaphor involves ellipsis: the premise is omitted and the attribute is transferred.
4
Understand the two interpretations
Abu al-Fath offers two possibilities: metaphor in attribution (the prevention is actually for the evidence) or metaphor in the term (the word is used for another meaning). The author adopts the latter.
This clarifies the precise nature of the figure of speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيان المنع الحقيقي والمجازي
قبل القائل القاضي عضد الدين في رسالته للآداب: لا يمنع النقل والمدعى إلا مجازا. والمراد بالنقل ما نقل من الكلام، وهو بالمعنى المصدر، لا بمعنى المنقول. والمدعى هو الدعوى. ومعنى لا يمنع: لا يستعمل لفظ المنع وما يشتق منه في طلب الدليل عليهما إلا مجازا، لغويا أو عقليا أو حذفيا. قال أبو الفتح: يحتمل أن يكون المراد بالمنع هنا معناه الحقيقي، والمجاز في قوله إلا مجازا عبارة عن المجاز في النسبة، فقولك هذا النقل ممنوع معناه أن دليله ممنوع. ويحتمل أن يكون المراد بالمنع استعمال لفظ المنع، والمجاز في الطرف. والمصنف حمل على الأخير. وبيان ذلك أن المنع في اصطلاحهم طلب الدليل على مقدمة الدليل من حيث هي مقدمة. ولما لم يكن النقل والمدعى من حيث هما مقدمة من دليل، فقولك هذا النقل ممنوع أو هذا المدعى ممنوع مجاز، لفيه حذف ومجاز في النسبة. وأما إذا استعملت لفظا آخر غير الألفاظ الأربعة (المنع والممانعة والمناقضة والنقض التفصيلي) وما يشتق منها في طلب الدليل عليهما، فلا مجاز فيه، بل هو حقيقة، كأن تقول لا نسلم هذا النقل أو لا نسلم هذا المدعى أو هو مطلوب البيان. هذا التفصيل في المدعى الغير المدلل. وأما إذا كان المدعى مدللا، فلا يمنع حقيقة ولا مجازا.
Section on explaining the real and metaphorical man'. Before: the speaker Qadi Adud al-Din in his Risala fi al-Adab: 'The report and the claim are not prevented except metaphorically.' The meaning of report here is what is transmitted in the debate, and it is in the verbal noun sense, not the thing transmitted. The claim is the assertion. The meaning of 'not prevented' is that the word 'man'' and its derivatives are not used in requesting evidence for them except metaphorically, either linguistically, rationally, or by ellipsis. Abu al-Fath said: It is possible that the intended meaning of 'man'' here is its real meaning, and the metaphor in the phrase 'except metaphorically' is a metaphor in attribution, so your saying 'this report is prevented' means its evidence is prevented. And it is possible that the intended meaning is the usage of the word 'man'', and the metaphor is in the term. The author adopted the latter. Explanation: 'man'' in their terminology is requesting evidence for a premise of the evidence insofar as it is a premise. Since the report and the claim as such are not premises of an evidence, your saying 'this report is prevented' or 'this claim is prevented' is metaphorical, because it involves ellipsis and metaphor in attribution. As for if you use another word other than the four terms (man', muman'a, munaqada, naqd tafsili) and their derivatives in requesting evidence for them, there is no metaphor in it, rather it is real, like saying 'we do not accept this report' or 'we do not accept this claim' or 'it is requested to be explained'. This detail is for the claim that is not evidenced; but if the claim is evidenced, it is not prevented really or metaphorically.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This section forms a detailed analysis of the term 'man'' (prevention) as used in dialectical debate. The author begins by citing Qadi Adud al-Din's statement: 'The report (naql) and the claim (mad'aa) are not prevented except metaphorically.' He clarifies that 'report' here refers to the act of reporting (verbal noun), not the reported content, and 'claim' is the assertion under debate. The meaning is that one does not literally say 'this report is prevented' in the sense of requesting evidence; it is a metaphorical usage. Two interpretations are offered by Abu al-Fath: one that takes 'man'' in its real sense but the metaphor lies in the attribution (i.e., 'this report is prevented' means its evidence is prevented), and another that takes the metaphor in the term 'man'' itself (i.e., using the word to mean something else). The author (al-Walidiyya?) adopts the latter. The core explanation: in the debaters' terminology, 'man'' specifically means requesting evidence for a premise of the evidence (i.e., for an undemonstrated premise). Since the report and the claim themselves are not premises of an evidence in their own right, applying 'man'' to them is metaphorical—it involves an ellipsis (the premise is omitted) and a metaphor in attribution. However, if one uses other words like 'لا نسلم' (we do not accept) or 'مطلوب البيان' (requested for explanation), these are literal because they are directly used to request evidence. This entire discussion applies only when the claim is not already evidenced; if the claim is already supported by evidence, it cannot be 'prevented' at all, neither literally nor metaphorically. The passage thus clarifies the precise technical meaning of a key term in dialectic, preventing misuse.

Key Terms
منع
نقل
مدعى
مجاز
استعمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the debaters' terminology, what does 'man'' (prevention) literally mean?
A) Forbidding the opponent to speak
B) Requesting evidence for a premise of the evidence
C) Denying the claim outright
D) Attacking the opponent's character

Key Takeaway
The term 'man' in jadal literally means requesting evidence for a premise; when applied to reports or claims, it is metaphorical, involving ellipsis.

Definition of Manع and Its Metaphorical Usage in Jadal
Author defines 'manع' and explains its literal/metaphorical application to naql and muddaʿā.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between literal and metaphorical uses of 'manع' in dialectical argumentation and identify the correct type when applied to naql and muddaʿā.

Core Idea
Manع in jadal is requesting evidence for a premise, not the whole claim.

In dialectical debate (jadal), the term 'manع' has a precise technical meaning: it is the request for evidence specifically on a premise of the opponent's evidence. But when applied to a transmission (naql) or a claim (muddaʿā) that are not themselves premises, the usage becomes metaphorical. This lesson unpacks the distinction and shows how to recognize literal and metaphorical applications in actual debate.

Guided Path
1
Understand the technical definition of manع
The author states that 'manع' in dialectical terminology means 'requesting evidence for a premise of the evidence, insofar as it is a premise, i.e., not evidenced.' Identify this definition and note that it specifically targets an undemonstrated premise.
This is the core definition; all subsequent discussion builds on it.
2
Identify why naql and muddaʿā are not premises
The author argues that a transmission (naql) or a claim (muddaʿā) in themselves are not premises of an evidence. Therefore, saying 'this naql is blocked' or 'this claim is blocked' is a metaphorical usage because the term is being applied to something that is not a premise.
3
Distinguish literal vs metaphorical wording
The passage explains that using the specific terms like 'mamnūʿ' (blocked) for naql or muddaʿā is metaphorical. However, using alternative phrases like 'lā nusallimu' (we do not accept) or 'mā huwa musallam' (it is not accepted) is literal, because those words are properly used for requesting evidence on any statement.
The metaphor lies in the predicate, not the subject.
4
Recognize the metaphorical case when the claim is already evidenced
If the muddaʿā is already supported by evidence (e.g., 'this shaykh is human because he is rational'), then requesting evidence for it is a metaphor in the relation, because the real request is for a premise of the evidence, not the claim itself.
5
Apply the distinction in a jadal setting
When debating, if an opponent says 'this transmission is blocked', recognize that this is metaphorical; the proper response is to ask for the specific premise they are challenging. If they say 'we do not accept this transmission', that is literal.

Source Text

ويان ذلك أن المنع وكذا الألفاظ المذكورة في اصطلاحهم طلب الدليل على مقدمة الدليل من حيث هي مقدمة أي غير مدللة. ولما لم يكن النقل من حيث هو نقل والمدعى من حيث هو مدعى مقدمة من دليل، فقولك هذا النقل ممنوع أو مناقض أو منقوض بنقض تفصيلي إلى غير ذلك، وهذا المدعى ممنوع أو مناقض إلى غير ذلك، مجاز لغوي، ولا يخفى ما فيه من المجاز في النسبة أو المجاز المرسل، لأن المجاز إنما يكون في قوله ممنوع لا في مجموع هذا النقل ممنوع. وأما إذا استعملت لفظا آخر غير الألفاظ الأربعة وما يشتق منها في طلب الدليل عليهما فلا مجاز فيه أصلا بل هو حقيقة، كأن تقول لا نسلم هذا النقل أو هو غير مسلم، أو لا نسلم هذا المدعى أو هو مطلوب البيان. وأما إذا كان المدعى مدللا كأن تقول هذا الشيخ إنسان لأنه ناطق وكل ناطق إنسان، فطلب الدليل عليه بأي لفظ كان من الألفاظ المستعملة مجاز في النسبة، والمراد طلب الدليل على شيء من مقدمات دليله بقرينة صارفة عما هو له. ولما كان طلب الدليل على النقل المقارن للتصحيح نادرا لم يتعرض له، مع أن المقصود بالذات من هذا البحث بيان المنع على المدعى، وأما بيان المنع على النقل فهو استطرادي. ويكفيك هذا البيان هنا، علمك الله ما لم تعلم.
And the explanation of that is that 'manع' and similarly the mentioned terms in their terminology are: requesting evidence for a premise of the evidence, insofar as it is a premise, i.e., not evidenced. And since 'naql' (transmission) as such and 'muddaʿā' (claim) as such are not premises of an evidence, your saying 'this naql is blocked' or 'contradicted' or 'refuted by particular refutation' etc., and 'this muddaʿā is blocked' or 'contradicted' etc., is a linguistic metaphor. And it is not hidden what is in it of metaphor in the relation or the unrestricted metaphor, because the metaphor is only in the word 'blocked', not in the totality of 'this naql is blocked'. And if you use another wording other than the four terms and their derivatives in requesting evidence for them (naql and muddaʿā), then there is no metaphor in it at all, rather it is literal, like when you say 'we do not accept this naql' or 'it is not accepted', or 'we do not accept this muddaʿā' or 'it is required of evidence'. And if the muddaʿā is evidenced, like if you say 'this shaykh is human because he is rational, and every rational is human', then requesting evidence for it with any wording from the used terms is a metaphor in the relation, and the meaning is requesting evidence for something from the premises of his evidence by a contextual indication turning it away from what it is for. And since requesting evidence for the naql that is accompanied by correction is rare, he did not address it, while the intended purpose of this discussion is the explanation of 'manع' upon the muddaʿā; as for the explanation of 'manع' upon the naql, it is tangential. And this explanation is sufficient for you here; may Allah teach you what you do not know.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion of the term 'manع' (blocking) and its cognates in the context of dialectical argument (jadal). The author begins by defining 'manع' in the technical terminology of the dialecticians: it is the request for evidence on a premise of the evidence, specifically when that premise is not itself evidenced. He then argues that when you say 'this naql (transmission) is blocked' or 'this claim is blocked', it is a metaphorical usage (majāz lughawī), because 'naql' and 'muddaʿā' are not premises of an evidence in themselves. The metaphor may be in the predicate 'mamnūʿ' (blocked) or in the relational attribution (majāz fī al-nisba). However, if one uses a different phrase like 'we do not accept this naql' or 'this claim is required of evidence', that is literal, because the words are used in their proper meaning. The author then considers the case where the claim is already evidenced, e.g., saying 'this shaykh is human because he is rational, and every rational is human'. Requesting evidence for such an evidenced claim is again metaphorical, because the real request is for evidence on a premise of the evidence, not on the claim itself. The discussion of naql is deemed tangential; the main focus is on the muddaʿā. The passage ends with a supplication for knowledge.

A) When applied to a claim that is not evidenced.
B) When applied to a transmission that is not yet corrected.
C) When a different wording like 'lā nusallimu' is used instead.
D) When the claim is already evidenced.

Key Takeaway
The term 'manع' in jadal is technically defined as requesting evidence for a premise, and its application to non-premises like naql or muddaʿā is metaphorical, while alternative expressions are literal.

Types of Prevention: Literal and Figurative
Author explains the logical and linguistic dimensions of prevention (manع) in dialectic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any instance of prevention (manع) in a dialectical argument into one of the five forms based on the literalness or figurativeness of the subject, predicate, and attribution.

Core Idea
Prevention is not always literal; context determines whether it is a metaphor in the predicate, subject, or relation.

In dialectical argumentation, the questioner often demands evidence for a claim by saying 'I prevent that premise.' But this prevention can be interpreted in multiple ways depending on whether the terms and relations are used literally or figuratively. This lesson unpacks the author's detailed analysis of prevention types, helping you recognize the logical and linguistic nuances behind a simple objection.

Guided Path
1
Understand the base example
The author gives the example: 'This shaykh is a human because he is rational, and every rational is a human.' The prevention (request for evidence) can be directed at the claim or its components. Grasp this typical syllogistic proof structure.
2
Identify the literal vs. figurative dimensions
The prevention request may be literal if the words are used in their real sense. But it can be figurative if the request uses a word in a non-literal way, such as 'claim' meaning 'premise' by necessity. Distinguish between metaphor in the word (linguistic) and metaphor in the relation (attribution).
Keep in mind the three components: subject (musnad ilayh), predicate (musnad), and attribution (isnād).
3
Learn the fivefold classification
The passage enumerates five cases: (1) all three components literal → literal prevention; (2) predicate metaphorical; (3) subject metaphorical; (4) attribution deletion metaphorical; (5) attribution rational metaphorical. Each leads to a different type of prevention: literal, metaphorical (allegory), deletion metaphor, or rational metaphor.
The fifth type (rational metaphor) is when the attribution itself is not real but intellectual, e.g., 'the king built the city' where the king is not the direct builder.
4
Apply the classification to new examples
Given a dialectical exchange, determine which form of prevention applies. For instance, if a mu'tarid says 'I prevent that Zayd is rational when you mean Zayd is rational by essence', check whether subject, predicate, and attribution are used literally. The classification clarifies the logical force of the objection.
This skill helps in evaluating whether a prevention is valid or requires further clarification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

You say: 'This shaykh is a human because he is rational, and every rational is a human.' Then the request for evidence upon it (i.e., upon the claim) by any word from the used words means its attribution is a metaphor in the relation, and the intended meaning is the request for evidence on something from the premises of his evidence by a contextual clue diverting from what it is for. This is if he did not intend from the claimant the premise and did not put it in the position of the annexed. But if he intended from him the premise by a relationship of necessity, then the word of the claim is a linguistic metaphor, and if he put it in the position of the annexed, then it is a deletion metaphor. The used words and the relation are literal, so the intended meaning is that there is a metaphor in the relation by possibility, i.e., it is possible that it is a metaphor in the relation. And it may be said that the specification is based on custom, for that estimation and intention is not in their custom. And since the request for evidence on the transmitted report accompanying correction is rare, he did not address it, although the essential purpose of this investigation is that prevention is against the claim. As for explaining prevention against the transmitted report, it is digressive, and the explanation of the discussion therein will come later. And it is possible that the claim generalizes the transmitted report because the transmitted report is in reality a claim, and its being placed opposite the claim in some places is of the type of placing the specific against the general. And this explanation suffices you (i.e., the explanation of the more specific meaning of prevention). Here, may Allah teach you, O child, what you did not know of the sciences and functions, oriented and non-oriented, and their application in the sciences. And it is understood from the speech of some of the virtuous that anything that accepts prevention, if it is prevented by the word of prevention, its position is such that either all of the predicate, subject, and attribution are literal, or one of the first two is metaphorical, or the third is a rational metaphor. These are five forms. So the request for evidence on the occasion of the first form by any consideration is literal prevention; on the occasion of the second and third forms, it is metaphorical prevention in the manner of allegory; on the fourth, it is deletion metaphorical prevention; on the fifth, it is rational metaphorical prevention. And it is as if it is a new terminology from him.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is an extended commentary on the concept of prevention (manع) in dialectical argumentation. The author begins with an example: 'This shaykh is a human because he is rational, and every rational is a human.' This is a typical syllogism. The opponent then demands evidence for the claim. The author explains that requesting evidence can be figurative (مجاز) in several ways. If the request is for evidence on a premise of the proof, it may be a metaphor in the relation (مجاز في النسبة) if the evidence is not explicitly requested as a premise. There are multiple layers: if the claimant (مدعي) does not intend the premise, the request is a metaphor in relation; if he does intend the premise, the word 'claim' itself is a linguistic metaphor. The author then introduces a fivefold classification: 1) all three elements (predicate, subject, attribution) are literal → prevention is literal. 2) predicate is metaphorical. 3) subject is metaphorical. 4) attribution is deletion metaphorical. 5) attribution is rational metaphorical. This classification helps in understanding the nature of the objection. The author concludes that this is a new terminology. The passage uses Islamic dialectical examples, centering on proof and objection in argumentation.

Key Terms
المنع
المدعي
المجاز
الإسناد

Key Takeaway
The classification of prevention into five types—literal, metaphorical (predicate/subject), deletion metaphorical, and rational metaphorical—provides a precise tool for analyzing objections in dialectical argumentation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify each of the following prevention statements according to the five forms: (a) 'I prevent that the world is created because you use 'created' figuratively.' (b) 'I prevent that Zayd is standing' where 'standing' is meant literally. (c) 'I prevent that the king built the city' where 'built' is attributed to the king rationally.
?

The Three Technical Meanings of 'Bahth' in Dialectic
Author defines 'bahth' and its technical uses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three technical meanings of 'bahth' and identify which is intended in a given dialectical text.

Core Idea
'Bahth' in dialectic means the whole exchange, not a single objection.

In this passage, the author clarifies a crucial term in jadal: 'bahth' (discussion). He distinguishes linguistic and technical meanings, showing that the intended meaning in dialectic is the mutual disputation between opponents. Understanding this helps us see why a muta'allil's attempt to shift to a new 'bahth' is problematic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic and three technical meanings of 'bahth'
The author lists: (1) carrying one thing onto another affirmatively, (2) proving a relation through reasoning, (3) disputation and discussion. Highlight that the third is intended in dialectic.
The first two are logical; the third is specifically dialectical.
2
Locate the author's argument on why a single objection is not a 'bahth'
The author points out that if 'bahth' means the sum of both opponents' speech, then the muta'allil's objection cannot be a 'bahth' by itself; it is only part of it.
This leads to the question: how can one transition to a new 'bahth' if the original is still ongoing?
3
Analyze the metaphorical explanations offered
The author considers calling the objection 'another discussion' as a metaphor, either because it causes a new discussion (cause-effect) or because it is part of the whole (part-whole).
This shows the flexibility of terminology in jadal.
4
Apply the definition to a dialectical scenario
Imagine a debate: the mudda'i makes a claim, the mu'tarid objects, and the muta'allil responds. According to this definition, the 'bahth' is the entire exchange, not just the muta'allil's response.
Thus, if the muta'allil attempts to change the subject, he is not starting a new discussion but failing to engage properly.

الشيء إيجابا. والثانى إثبات النسبة إيجابية كانت أو سلبية بطريق الاستدلاله والثالث للمناظرة والمباحثة والمراد هنا المعنى الثالث . فعلى هذا لا يكون اعتراض المعلل نفس البحث فكيف بعد انتقالا إلى بحث آخر أركان البحث بل هو محل البحث وليس للمعلل حينئذ كلام آخر سواء حتى يكون هو مع اعتراض السائل بحثا ، فالبحث لا يتحقق إلا باعتراض السائل على المدعى المدلل ويشعر بذلك تعريفهم المناظرة بالنظر من الجانبين فى النسبة بين الشيئين إظهارا للصواب. فإن المراد بالنسبة نسبة الدعوى فهذا يقتضى أن يوجد لكل من الجانبين كلام متعلق بتلك النسبة هذا مع أن قولهم انتقال إلى بحث آخر يشعر أن معنى الدعوى المجردة عن الدليل وكذا إبطاله يتحقق به البحث إذ حكموا بوجود الانتقال إلى بحث آخر عند منع السند ومنع صلاحيته وإبطالها ولعل الصواب أن قولهم إلى بحث آخر مجازا إما باعتبار تسمية مجموع المدعى الغير المدلل والاعتراض عليه بحثا على سبيل السبية وإما باعتبار تسمية كلام المعلل عند ذلك الانتقال بحثا مجازا بعلاقة السببية إذ هي سبب فى المادة لدفع السائل إياه انتهى . أقول ليس المراد بالنظر في تعريف المناظرة ترتيب أمور معلومة لتحصيل المجهول وإلا لم يقتال على المنع أصلا بل المراد منه حركة تخيلية ولاشك أن للمملك حركة تخيلية فى نسبة مدعاه ولو مجردا فاذا منعه السائل يكون حركة أيضا فى تلك النسبة فيتحقق النظر من الجانبين وهو البحث فالمدعى خارج عن الحركتين ومحل لهما فلئن سلمنا كونه جزءا منها فتسميته مبحثا لكونه جزءا سابقا من البحث فكأنه محله ، ولا يبعد أن يقال للبحث معنى رابع عرفى وهو الاعتراض يشهده تلك المقالات وهو المراد هنا فلا مجاز فى البحثين . واعلم. أن انتقال المعلل إلى بحث آخر إفحام وانقطاع المبحث الأول فانه لما كان الواجب على المعلل لمانع ( شيئا من كلامه ) هو الاثبات والاستدلال حقيقة أو حكما كما عرفت تفصيله من كلام المعلل ( فلا ينفعه منع المنع ، ومعناه منع صحته ) فكأنه ادعى ضمنا أن ( عند
Chapter on explaining the transition of the muta'allil to another discussion, which is his refuting something from the objector's speech while his objection remains. He said in al-Taqrir: 'Al-baḥth linguistically means investigation and examination. Terminologically, it is applied to three meanings: first, carrying one thing onto another affirmatively; second, proving a relation, whether affirmative or negative, by way of reasoning; third, disputation and discussion. The intended meaning here is the third meaning.' So on this basis, the muta'allil's objection is not itself the discussion (baḥth), so how can it be a transition to another discussion? Rather, it is a part of it, since discussion (mubāḥatha) is the sum of the speech of both opponents. Unless it is said that his objection is called another discussion because it is a cause for it, or because it is a part of it, in the manner of naming the cause after the effect or naming the part after the whole. He also said: 'There is a doubt here: that baḥth in the sense of disputation is not applied to one speech of the two opponents but to the sum of both speeches. So calling the claim a discussion would require that there be no discussion at the time of preventing a non-evidenced claim, nor at the time of invalidating it, because the claim is outside the pillars of the discussion; rather, it is the subject of the discussion, and the muta'allil then has no other speech that together with the questioner's objection constitutes a discussion. So discussion is only realized by the questioner's objection to an evidenced claim. This is indicated by their definition of disputation as 'consideration from both sides concerning the relation between two things to manifest the truth.' For the intended relation is the relation of the claim. This requires that each of the two sides have speech pertaining to that relation. This is despite the fact that their statement 'transition to another discussion' suggests that the meaning of the claim stripped of evidence, as well as its invalidation, realizes discussion, because they ruled that transition to another discussion occurs at the prevention of the premise and the prevention of its validity and its invalidation. Perhaps the correct view is that their statement 'to another discussion' is figurative, either by considering the sum of the non-evidenced claim and the objection to it as a discussion in a causal sense, or by considering the muta'allil's speech at that transition as a discussion metaphorically by a causal relationship, since it is a cause in substance for repelling the questioner from it.' I say: The intended meaning of 'consideration' (naẓar) in the definition of disputation is not the arrangement of known matters to attain the unknown, otherwise there would be no talk about prevention at all. Rather, the intended meaning is an imaginary movement. There is no doubt that for the one who makes a claim, there is an imaginary movement in the relation of his claim, even if it is stripped. So when the questioner prevents it, there is also a movement in that relation, so consideration from both sides is realized, and that is the discussion. The claim is outside the two movements and a locus for them. If we grant that it is a part of them, then calling it a discussion (mabḥath) because it is a prior part of the discussion, it is as if it is its locus. It is not far-fetched to say that baḥth has a fourth customary meaning, which is objection (i‘tirāḍ), as witnessed by those articles, and that is what is intended here, so there is no metaphor in the two discussions. Know that the muta'allil's transition to another discussion is a refutation and termination of the first discussion. For since the duty of the muta'allil towards one who prevents something of his speech is to prove and reason truly or virtually, as you have already learned the details from the speech of the muta'allil, then 'prevention of prevention' does not benefit him, and its meaning is preventing its validity. So it is as if he implicitly claimed that when (unfinished).
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of the concept of 'transition to another discussion' (intiqāl al-muta‘allil ilā baḥth ākhar) in dialectical argumentation. The author begins by defining 'baḥth' (discussion) linguistically and terminologically, noting three technical meanings: (1) affirming one thing of another, (2) proving a relation through reasoning, and (3) disputation and discussion, which is the intended meaning here. The key point is that the muta'allil (the one who provides the cause/evidence) sometimes tries to shift to a new line of argument when his opponent objects. This transition is problematic because if 'baḥth' means the entire exchange between two opponents, then a single objection cannot be called a 'baḥth' on its own. The author explores whether the transition is a new discussion or part of the original one, considering metaphorical extensions. He then addresses a doubt: the definition of disputation as 'consideration from both sides' implies that both sides must have a contribution; the claim itself is not part of the discussion but its subject. However, the author suggests a fourth customary meaning of 'baḥth' as 'objection' (i‘tirāḍ), which resolves the issue. Finally, he affirms that such a transition is an 'ifḥām' (refutation) and termination of the first discussion, as the muta'allil's duty is to prove his claim, not to shift the topic. The passage uses technical terms from the Islamic sciences of jadal (dialectics) and is rich in conceptual analysis.

Why the Muta'allil's Transition is a Refutation
Author concludes that transition to another discussion is an ifham and termination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the muta'allil's attempt to transition to another discussion is a sign of defeat and terminates the original discussion.

Core Idea
Shifting the argument topic in a debate signals the loss of the original line of reasoning.

Building on the definition of 'bahth', this lesson examines the ruling on the muta'allil's transition to another discussion. The passage concludes that such a move is an 'ifḥām' (refutation) and cuts off the original discussion. Understanding why helps us evaluate the effectiveness of argumentative strategies in jadal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the muta'allil's duty in the debate
The muta'allil is required to prove his claim with evidence (ṭarīq al-istidlāl). This is his primary responsibility.
His role is to support his initial position, not to escape to new ground.
2
Recognize the transition as a shift in argumentative focus
When the muta'allil faces an objection, instead of defending his evidence, he may start a new line of discussion. The passage calls this 'intiqāl ilā baḥth ākhar'.
This is a common tactic but is here deemed ineffective.
3
Analyze the consequence: it is an ifḥām (refutation) and termination
The author explicitly states: 'اعلم أن انتقال المعلل إلى بحث آخر إفحام وانقطاع المبحث الأول' – it is a refutation and termination of the first discussion.
By transitioning, the muta'allil implicitly admits his original argument could not stand.
4
Evaluate why this move fails to benefit him
The author explains that 'man‘ al-man‘' (prevention of prevention) does not help him. He cannot counter an objection by simply changing the subject; he must engage with the objection directly.
Thus, the transition is effectively a surrender of the original point.

Chapter on explaining the transition of the muta'allil to another discussion, which is his refuting something from the objector's speech while his objection remains. He said in al-Taqrir: 'Al-baḥth linguistically means investigation and examination. Terminologically, it is applied to three meanings: first, carrying one thing onto another affirmatively; second, proving a relation, whether affirmative or negative, by way of reasoning; third, disputation and discussion. The intended meaning here is the third meaning.' So on this basis, the muta'allil's objection is not itself the discussion (baḥth), so how can it be a transition to another discussion? Rather, it is a part of it, since discussion (mubāḥatha) is the sum of the speech of both opponents. Unless it is said that his objection is called another discussion because it is a cause for it, or because it is a part of it, in the manner of naming the cause after the effect or naming the part after the whole. He also said: 'There is a doubt here: that baḥth in the sense of disputation is not applied to one speech of the two opponents but to the sum of both speeches. So calling the claim a discussion would require that there be no discussion at the time of preventing a non-evidenced claim, nor at the time of invalidating it, because the claim is outside the pillars of the discussion; rather, it is the subject of the discussion, and the muta'allil then has no other speech that together with the questioner's objection constitutes a discussion. So discussion is only realized by the questioner's objection to an evidenced claim. This is indicated by their definition of disputation as 'consideration from both sides concerning the relation between two things to manifest the truth.' For the intended relation is the relation of the claim. This requires that each of the two sides have speech pertaining to that relation. This is despite the fact that their statement 'transition to another discussion' suggests that the meaning of the claim stripped of evidence, as well as its invalidation, realizes discussion, because they ruled that transition to another discussion occurs at the prevention of the premise and the prevention of its validity and its invalidation.
