Preclusion of Preclusion: A Useless Move in Jadal
Author states that preclusion of preclusion does not benefit the interlocutor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a debater commits 'man' al-man'' and explain why it fails to meet the burden of proof.

Core Idea
Rejecting an objection without proof is a dead end—only substantive refutation advances the debate.

In dialectical debate (jadal), the interlocutor (mu'allim) has a clear duty when challenged: he must provide proof or refute the objection. This lesson introduces a prohibition against a common yet useless move—simply dismissing the opponent's preclusion without addressing its substance. Understanding this rule is crucial for effective argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the roles in the jadal scenario
Recognize the mu'allim (claimant) and the māni' (objector who raises a preclusion). The mu'allim is responsible for proving his claim.
The passage uses 'al-mu'allil' and 'al-māni''.
2
Recognize the mu'allim's duties when a preclusion occurs
The mu'allim must provide proof via one of several methods: adducing evidence, invalidating the opponent's evidence, refining, changing, or transferring the evidence.
These are detailed earlier in the text.
3
Contrast beneficial moves with 'preclusion of preclusion'
Beneficial moves engage the objection's substance. 'Man' al-man'' is simply denying the validity of the preclusion without proof.
The author defines it as 'preclusion of its validity'.
4
Understand why man' al-man' is useless
It does not produce proof (lā yūjibu al-ithbāt) and leads to a dead end (inghām) in the discussion.
The mu'allim fails his duty to prove.
5
Apply the rule in a real debate
When analyzing a debate exchange, check if the mu'allim's response is substantive or merely dismissive. If dismissive, it is man' al-man' and non-beneficial.
This helps evaluate argument quality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل) لما ذكر فيما سبق ما ينفع المعلل وما ينفع السائل وما لا ينفعه أراد أن يذكر ما لا ينفع المعلل واعتنى بشأنه فأورد له فصلا مستقلا وما ذكره من حض ما ينفعه فاستطرادي ( لما كان الواجب على المعلل عند منع المانع ( شيئا من كلامه ) هو الإثبات والاستدلال مطلقا إما بإقامة الدليل عليه أو بإبطال السند المساوي أو بالتحرير أو بتغيير الدليل أو بالانتقال إلى دليل آخر (كما عرفت تفصيله ) وعند نقض الناقض ومعارضة المعارض هو دفع النقض والمعارضة على دليله فاشتغاله بما لا يفيده إنجام وانقطاع للبحث ) فلا ينفعه منع المنع ) مطلقا لأنه لا يوجب الإثبات ) ومعناه منع صحته)
Chapter: When he mentioned earlier what benefits the interlocutor and what benefits the questioner and what does not benefit him, he intended to mention what does not benefit the interlocutor and paid attention to its matter, so he devoted a separate chapter to it. What he mentioned regarding what benefits him is incidental. Since the duty of the interlocutor when the objector precludes something from his speech is absolute proof and demonstration, either by adducing evidence for it or by invalidating the equivalent evidence or by refining or by changing the evidence or by transferring to another evidence (as you have learned its detail), and when the critic criticizes and the opponent opposes, he must repel the criticism and opposition on his evidence, so occupying himself with what does not benefit him is a dead end and a cessation of the discussion. Therefore, preclusion of preclusion does not benefit him at all, because it does not produce proof, and its meaning is preclusion of its validity.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage, which opens a new chapter in the Waldiyya, establishes a fundamental rule in the etiquette of dialectical debate (jadal). The author states that when an interlocutor (al-mu'allim) is met with a preclusion (man') from the opponent (al-māni'), his duty is to provide proof or refutation by one of several methods: adducing evidence, invalidating the opponent's evidence, refining the argument, changing the evidence, or moving to another argument. He must also defend his evidence against criticism and opposition. Any activity that does not serve this purpose is a dead end (inghām) and causes the discussion to break off. Specifically, the author says 'preclusion of preclusion does not benefit him' — that is, simply rejecting the opponent's objection by saying 'I do not accept your preclusion' without addressing its basis does not constitute proof and thus is useless. The meaning of 'preclusion of preclusion' (man' al-man') is to deny the validity of the opponent's objection. This is a crucial point: the mu'allim must engage with the substance of the objection, not merely dismiss it.

Key Terms
منع المنع
المعلل
المانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because it does not produce proof or establish the claim, it merely denies the opponent's objection without substance.
C) Because it is equivalent to accepting the opponent's claim.
D) Because it is only allowed when the opponent is a mubtadi' (innovator).

Key Takeaway
In jadal, the mu'allim must engage with the substance of objections; mere denial (man' al-man') is a dead end and does not fulfill the burden of proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Three Meanings of 'Search' and Its Role in Disputation
Author defines 'search' and its three terminological meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the three terminological meanings of 'search' (al-baḥth) is intended in a given dialectical passage and explain why the other meanings are not applicable.

Core Idea
Search in dialectics means disputation, not just any investigation.

In this passage, the author clarifies the technical term 'search' (al-baḥth) used in the context of jadal. He distinguishes its linguistic meaning from three terminological meanings, selecting the third as the intended sense. This precision is crucial for understanding why an objection is not itself a separate search but part of the discussion. Understanding this distinction will help you correctly identify the roles of the interlocutor and objector in a debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic meaning of baḥth
The author gives the lexical meaning: 'examination and investigation.' Note that this is the general sense.
2
List the three terminological meanings
(1) predication, (2) establishment of a relation by inference, (3) disputation and discussion. Understand each one.
These are technical terms in the Islamic logical tradition.
3
Determine which meaning is intended here
The author explicitly says the intended is the third. Why? Because the context is dialectical debate.
The passage says: 'The intended here is the third meaning.'
4
Apply the meaning to the concept of objection
If search means disputation, then a single objection cannot be the whole search; it is only part of it. This sets up the problem of whether a transfer to another search occurs.

أركان البحث بل هو محل البحث وليس للمعلل حينئذ كلام آخر سواء حتى يكون هو مع اعتراض السائل بحثا ، فالبحث لا يتحقق إلا باعتراض السائل على المدعى المدلل ويشعر بذلك تعريفهم المناظرة بالنظر من الجانبين في النسبة بين الشيئين إظهارا للصواب. فإن المراد بالنسبة نسبة الدعوى فهذا يقتضي أن يوجد لكل من الجانبين كلام متعلق بتلك النسبة هذا مع أن قولهم انتقال إلى بحث آخر يشعر أن معنى الدعوى المجردة عن الدليل وكذا إبطاله يتحقق به البحث إذ حكموا بوجود الانتقال إلى بحث آخر عند منع السند ومنع صلاحيته وإبطالها ولعل الصواب أن قولهم إلى بحث آخر مجازا إما باعتبار تسمية مجموع المدعى الغير المدلل والاعتراض عليه بحثا على سبيل السبية وإما باعتبار تسمية كلام المعلل عند ذلك الانتقال بحثا مجازا بعلاقة السببية إذ هي سبب في المادة لدفع السائل إياه انتهى . أقول ليس المراد بالنظر في تعريف المناظرة ترتيب أمور معلومة لتحصيل المجهول وإلا لم يقتال على المنع أصلا بل المراد منه حركة تخيلية ولا شك أن للمدعي حركة تخيلية في نسبة مدعاه ولو مجردا فإذا منعه السائل تكون حركة أيضا في تلك النسبة فيتحقق النظر من الجانبين وهو البحث فالمدعي خارج عن الحركتين ومحل لهما فلئن سلمنا كونه جزءا منها فتسميته مبحثا لكونه جزءا سابقا من البحث فكأنه محله ، ولا يبعد أن يقال للبحث معنى رابع عرفي وهو الاعتراض يشهده تلك المقالات وهو المراد هنا فلا مجاز في البحثين . واعلم. أن انتقال المعلل إلى بحث آخر إفحام وانقطاع المبحث الأول فانه) لما كان الواجب على المعلل لمانع ( شيئا من كلامه ) هو الإثبات والاستدلال حقيقة أو حكما كما عرفت تفصيله من كلام المعلل ( فلا ينفعه منع المنع ، ومعناه منع صحته )
Chapter on explaining the transfer of the interlocutor to another discussion, which is the questioner's rejection of something from the objector's speech while his objection remains. He said in al-Taqrīr: ‘Search’ (al-baḥth) in language means examination and investigation. In terminology, it is used in three meanings: first, the predication of a thing upon a thing affirmatively; second, the establishment of a relation, whether affirmative or negative, by way of inference; third, for disputation and discussion (al-munāẓara wa al-mubāḥatha). The intended here is the third meaning. Accordingly, the objection of the interlocutor is not itself the search, so how can it be a transfer to another search? Rather, it is part of it, since discussion consists of the totality of the two opponents’ speech. However, it may be said that the interlocutor’s objection is called another search because it is a cause leading to it or because it is a part of it, by way of calling the cause by the name of the effect or calling the part by the name of the whole. He also said: There is a doubt here for me: that ‘search’ in the sense of disputation is not applied to the speech of one of the two opponents but to the totality of their speech. So calling the claim a ‘search’ would imply that search does not exist when the claimant precludes what is not supported, nor when he invalidates, because the claimant is outside the pillars of the search; rather, he is the object of the search, and the interlocutor at that time has no other speech such that it together with the questioner’s objection would constitute a search. So search is only realized by the questioner’s objection to the supported claim. This is suggested by their definition of disputation as ‘looking from both sides at the relation between two things to manifest correctness’. For what is meant by ‘relation’ is the relation of the claim. This requires that there exists from each side speech related to that relation. This is despite the fact that their statement ‘transfer to another search’ suggests that the meaning of the bare claim without evidence, and likewise its invalidation, realizes search, since they ruled that transfer to another search occurs at preclusion of the evidence, preclusion of its suitability, and its invalidation. Perhaps the correct view is that their statement ‘to another search’ is metaphorical, either by calling the totality of the unsupported claim and the objection to it a search by way of causality, or by calling the speech of the interlocutor at that transfer a search metaphorically by the relation of causality, since it is a cause in the matter for the questioner to repel it. End. I say: What is meant by ‘looking’ in the definition of disputation is not the arrangement of known things to obtain the unknown, otherwise they would not quarrel over preclusion at all; rather, what is meant is an imaginative movement. And there is no doubt that the claimant has an imaginative movement regarding the relation of his claim, even if bare. So when the questioner precludes him, there is also a movement regarding that relation, so ‘looking from both sides’ is realized, and that is the search. Thus the claimant is outside the two movements and is a locus for them. If we concede that he is part of them, then he is called a ‘search’ because he is a prior part of the search, as if he is its locus. It is not far-fetched to say that ‘search’ has a fourth conventional meaning, which is objection, witnessed by those statements, and that is what is meant here, so there is no metaphor in the two ‘searches’. And know that the transfer of the interlocutor to another search is a silencing and a cessation of the first discussion. For) since the duty of the interlocutor when the precluder precludes something from his speech is absolute proof and demonstration, actually or effectively, as you have learned the details from the speech of the interlocutor, (so preclusion of preclusion does not benefit him, and its meaning is preclusion of its validity.”
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion on the concept of 'transfer to another search' (intiqāl ilā baḥth ākhar) in dialectical debate. It begins by defining 'search' (al-baḥth) linguistically and terminologically, giving three meanings: predication, establishing relation, and disputation. The intended meaning here is the third (disputation). This raises a problem: if the interlocutor (al-mu'allim) makes an objection to the opponent's claim, that objection cannot itself be the entire discussion; rather it is part of it, since discussion involves both parties. The author then presents a doubt (shubha) from the text al-Taqrīr: if 'search' means the totality of speech, then calling the bare claim a 'search' is problematic, because the claimant is outside the search. The resolution proposed is that 'transfer to another search' is used metaphorically: either the unsupported claim plus objection is called a search due to causality, or the interlocutor's speech itself is called a search because it causes the opponent to respond. The author then offers his own analysis: the 'looking' in the definition of disputation is not a rigorous inference but an imaginative movement (ḥaraka takhayyuliyya). The claimant has this movement toward his claim, and the opponent's preclusion adds another movement, thus both sides 'look' and discussion occurs. The claimant is not part of the search but its locus. Alternatively, 'search' can have a fourth conventional meaning: 'objection'. Thus there is no metaphor. The passage concludes by reiterating that transferring to another discussion is a dead end and that preclusion of preclusion is not beneficial. This section clarifies an important terminological point in the theory of jadal.

Key Terms
البحث (al-baḥth)
المناظرة (al-munāẓara)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which terminological meaning of 'search' is intended in the context of 'transfer to another search'?
A) Predication
B) Establishment of a relation by inference
C) Disputation and discussion
D) Examination in language

Key Takeaway
The term 'search' (al-baḥth) in this dialectical context specifically means disputation (munāẓara), not mere investigation or inference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify which of the three terminological meanings of baḥth is used in the following sentence from a usul work: 'The baḥth of this issue involves preclusion of evidence.' (Answer: the second meaning, establishment of a relation by inference, because it's about inferential reasoning.)

Is an Objection a Separate Search? Resolving the Doubt in al-Taqrīr
Author resolves whether an objection constitutes a separate search.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why calling an objection a 'search' is metaphorical or based on a fourth conventional meaning, using the analysis from the passage.

Core Idea
An objection is not itself a search but a part or cause of it.

After defining 'search', the author raises a doubt: if search is the total speech of both opponents, then calling the claimant's speech (without evidence) a search is problematic. The resolution involves metaphorical usage or proposing a fourth meaning. This lesson walks you through the doubt and its resolution as presented in the text.

Guided Path
1
Understand the doubt
The doubt is that 'search' as disputation cannot apply to one side's speech alone. So calling the claimant's objection a 'search' seems wrong.
The claimant's speech without evidence is not part of the pillars of search.
2
Examine the metaphorical solutions
(a) Calling the combined speech (unsupported claim + objection) a search by causality; (b) Calling the interlocutor's speech a search because it causes the opponent to respond.
These are two proposed metaphors from al-Taqrīr.
3
Consider the author's alternative
The author suggests that 'looking' in the definition of disputation is not rigorous inference but imaginative movement; thus even a bare claim involves movement, and when combined with the objection, both sides are involved.
This reframes what 'looking' means.
4
Propose a fourth meaning
The author says it's not far-fetched that 'search' has a fourth conventional meaning: 'objection'. This avoids metaphor.
This directly solves the problem by redefining the term.

فصل في بيان انتقال المعلل إلى بحث آخر وهو دفع السائل شيئا من كلام المعترض مع بقاء اعتراضه . قال في التقرير : البحث في اللغة الفحص والتفتيش. وفي الاصطلاح : يطلق على ثلاثة معان : الأول حمل الشيء على الشيء إيجابا. والثاني إثبات النسبة إيجابية كانت أو سلبية بطريق الاستدلال. والثالث للمناظرة والمباحثة والمراد هنا المعنى الثالث ، فعلى هذا لا يكون اعتراض المعلل نفس البحث فكيف بعد انتقالا إلى بحث آخر بل يكون جزءه إذ المباحثة عبارة عن مجموع كلام الخصمين إلا أن يقال سمي اعتراض المعلل بحثا آخر لكونه سببا إليه أو لكونه جزءا منه من قبيل تسمية السبب باسم المسبب أو تسمية الجزء باسم الكل وقال أيضا فيه لي Ineffective Preclusions in Jadal
Author enumerates ineffective preclusions in jadal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify three types of ineffective preclusions (man') in a jadal exchange and explain why each fails to benefit the interlocutor.

Core Idea
Not every objection advances proof; only preclusions that lead to proof are beneficial in debate.

In the art of jadal, not every objection is a valid move. This passage from the Waldiyya classifies several types of preclusion that are technically permissible but practically useless. Understanding these keeps your argumentative strategy sharp and prevents wasted moves.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three main ineffective preclusions
From the passage, list: (1) preclusion of the essence of preclusion (man' dhāt al-man'), (2) preclusion of evidence (man' al-sanad), (3) preclusion of suitability of evidence (man' ṣalāḥiyyat al-sanad).
2
Analyze why each is ineffective
For each, note the reason from the text: (a) preclusion of preclusion is mukābara, asking for proof of a request for proof; (b) preclusion of definitive evidence is valid but does not produce proof; preclusion of possible evidence is invalid because possibility does not repel possibility; (c) preclusion of suitability is valid but does not prove the claim.
The principle: the interlocutor must prove the premise or claim.
3
Apply the principle of benefit
The Hanafi commentator states that any preclusion that does not necessitate proving the precluded premise is useless. This applies to all three types above.
4
Test with a debate scenario
In a mock debate, if the opponent precludes your evidence, and you respond by precluding their preclusion, you are not proving your claim. Recognise this as an ineffective move.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما منع ذات المنع فهو مكابرة إذ المنع طلب الدليل ولا معنى لطلب الدليل على طلب الدليل (تقريره لا نسلم صحة ورود هذا المنع) لذلك الممنوع (ولم لا يجوز أن يكون الممنوع بديهيا جليا أو مسلما عندك وكذا لا ينفعه منع السند) مطلقا ( الذي ذكر على سبيل القطع ) لأنه لا يوجب الإثبات أيضا . وأما الذي ذكر على سبيل الجواز فلا يصح منعه إذ الجواز لا يدفع الجواز، وبالجملة أن منع صحة المنع صحيح لأن المانع ادعى صحة منعه ضمنا فاعرف لكن لا ينفع المعلل ، وكذا منع السند الذي ذكر على سبيل القطع صحيح لكن لا ينفع المعلل . وأما السند الذي ذكر على سبيل الجواز فلا يصح منعه إذ الجواز لا يدفع الجواز لأن الشك لا يقابل الشك فلا يدفعه . واعلم أنهم اختلفوا في أن السند هل هو في الحقيقة من قبيل التصديقات أو من قبيل التصورات فذهب البعض إلى الأول ، والبعض الآخر إلى الثاني ، ولا يخفى أن كون منع السند الذي على سبيل القطع صحيحا إنما يتأتى على الأول لا على الثاني فليتأمل ( قال الشارح الحنفي: منع المعلل مطلقا المنع ( أي منع السائل ومنع ما يؤيده ) من تنوير السند سواء كان على سبيل القطع أو على سبيل الجواز لا يوجب إثبات المقدمة الممنوعة الذي يجب على المعلل عند منع المانع شيئا من كلامه ، وكل ما لا يوجب إثبات المقدمة لا ينفع المعلل فمنع المنع ومنع ما يؤيده لا ينفع المعلل فهو دليل لكل واحد من عدم النفعين انتهى ، وكذا منع منع المدعى ومنع ما يؤيده لا يوجب إثبات المدعى الذي يجب على المعلل عند منع المانع ، ويمكن تعميم كلام الشارح الحنفي بوجه يشمل كلتا الصورتين ، لأن أول كلامه عام وإن كان آخر كلامه مخصوصا بالمقدمة) وكذا لا ينفعه منع صلاحية السند للسندية ) أي لكونه سندا لأنه لا يوجب إثبات الواجب عليه يريد أنه منعها صحيح لأن المانع لما ذكر السند فكأنه ادعى صلاحية سنده للسندية والدعوى الضمنية يصح منعها ، لكن هذا المنع لا ينفع المعلل (مستندا بعمومه ) مطلقا أو من وجه أو بمباينته لنقيض الممنوع صحيح وروده والدعوى الضمنية تقبل المنع لكنه ليس بنافع لعدم إثباته الممنوع . وأما منع ذات المنع فهو مكابرة ، إذ المنع طلب الدليل ولا معنى لطلب الدليل على طلب الدليل المشاهد ) تقريره أنا لا نسلم صحة ورود هذا المنع ) لذلك الممنوع ) لم لا يجوز أن يكون الممنوع بديهيا جليا ) أو مسلما عندك ) وكذا لا ينفعه منع ) ذات ( السند الذي ذكر على سبيل القطع ) وكذا لا ينفع المعلل منع جواز السند الذي ذكر على سبيل التجويز كأن يقول السائل : لا نسلم هذا لم لا يجوز أن يكون الأمر كذا فيقول المعلل لا نسلم جواز كون الأمر كذا وكذا . وأما منع ذات ذلك السند فانه وإن لم ينفع ليس بانتقال إلى بحث آخر بل هو مكابرة أيضا إذا الجواز لا يدفع الجواز ، وإنما لا ينفع المعلل تلك المنوع لما ) قال الشيخ الحنفي : منع المنع ومنع ما يؤيده ) من السند ( لا يوجب إثبات المقدمة ) الممنوعة ( الذي يجب على المعلل عند منع المانع شيئا من المقدمة) ( كلامه انتهى ) وكذا لا ينفعه منع صلاحية السند للسندية مستندا بعمومه ) مطلقا أو من وجه أو بمباينته لنقيض الممنوع ، لأن المانع لما أسنده فكأنه أدعى ضمنا صلاحيته للسندية
As for preclusion of the essence of the preclusion, it is stubborn denial (mukābara), since preclusion is a request for proof, and there is no meaning to requesting proof for a request for proof. His explanation: 'We do not accept the validity of this preclusion' for that which is precluded, 'and why may not the precluded be self-evident or accepted by you?' Similarly, preclusion of the evidence (man' al-sanad) in absolute terms, that which is mentioned as a definitive statement, does not benefit him, because it does not produce proof either. As for that which is mentioned as a possible statement, its preclusion is not valid, since possibility does not repel possibility. In summary, preclusion of the validity of the preclusion is valid because the precluder implicitly claimed the validity of his preclusion, so know, but it does not benefit the interlocutor. Likewise, preclusion of the evidence that is mentioned as definitive is valid but does not benefit the interlocutor. As for the evidence mentioned as possible, its preclusion is not valid, since possibility does not repel possibility, because doubt does not confront doubt, so it does not repel it. And know that they differed as to whether the evidence (al-sanad) is in reality a type of assent (taṣdīqāt) or a type of conception (taṣawwurāt); some went for the first, others for the second. It is not hidden that the validity of preclusion of evidence that is definitive only holds on the first view, not on the second, so reflect. (The Hanafi commentator said: 'The interlocutor's absolute preclusion of the preclusion (i.e., preclusion of the questioner and preclusion of what supports it) from the outcome of the evidence, whether definitive or possible, does not necessitate proving the precluded premise, which is obligatory upon the interlocutor when the precluder precludes something from his speech. And everything that does not necessitate proving the premise does not benefit the interlocutor. Thus, preclusion of preclusion and preclusion of what supports it does not benefit the interlocutor; he is a proof for each of the two non-benefits. End. Likewise, preclusion of the preclusion of the claimant and preclusion of what supports it does not necessitate proving the claim, which is obligatory upon the interlocutor when the precluder precludes. And it is possible to generalize the speech of the Hanafi commentator in a way that includes both forms, because the beginning of his speech is general, although the end of his speech is particular to the premise.) Similarly, preclusion of the suitability of the evidence for being evidence (i.e., its being a support) does not benefit him, because it does not necessitate proving what is obligatory upon him. He means that its preclusion is valid because when the precluder mentions the evidence, he implicitly claims the suitability of his evidence for being evidence, and an implicit claim is valid to preclude, but this preclusion does not benefit the interlocutor, based on its generality absolute, or partial, or its contradiction to the opposite of the precluded: its validity of occurrence and the implicit claim accept preclusion, but it is not beneficial because it does not prove the precluded. As for preclusion of the essence of the preclusion, it is stubborn denial, since preclusion is a request for proof, and there is no meaning to requesting proof for a witnessed request for proof. His explanation: 'We do not accept the validity of this preclusion' for that which is precluded, 'why may not the precluded be self-evident or accepted by you?' Likewise, preclusion of the essence of the evidence that is mentioned as definitive does not benefit him. And likewise, preclusion of the possibility of the evidence that is mentioned by way of probability does not benefit the interlocutor, such as when the questioner says: 'We do not accept this; why may it not be that the matter is thus?' and the interlocutor says: 'We do not accept the possibility of the matter being thus.' As for preclusion of the essence of that evidence, even if it does not benefit, it is not a transfer to another discussion, but it is also stubborn denial, since possibility does not repel possibility. Those preclusions only do not benefit the interlocutor because (what the Hanafi scholar said: 'Preclusion of preclusion and preclusion of what supports it from the evidence does not necessitate proving the precluded premise, which is obligatory upon the interlocutor when the precluder precludes something of the premise.' His speech ends.) Likewise, preclusion of the suitability of the evidence for being evidence, based on its generality absolute, or partial, or its contradiction to the opposite of the precluded, because when the precluder uses it, he implicitly claims its suitability for being evidence.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a detailed enumeration and analysis of the various types of preclusion (man') that do not benefit the interlocutor (al-mu'allim) in a dialectical debate. It covers: (1) Preclusion of the essence of the preclusion (man' dhāt al-man') – simply rejecting the opponent's objection without reason, which is 'stubborn denial' (mukābara) because the opponent's preclusion itself is a request for proof, and requesting proof for a request is meaningless. (2) Preclusion of the evidence (man' al-sanad) – when the opponent's evidence is presented as definitive (qaṭ'), precluding it is technically valid but does not produce proof; if the evidence is presented as possible (jawāz), precluding it is invalid because possibility does not repel possibility. (3) Preclusion of the suitability of the evidence (man' ṣalāḥiyyat al-sanad) – denying that the opponent's evidence is valid as a support; this again does not lead to proof. The passage also includes a note on a scholarly disagreement about whether evidence (sanad) belongs to the category of assent (taṣdīq) or conception (taṣawwur), which affects the validity of preclusion. Then the Hanafi commentator is quoted to reinforce the point: any form of preclusion that does not result in proving the required premise or claim is useless. The text concludes by distinguishing these ineffective preclusions from a legitimate 'transfer to another discussion'. Throughout, the key principle is that the interlocutor must provide positive proof or refutation, not merely negate the opponent's moves. Examples from debate practice illustrate each type.

Key Terms
منع السند
مكابرة
صلاحية السند للسندية

Checkpoint
Why is preclusion of the evidence (man' al-sanad) when the evidence is presented as possible (jawāz) considered invalid?
A) Because possible statements cannot be precluded at all.
B) Because possibility does not repel possibility; it would be like matching doubt with doubt.
C) Because the interlocutor must first prove the evidence is impossible.
D) Because the Hanafi commentator prohibited it.

Key Takeaway
In jadal, only preclusions that produce proof are beneficial; mere denial or matching possibilities is ineffective.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a jadal exchange, the opponent says: 'We do not accept that the world is temporally originated because it might be eternal.' The interlocutor replies: 'I do not accept that preclusion.' Identify the type of ineffective preclusion and explain why it does not benefit.

The Rule of Possibility: Possibility Does Not Repel Possibility
Author explains why precluding possible evidence is invalid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule 'al-jawāz lā yadfa' al-jawāz' to evaluate whether a preclusion of evidence is valid in a jadal debate.

Core Idea
A possible objection cannot refute a possible claim; doubt does not repel doubt.

A key principle in this passage is that when evidence is presented as merely possible (jawāz), precluding it is inherently invalid. Understanding why requires seeing that possibility is not sufficient to refute possibility. This rule prevents debates from degenerating into a mere clash of possibilities.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish definitive (qaṭ') vs possible (jawāz) evidence
In the passage, the evidence (sanad) can be presented as a definitive statement (qaṭ') or as a possible scenario (jawāz). The type affects whether preclusion is valid.
Definitive: 'Zaid is standing.' Possible: 'Zaid may be standing.'
2
Apply the rule to definitive evidence
Preclusion of definitive evidence is technically valid (you can deny it) but does not produce proof, so it's useless.
3
Apply the rule to possible evidence
Preclusion of possible evidence is invalid because 'possibility does not repel possibility' and 'doubt does not confront doubt'. The opponent's possible alternative is not refuted by another possibility.
Example: Opponent says 'It may be X.' Interlocutor says 'It may not be X.' This is a stalemate.
4
Connect to broader debate strategy
The interlocutor must provide actual proof, not just match possibilities. This ensures debates aim for certainty, not mere plausibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما منع ذات المنع فهو مكابرة إذ المنع طلب الدليل ولا معنى لطلب الدليل على طلب الدليل (تقريره لا نسلم صحة ورود هذا المنع) لذلك الممنوع (ولم لا يجوز أن يكون الممنوع بديهيا جليا أو مسلما عندك وكذا لا Distinguishing Types of Objections in Jadal
Author distinguishes valid non-beneficial objections from stubborn objections in jadal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish between man' sihhat al-man' and man' dhat al-man', and explain the rules for objecting to the proof (sand) in a dialectical exchange.

Core Idea
In jadal, objecting to an objection's validity is valid but not beneficial; objecting to the act of objecting is stubborn.

In dialectical exchanges (jadal), not all objections are created equal. The author of this passage draws a critical distinction between objecting to the validity of an objection (man' sihhat al-man') and objecting to the essence of an objection (man' dhat al-man'). Understanding this distinction is essential for evaluating the legitimacy and benefit of objections in a debate. The passage also covers objecting to the proof (sand) and its classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of man'
In the Arabic text, find the phrases منع صحة المنع and منع ذات المنع. Also note the reference to منع السند (objecting to the proof). Understand the literal meaning of each.
منع صحة المنع = objection to the validity of the objection; منع ذات المنع = objection to the essence of the objection; منع السند = objection to the proof.
2
Analyze the implicit claim
The author says that when a person objects, he implicitly claims that his objection is valid (صحة وروده). Therefore, objecting to that implicit claim (man' sihhat al-man') is a valid move because implicit claims are subject to objection.
This is a key insight: even though the objection is not explicit, it can be targeted.
3
Understand why man' dhat al-man' is stubbornness
The author argues that objection (man') is a request for proof (طلب الدليل). Objecting to the act of objecting means requesting proof on a request for proof, which is meaningless (لا معنى للطلب على الطلب). Hence, it is stubbornness (مكابرة).
This distinguishes a legitimate move from an illegitimate one.
4
Evaluate the rules for objecting to the proof (sand)
The passage distinguishes between sand mentioned as certain (قطعي) and as possible (جوازي). Objecting to a certain proof is valid but not beneficial; objecting to a possible proof is not valid because possibility does not repel possibility.
Also note the scholarly disagreement: is sand a tasdiq (assent) or tasawwur (concept)? The validity of objecting to certain proof depends on it being an assent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أى صحة وروده بقرينة اللاحق، لأن المانع لما منع شيئا من كلامه فكأنه ادعى أن منعه يصح وروده والدعوى الضمنية تقبل المنع لكنه ليس بنافع. وأما منع ذات المنع فهو مكابرة إذ المنع طلب الدليل ولا معنى للطلب على الطلب. تقريره: لا نسلم صحة ورود هذا المنع لذلك الممنوع، ولم لا يجوز أن يكون الممنوع بديهيا جليا أو مسلما عندك. وكذا لا ينفعه منع السند مطلقا الذي ذكر على سبيل القطع لأنه لا يوجب الإثبات أيضا. وأما الذي ذكر على سبيل الجواز فلا يصح منعه إذ الجواز لا يدفع الجواز. وبالجملة أن منع صحة المنع صحيح لأن المانع ادعى صحة منعه ضمنا فاعرف لكن لا ينفع المعلل. وكذا منع السند الذي ذكر على سبيل القطع صحيح لكن لا ينفع المحلل. وأما السند الذي ذكر على سبيل الجواز فلا يصح منعه إذ الجواز لا يدفع الجواز لأن الشك والشك لا يقابل الشك فلا يدفعه. واعلم أنهم اختلفوا في أن السند هل هو في الحقيقة من قبيل التصديقات أو من قبيل التصورات فذهب البعض إلى الأول، والبعض الآخر إلى الثاني، ولا يخفى أن كون منع السند الذي على سبيل القطع صحيحا إنما يتأتى على الأول لا على الثاني فليتأمل.
Meaning: the validity of its occurrence due to the context of the subsequent [objection]? Because when the objector objects to something in the responder's speech, it is as if he claimed that his objection is valid in its occurrence, and an implicit claim is subject to objection, but it is not beneficial. As for objecting to the essence of the objection, it is stubbornness, because objection is a request for proof, and there is no meaning to requesting proof on a request [for proof]. Its restatement: We do not accept the validity of this objection's occurrence for that which is objected to; why may it not be that the objected matter is self-evident or conceded by you? And similarly, objecting to the proof absolutely, which is mentioned as certain, does not benefit him because it does not establish proof as well. As for that which is mentioned as possible, it is not valid to object to it because possibility does not repel possibility. In summary, objecting to the validity of the objection is valid because the objector implicitly claimed the validity of his objection—know this—but it does not benefit the responder. Similarly, objecting to the proof mentioned as certain is valid but does not benefit the responder. As for the proof mentioned as possible, it is not valid to object to it because possibility does not repel possibility, since doubt does not confront doubt and thus does not repel it. Know that they differed over whether the proof is actually of the category of assents or of the category of concepts; some went to the first, others to the second. It is not hidden that the validity of objecting to the proof that is certain only holds on the first [view], not the second, so reflect.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage defines the types of objections (man') in jadal, specifically distinguishing between objecting to the validity of an objection (man' sihhat al-man') and objecting to the essence of the objection (man' dhat al-man'). The author explains that when an objector objects to a point, he implicitly claims his objection is valid; thus, objecting to that implicit claim is valid (since implicit claims are open to objection), but it is not beneficial to the responder because it does not establish the disputed premise. In contrast, objecting to the essence of the objection—i.e., rejecting the very act of objecting—is pure stubbornness, because an objection is a request for proof, and requesting proof on top of a request is meaningless. The passage then turns to objecting to the proof (man' al-sand). The proof may be presented as certain (qat'i) or as possible (ijtimali). Objecting to a certain proof is valid but does not benefit the responder, while objecting to a possible proof is not valid, because possibility can only be countered by impossibility, not by another possibility. The author also notes a scholarly disagreement: is the proof (sand) a kind of assent (tasdiq) or a kind of concept (tasawwur)? The ruling that objecting to a certain proof is valid only holds on the view that it is an assent. This passage is crucial for understanding the tactical options in dialectical exchange.

Key Terms
المنع (al-man'): An objection raised by the questioner (sa'il) against a premise or argument of the responder (mu'allil) in jadal. [Note: No significant shift—stable term in adab al-bahth.]
منع صحة المنع (man' sihhat al-man'): Objecting to the validity of the objection itself, i.e., claiming that the objection is not properly applicable. [Note: Specific to jadal literature; not a standard mantiq term.]
منع ذات المنع (man' dhat al-man'): Objecting to the very act of objecting, considered stubbornness (mukabara). [Note: Specific to jadal; not used in pure logic.]
السند (al-sand): The proof or evidence presented by the objector to support his objection. [Note: In jadal, sand refers to the supporting argument for an objection; in other contexts it may mean 'support' or 'reliance'.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the objected matter is always self-evident.
D) Because the proof is always certain.

Key Takeaway
In jadal, objecting to the validity of an objection is a valid move but does not advance the debate; objecting to the act of objecting is logically invalid and considered stubbornness.

Types of Objections in Jadal: What Benefits the Responder
Commentator explains that objecting to the objection and its support does not prove the premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between beneficial and non-beneficial objections in a jadal exchange according to the Hanafi commentator.

Core Idea
Objecting to the objection itself is stubbornness; only establishing the premise or nullifying the essence of the proof benefits the responder.

In Islamic dialectic (jadal), not every objection (man') is a winning move. The Hanafi commentator in this passage refines the rules: the responder must focus on establishing the disputed premise. Objecting to the objection itself or to what supports it, without establishing the premise, is futile. You need to know which moves are valid and which are merely transitions to another discussion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the responder's goal in jadal
The responder (mu'allil) aims to establish the objected premise. Any move that does not achieve that is non-beneficial.
2
Distinguish between objection to the objection vs. objection to the proof
Man' al-man' is objecting to the questioner's objection, while man' ma yu'ayyiduhu is objecting to the proof supporting that objection. Both are non-beneficial because they don't prove the disputed premise.
The commentator states: 'منع المنع ومنع ما يؤيده لا ينفع المعلل'.
3
Analyze the concept of suitability of the proof (salahiyyat al-sand)
Objecting to the suitability of the proof is valid but also non-beneficial because it doesn't establish the premise. It only challenges the implicit claim of the proof's validity.
The commentator says: 'منع صلاحية السند للسندية ... لا ينفعه'.
4
Understand the exception: nullifying the essence of the proof
If the responder can nullify the proof itself (dhat al-sand), that is beneficial because it removes the support of the objection. Similarly, nullifying the clarification (tanwir) that entails the opposite of the objected matter benefits the responder.
The commentator gives an example with 'إبطال السند الأخص' and notes: 'إبطال التنوير الذي يلزم لنقيض الممنوع ينفعه'.
5
Apply the rule to a simple jadal scenario
If the questioner says 'Why may it not be permissible?' and the responder says 'We do not accept the possibility,' that is stubbornness. Instead, the responder should tackle the premise directly.
The commentator calls this 'مكابرة' (stubbornness).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشارح الحنفى: منع المعلل مطلقا المنع (أى منع السائل ومنع ما يؤيده) من تنوير السند سواء كان على سبيل القطع أو على سبيل الجواز لا يوجب إثبات المقدمة الممنوعة الذي يجب على المعلل عند منع المانع مطلقا شيئا من كلامه، وكل ما لا يوجب إثبات المقدمة لا ينفع المعلل فمنع المنع ومنع ما يؤيده لا ينفع المعلل. وكذا لا ينفعه منع صلاحية السند للسندية، أي لكونه سندا لأنه لا يوجب إثبات الواجب عليه، يريد أنه منعها صحيح لأن المانع لما ذكر السند فكأنه ادعى صلاحية سنده للسندية والدعوى الضمنية يصح منعها لكن هذا المنع لا ينفع المعلل مستندا بعمومه مطلقا أو من وجه أو بمباينته لنقيض الممنوع. وأما منع ذات المنع فهو مكابرة، إذ المنع طلب الدليل ولا معنى لطلب الدليل على طلب الدليل. وكذا لا ينفع المعلل منع جواز السند الذي ذكر على سبيل التجويز، كأن يقول السائل: لا نسلم هذا، لم لا يجوز أن يكون الأمر كذا، فيقول المعلل: لا نسلم جواز كون الأمر كذا وكذا. وأما منع ذات ذلك السند فانه وإن لم ينفع ليس بانتقال إلى بحث آخر بل هو مكابرة أيضا إذ الجواز لا يدفع الجواز. وكذا لا ينفعه إبطال صلاحيته للسندية، أي لكونه سندا معتبرا مستدلا بعمومه مطلق أو من وجه أو بمباينته لنقيض الممنوع، كأن قال السائل: لا تسلم أنه ليس بإنسان، لم لا يجوز أن يكون حيوانا. فقال المعلل: صلاحية الحيوان للسندية باطل لأنه أعم من نقيض الممنوع، وهذا ليس بإبطال ذات السند، إذ لو كان إبطالا لذاته لنفع المعلل هنا لأن إبطال السند الأعم ينفع للمعلل. قال بعض الأفاضل: منع ذات السند غير مفيد، ومنع صلاحيته للسندية وإبطال تلك الصلاحية مفيدان. واعترض عليه بأنه إن أراد أنهما مفيدان للمعلل بأن يوجب إثبات الممنوع كإبطال ذات السند فهو غير صحيح، لأن السند إذا لم يصلح للسندية يبقى المنع مجردا وهو موجه أيضا. وإن أراد أنهما موجهان باعتبار قصد الانتقال إلى بحث آخر فمنع ذات السند موجه ومفيد باعتبار ذلك القصد. وأجيب بأنه أراد المعنى الثاني وأراد من منع ذات السند منع متعلق الجواز إذا كان السند مصدرا به فانه غير موجه بوجه أصلا إذ ليس في المقابلة. وكذا لا ينفعه إبطال عبارة المانع والناقض أو المعارض بمخالفتها القانون العربي كالصرف والنحو والعروض وغيرها. إنما اقتصر هنا على الإبطال ولم يذكر المنع تنبيها على ما اشتهر بين الطلبة إذ المعترض على العبارة مستدل وموجهها مانع. قال في التقرير: وكذا لا ينفع المعلل إبطال السند الأخص مطلقا أو من وجه وإبطال السند المبائن وإبطال تنوير السند ومنعه انتهى. لكن ينبغي أن يعلم أن إبطال التنوير الذي يلزم لنقيض الممنوع ينفعه إذ ذلك الإبطال يثبت الممنوع. فاشتغال المعلل بهذه الاعتراضات انتقال منه إلى بحث آخر يجب على السائل دفعه.
The Hanafi commentator said: The responder's objection absolutely to the objection (i.e., the questioner's objection and what supports it) from the clarification of the proof, whether it is on the basis of certainty or possibility, does not necessitate the establishment of the objected premise which is incumbent upon the responder when the objector objects to something in his speech absolutely, and everything that does not necessitate the establishment of the premise does not benefit the responder. So objecting to the objection and what supports it does not benefit the responder. Likewise, objecting to the suitability of the proof for being a proof, i.e., its being a proof, does not benefit him because it does not establish what is incumbent upon him. He means that this objection is valid because when the objector mentioned the proof, it is as if he implicitly claimed the suitability of his proof for being a proof, and an implicit claim is subject to objection, but this objection does not benefit the responder, relying on its generality absolutely, or partially, or on its difference from the opposite of the objected matter. As for objecting to the essence of the objection, it is stubbornness, because objection is a request for proof, and there is no meaning to requesting proof on a request for proof. Likewise, objecting to the possibility of the proof that is mentioned on the basis of possibility does not benefit the responder, such as when the questioner says: 'We do not accept this; why may it not be that the matter is such?' and the responder says: 'We do not accept the possibility of the matter being such.' As for objecting to the essence of that proof, even if it does not benefit, it is not a transition to another discussion but rather stubbornness also, because possibility does not repel possibility. Likewise, nullifying its suitability for being a proof, i.e., its being a reliable proof, by arguing with its generality absolutely or partially or by its difference from the opposite of the objected matter, does not benefit him, such as when the questioner says: 'We do not accept that it is not a human; why may it not be an animal?' and the responder says: 'The suitability of animal for being a proof is invalid because it is more general than the opposite of the objected matter.' And this is not a nullification of the essence of the proof, for if it were a nullification of its essence, it would benefit the responder here because nullifying the more general proof benefits the responder. Some virtuous scholars said: Objecting to the essence of the proof is not beneficial, but objecting to its suitability for being a proof and nullifying that suitability are beneficial. It was objected that if he means they are beneficial to the responder by necessitating the establishment of the objected matter like nullifying the essence of the proof, then it is incorrect, because if the proof is not suitable for being a proof, the objection remains abstract and it is also directed. But if he means they are directed by way of intending to transition to another discussion, then objecting to the essence of the proof is directed and beneficial by virtue of that intention. It was answered that he meant the second meaning, and by objecting to the essence of the proof he meant objecting to the related possibility when the proof is originally derived, then it is not directed at all because there is no comparison. Likewise, nullifying the wording of the objector, the critic, or the opponent due to its violation of the Arabic grammatical rule, such as morphology, syntax, prosody, etc. He only mentioned nullification and did not mention objection, as a reminder of what is well-known among students, that the one who objects to the wording is a proof-giver and the one who directs it is an objector. He said in al-Taqrir: Likewise, nullifying the specific proof absolutely or partially, nullifying the proof that is distinct, and nullifying the clarification of the proof and objecting to it does not benefit the responder. But it should be known that nullifying the clarification that is necessary for the opposite of the objected matter benefits him, because that nullification establishes the objected matter. So the responder's occupation with these objections is a transition from him to another discussion that the questioner must repel.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) by the Hanafi commentator on the rules of objections (man') in jadal. It begins by asserting that the responder's (mu'allil) objection to the objection itself (man' al-man') and to what supports it (man' ma yu'ayyiduhu) does not establish the disputed premise, and therefore is not beneficial. This holds whether the proof (sand) is presented as certain or possible. The commentator then explains that objecting to the suitability of the proof (salahiyyat al-sand) is valid because the objector implicitly claims its suitability, but it is not beneficial because it does not prove the premise. He reiterates that objecting to the essence of the objection (dhat al-man') is stubbornness. A distinction is made: objecting to the possibility of a proof that is stated as possible is not beneficial, and objecting to the essence of such a proof is also stubbornness because possibility does not repel possibility. The commentator then discusses nullifying the suitability of the proof (ibtal salahiyyat al-sand), giving an example: if the questioner objects to a premise by saying 'why may it not be an animal?', the responder might say 'the suitability of 'animal' as a proof is invalid because it is more general than the opposite of the objected matter.' This is not a nullification of the essence of the proof, which would be beneficial. There follows a scholarly disagreement: some say objecting to the suitability and nullifying it are beneficial, but an objection is raised that if they do not establish the premise like nullifying the essence, then they are not beneficial; if they are considered as transitions to another discussion, then even objecting to the essence is beneficial. The answer clarifies that the intended meaning is the second, and that objecting to the essence in the sense of objecting to a related possibility is not valid. Finally, the passage covers nullifying the opponent's wording (ibtal al-'ibarah) if it violates Arabic grammar rules; this nullification is a kind of proof (istidlal), not an objection. The commentator notes that nullifying the clarification (tanwir) that entails the opposite of the objected matter is actually beneficial. In sum, engaging in these types of objections represents a transition to a new discussion for the responder, which the questioner must address.

Key Terms
منع المنع
منع ما يؤيده
صلاحية السند للسندية
إبطال العبارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In jadal, only moves that directly establish or nullify the basis of the objection are beneficial; objecting to the objection itself is futile.

Two Valid Grounds for Rebutting a Denial in Jadal
Author explains two valid ways to rebut a denial in jadal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two valid grounds for rebutting a denial in jadal (self-evidence and opponent’s admission) and identify whether a rebuttal is dialectical-binding or truth-seeking.

Core Idea
In jadal, a denial is rebuttable only if the proposition is self-evident or admitted by the opponent — and the rebuttal is binding, not demonstrative.

In formal dialectical disputation (jadal), the opponent may deny a proposition. Not every rebuttal to that denial is effective. The author specifies two scenarios where the rebuttal is valid: when the denied proposition is self-evident (badihi jali) or when it is already accepted by the opponent (musallam). However, he stresses that these rebuttals are binding answers (jawab ilzami) and dialectical proofs (dalil jadali), not truth-seeking demonstrations. This lesson will guide you through both methods and help you understand their proper use in debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two grounds for rebuttal
Read the passage and locate the two distinct methods of invalidating a denial: one based on self-evidence (badiha) and one based on the opponent's admission (musallam). Underline the relevant phrases.
2
Reconstruct the self-evidence syllogism
The author provides a syllogism: 'The denied item is self-evident; every self-evident item has an invalid denial; therefore the denial is invalid.' Write it formally and identify premises and conclusion.
This is a categorical syllogism in the first figure.
3
Explain why it equals establishment
The author says this rebuttal is equivalent to establishing the denied item. Why? Because self-evident truths cannot be proven; showing the denial is invalid suffices to affirm the proposition.
Self-evident propositions are axiomatic.
4
Analyze the admission-based rebuttal
The mu'allil argues that the denied item is admitted by the opponent (musallam) — either from his school's necessities or explicit acceptance. The rebuttal is that the denial contradicts his own commitments.
This relies on the opponent's prior concessions.
5
Distinguish binding vs. truth-seeking answers
The author clarifies that these rebuttals are 'jawab ilzami' (binding) and 'dalil jadali' (dialectical). They aim to compel the opponent in the debate, not to establish objective truth. Under what circumstances are they appropriate?
They are valid in dialectical contexts, not when seeking demonstrative certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نعم بقى شيء ينفعه وهو إبطال المنع مستدلا عليه بداهة الممنوع بدلالة جلية وطريق الاستدلال أن يقال للمنوع بديهى جلى وكل بديهى جلى باطل المنع وكل ماطل المنع فهو ثابت فالممنوع ثابت وهذا الأبطال بمنزلة إثبات الممنوع إذ لا يتصور الاثبات فى البديهى وكذا ينفعه إبطال المنع مستدلا عليه بدعوى أن الممنوع مسلم عند المانع لكونه من ضروريات مذهبه او بوجه آخر وتقديرها أن هذا المنع باطل لأنه وارد على البديهى الجلى أو على ما هو مسلم عندك فكل منع شأنه ما ذكر باطل لكن هذا الإبطال جواب إلزامي ودليل جدلي لا تحقيقى فلا يصح عند إرادة إظهار الحق فكأنه قال إن الممنوع مسلم عندك وكل ما هو مسلم عندك ثابت عندك ولا يدعى حينئذ الرجوع عن تسليم ما سلمه إلا إذا لم يكن من ضروريات مذهبه ولم يكن بديهيا جليا وأما إذا كان بديهيا جليا أو من ضروريات مذهبه فلا اعتبار لرجوعه ومنعه
Yes, there remains something that benefits him: the invalidation of the denial by adducing the self-evidence of the denied item, with clear indication. The method of inference is to say: 'The denied item is self-evident and clear. Everything self-evident and clear has an invalid denial. Everything whose denial is invalid is established. Therefore, the denied item is established.' This invalidation is equivalent to establishing the denied item, since establishment of the self-evident is inconceivable. Similarly, it benefits him to invalidate the denial by adducing the claim that the denied item is admitted by the denier, because it is among the necessities of his school or for another reason. Its formulation is that this denial is false because it is directed at the self-evident and clear or at what is admitted by you. So every denial whose characteristic is as mentioned is false. But this invalidation is a binding answer and a dialectical proof, not a truth-seeking one. It is not valid when one intends to manifest the truth. It is as if he said: 'The denied item is admitted by you, and everything admitted by you is established by you.' And one cannot at that time claim retraction from what he admitted, unless it was not among the necessities of his school and was not self-evident and clear. But if it was self-evident and clear or among the necessities of his school, then his retraction and denial are of no consequence.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage discusses the valid rebuttals (إبطال المنع) available to the mu'allil (reasoner) in a dialectical disputation (jadal). The author identifies two specific cases where the mu'allil can effectively counter the opponent's denial (man'): first, when the denied proposition is self-evident (badihi jaliy), and second, when it is admitted (musallam) by the opponent himself. For the first case, the author provides a clear syllogistic formulation: the denied item is self-evident; every self-evident item has an invalid denial; therefore the denial is invalid and the denied item is established. This is presented as equivalent to proving the denied item, since self-evident truths cannot be proven through external arguments—they are foundational. For the second case, the mu'allil argues that the denial is false because it contradicts the opponent's own admissions, either from his school's necessities or other explicit agreements. The author then emphasizes that such rebuttals are binding (ilzami) and dialectical (jadali), not truth-seeking (tahqiqi). This means they aim to silence the opponent within the debate rather than establish objective truth. Therefore, if the goal is to manifest truth, these moves are inappropriate. Furthermore, if the opponent attempts to retract his admission after such a rebuttal, his retraction is disregarded if the admitted point was self-evident or a necessary part of his own doctrine. This illustrates the rules of engagement in formal dialectic.

Key Terms
إبطال المنع
بديهة
مسلم
جواب إلزامي
دليل جدلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Whenever the opponent is silent.

Key Takeaway
A valid rebuttal in jadal requires the denied proposition to be either self-evident or already accepted by the opponent; such rebuttals are dialectically binding but not demonstrative.

Definition of Mu'arada and Its Mawrid
Author discusses the definition of mu'arada, its mawrid, and critiques the famous definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two views on the subject (mawrid) of mu'arada and explain why the author departed from the famous definition.

Core Idea
Mu'arada requires establishing a contradictory, not just any opposite; its subject is debated between the claim and the evidence.

In dialectical debates (munazara), mu'arada is a key move to invalidate an opponent's argument. But what exactly counts as mu'arada, and what does it target? This passage defines mu'arada by contrasting it with mere generalities or divergences, and reveals a disagreement over whether its subject is the opponent's claim or the evidence. Understanding this distinction sharpens your ability to use mu'arada correctly in Islamic scholarly debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify what counts as mu'arada
The author begins by excluding establishing something more general or divergent — these do not set up a contradictory and thus do not invalidate the original. Mu'arada must establish a naqīḍ (contradictory). Notice the precision: not just any opposite, but one that directly refutes the original proposition.
2
Contrast the two views on mawrid
The passage reports a disagreement: some say mu'arada invalidates the argued claim by proving its opposite, so the mawrid is the claim (al-mudda'a al-mudallal). Others say it invalidates the evidence by proving the opposite of what the evidence claimed, so the mawrid is the evidence (al-dalīl). Understand how each view changes the target of the dialectical move.
The author considers the first view 'more apparent' (al-azhar).
3
Understand the objection to the famous definition
The famous definition is: 'Establishing evidence for the opposite of what the opponent established evidence for.' An objection is that this definition applies to usurpation (ghasb) — a trivial response that doesn't truly challenge. The author explains why the definition is still valid: 'establishing' here means 'invalidating by establishing', so mere contradiction without invalidation is excluded.
The author also mentions three answers to the objection that the definition is not exclusive.
4
See how the author defends his own definition
The author departs from the famous definition because of the objection that it includes unwarranted cases. He qualifies his definition to cover both schools on mawrid and to avoid the usurpation counterexample. Reflect on how a logical definition must be both comprehensive (jāmi') and exclusive (māni').
The defense uses the principle that mentioning the cause (establishing) can stand for the effect (invalidation).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إثبات الأعم مطلقا أو من وجه وإثبات المباين، فليس بمعارضة إذ بائباً نهما لا يثبت النقيض حتى يبطل العين. أعلم أنه لو قال بدل هذا التعريف إثبات السائل خلاف ما ادعاء المملل واستدل عليه المكان أخصر إذ الخلاف يشمل النقيض وما يستلزمه من المساوى والأخص مطلقا منه: وأجيب عنه بأن الخلاف شامل لما لا يستلزم النقيض من المباين والأعم مطلقا أو من وجه وإثباتهما لا يضر للطل. ويمكن أن يجاب عنه بأنه قصد التمهيد لما سيأتى ومزيد التوضيح. قال بعض الأفاضل اعلم أن مورد المنع هو المقدمة بالاتفاق وقد عرفته ومورد النقض هو الدليل على الأصح وستعرفه. واختلف في مورد المعارضة فمن قال إنها إبطال المدعي المدلل باثبات خلافه يقول إن موردها هو المدعى المدلل وهو الأظهر، ومن قال إنها إبطال الدليل بإثبات خلاف مدعاه يقول إن موردها هو الدليل. فان قلت لا ينطبق هذا التعريف كالتعريف المشهور، وهو إقامة الدليل على خلاف ما أقام عليه الخصم الدليل بأحد التعريفين فيكون تعريف المصنف مباينا. قات المراد من الاثبات والإقامة الإبطال بالاثبات والإقامة، لكن ذكر السبب وأريد المسبب إفادة أن التعريف منطبق للمذهبين وإشارة إلى أن التصريح بدعوى البطلان ليس بلازم بل يكفى الاثبات والاقامة فاندفع ما يمكن أن يقال إن كلا من التعريفين غير مانع لصدقهما على الغصب، فانه إذا قال المحلل هذا الشبح إنسان لأنه ناطق، وقال السائل لا نسلم كونه ناطقابل هو ليس بإنسان أوليس بحيوان لأنه فيس بناطق أو ليس بماش فان هذا الغصب يصدق عليه ذلك الاثبات والاقامة فاذا أريد بهما الابطال لا يصدقان عليه وإنما عدل عن التعريف المشهور لورود السؤال عليه بأنه غير مانع لتداوله على الاستدلال بحدوث العالم على تغيره فى المعارضة لمن استدل على وجود صانعه، وإن أجيب عنه أولا بأن المراد من الخلاف ما ينافيه، وثانيا بأنه ذكر العام وأريد الخاص، ولا دلالة للعام على الخاص باحدى الدلالات الثلاث. وإن أجيب عنه أيضا بأن التقييد بالخصم يخصصه وهو موضوع في عرفهم للمنافي.
And as for establishing the more general absolutely or from a certain aspect, and establishing the divergent, it is not mu'arada, because neither of them establishes the contradictory so as to invalidate the original. Know that if he had said instead of this definition 'the disputant's establishing the opposite of what the proponent claimed and argued' it would be more concise, since 'opposite' includes the contradictory and what entails it from the coextensive and the more specific absolutely. And it was answered that 'opposite' includes that which does not entail the contradictory from the divergent and the more general absolutely or from a certain aspect, and establishing them does not harm the proponent. And it can be answered that he intended preparation for what will come and additional clarification. Some distinguished scholars said: Know that the subject of denial is the premise unanimously, and you know it; and the subject of counter-evidence is the evidence according to the correct view, and you will know it. And they differed regarding the subject of mu'arada: whoever says it is invalidating the argued claim by establishing its opposite says its subject is the argued claim, which is more apparent; and whoever says it is invalidating the evidence by establishing the opposite of its claim says its subject is the evidence. So if you say: This definition does not correspond to the famous definition, which is 'The establishment of evidence for the opposite of what the opponent established evidence for, according to one of the two definitions,' so the author's definition would be divergent. Answer: The intended meaning of 'establishing' and 'producing' is 'invalidating by establishing and producing,' but he mentioned the cause and intended the effect, indicating that the definition applies to both schools and that explicitly claiming invalidity is not necessary; rather, establishing and producing suffices. So what could be said is refuted: that each of the two definitions is not exclusive because they apply to usurpation, for if the proponent says, 'This apparition is a human because it is rational,' and the respondent says, 'We do not grant that it is rational; rather, it is not a human or not an animal because it is not rational or not walking,' then this usurpation applies to that establishing and producing; but if what is meant by them is invalidation, they do not apply to it. And he only departed from the famous definition because of the objection that it is not exclusive because it includes the argument from the temporal origination of the world about its change in mu'arada against one who argued for the existence of its Maker, and if it is answered first that what is meant by 'opposite' is that which contradicts it, and second that he mentioned the general and intended the specific, but the general does not indicate the specific by any of the three indications, and if it is also answered that the restriction with the opponent specifies it, and it is in their convention for the contradictory.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage defines mu'arada (opposition in debate) and clarifies its subject (mawrid). The author begins by distinguishing mu'arada from merely establishing something more general or divergent, which does not produce a contradictory. He notes that if the definition used 'opposite' it would be more concise, but a possible objection is that 'opposite' includes things that do not entail a contradictory, like the more general or divergent. The author then explains the disagreement: some say mu'arada invalidates the claim by proving its opposite (so the subject is the claim), others say it invalidates the evidence by proving the opposite of what the evidence claimed (so the subject is the evidence). The author defends the definition against the charge that it does not match the famous definition: 'establishing evidence for the opposite of what the opponent established evidence for'. He argues that 'establishing' and 'producing' mean 'invalidating by establishing/producing', so the definition covers both schools and does not require explicit mention of invalidity. He then refutes the objection that the definition applies to usurpation (ghasb) - an example where the respondent merely denies or offers an alternative without establishing a genuine contradictory. Finally, he mentions three answers to the objection that the definition is not exclusive: (1) 'opposite' means contradictory, (2) the general term is intended for the specific, (3) the restriction to the opponent specifies it as contradictory.

مورد
نقيض
إبطال

C) One view says the subject is the opponent's character, the other says it is the argument's form.
D) One view says the subject is the universal, the other says it is the particular.

Key Takeaway
Mu'arada is a precise dialectical move that requires establishing a contradictory to invalidate, with its subject being either the claim or the evidence, and the author's refinement ensures it excludes trivial disagreements.

Types of Mu'arada in Dialectical Exchange
Author provides a concrete example of mu'arada with three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct the three types of mu'arada: contradictory, coextensive, and more specific, using a categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
Mu'arada uses contradictory, coextensive, or more specific arguments to oppose a claim.

In jadal, mu'arada is a powerful technique to oppose an opponent's claim without directly denying the evidence. The passage provides a concrete example: the proponent argues something is not human because it is a stone. The opponent can respond by establishing the contradictory (it is human because it is rational), the coextensive (it is a laugher because it is astonished), or the more specific (it is black because it is from Abyssinia). Understanding these three types helps you structure a valid opposition in any dialectical debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proponent's claim and evidence
Find the claim being argued: 'This thing is not a human.' The evidence is a categorical syllogism: 'It is a stone; every stone is not a human; therefore it is not a human.'
2
Analyze the opponent's three strategies
The opponent can oppose by establishing the contradictory (negating the original claim), the coextensive (equivalent meaning), or the more specific (a narrower category). In the example: contradictory — 'it is human because rational'; coextensive — 'it is a laugher because astonished'; more specific — 'it is black because from Abyssinia.'
3
Determine the logical relationship of each type
The contradictory directly negates the conclusion. The coextensive term equals the original predicate in extension. The more specific term is a subset of the original predicate. Each relationship ensures the opponent's argument can refute the claim.
Coextensive terms: 'human' and 'laugher' are coextensive in potentiality. More specific: 'black' is a type of human.
4
Formalize the opponent's contradictory as a categorical syllogism
The opponent says: 'It is rational; every rational is human; so it is human.' This is a first-figure Barbara syllogism (all R are H; all T are R; all T are H). Check validity: all premises are true, conclusion follows.
Similarly, the coextensive and more specific arguments can be formalized.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كأن ادعى المعلل لا إنسانية شيء واستدل عليها بأن قال هذا الشيء لا إنسان لأنه حجر وكل حجر لا إنسان فهو لا إنسان. فعارضه (الضمير إما راجع إلى المعلل أو المدعى المستفاد من ادعى أو إلى الدليل المستفاد من استدل) باثبات إنسانيته (أى إنسانية ذلك الشيء وهو النقيض بأن يقول إنه ناطق وكل ناطق إنسان فذلك الشيء إنسان) أو باثبات ضاحكيته (وهو المساوى بأن قال إنه متعجب وكل متعجب ضحك بالقوة) أو باثبات إنه زنجي (وهو الأخص بأن قال إنه إنسان من بلاد الحبشة وكل إنسان من بلاد الحبشة فهو زنجي). فللسائل عند إرادة تقرير المعارضة أن يقول للمعلل: دليلك وإن دل على ما ادعيت أى الشيء الذي ادعيته أى وإن استلزمه وقيل أى وإن ادعيت صحته بأن يلزم منه عين ما ادعيت أو ما يستلزمه من المساوى له أو الأخص منه مطلقا، وفيه أنه وإن كان صحيحا في نفسه لكنه ممالا يحتاج إليه في هذا للسكان بل هو قريب من الهذيان) لكن عندى ما ينفى أى دليل ينفى ما ادعيت يعنى ينتج خلاف مدعاك من النقيض أو المساوى أو الأخص مطلقا كما مر تصويره. ولا يجوز للسائل أن يقول وإن ثبت أو إن صدق بدل وإن دل فما كان استلزامه قطعيا لكلا يلزم التناقض. ولذا قال السمر قندى حقيقة المعارضة أن يسلم السائل دليل المعلل لا بمعنى اعتقاد ثبوته وإلا يلزم اعتقاد ثبوت مدلولة فيكون معارضته تناقضا بل بمعنى عدم التعرض له ويرد المدلول ويستدل على ما ينافيه فاعرف. ودفع المملل المعارضة إما بمنع بعض مقدمات دليل المعارض وهو المناقضة، أو باثبات المملل فساد دليله أى دليل المعارض بتخلف الحكم أو باستلزامه خصوص الفساد وهو النقض الإجمالي وسيأتي تفصيل النقض الإجمالي في المقالة الثالثة.
As if the proponent claimed non-humanity of something and argued for it by saying 'This thing is not a human because it is a stone, and every stone that is not a human is not a human.' So the opponent opposed (the pronoun referring either to the proponent or the inferred claim or the inferred evidence) by establishing its humanity (i.e., the humanity of that thing, which is the contradictory, by saying 'It is rational and every rational is a human, so that thing is a human') or by establishing its laughter (which is the coextensive, by saying 'It is astonished and every astonished is a laugher potentially') or by establishing that it is a black person (which is the more specific, by saying 'It is a human from Abyssinia and every human from Abyssinia is black'). So for the respondent, when intending to articulate the opposition, he should say to the proponent: 'Your evidence, even if it indicates what you claimed (i.e., the thing you claimed, i.e., even if it entails it, or it is said: even if you claim its correctness such that it entails the very thing you claimed or what entails it from the coextensive to it or the more specific absolutely, and in it that although it is correct in itself, it is something not needed in this context, rather it is close to nonsense), but I have what negates (i.e., evidence that negates what you claimed), meaning it produces the opposite of your claim from the contradictory or the coextensive or the more specific absolutely, as the illustration preceded.' And it is not permissible for the respondent to say 'even if it is established' or 'even if it is true' instead of 'even if it indicates', if its entailment is certain, because then contradiction would occur. Hence al-Samarqandi said: The reality of mu'arada is that the respondent concedes the proponent's evidence, not in the sense of believing its truth (otherwise he would believe the truth of its implicans, so his opposition would be a contradiction), but in the sense of not addressing it, and he refutes the implicans and argues for what contradicts it, so know this. And the proponent repels the opposition either by denying some premises of the opponent's evidence, which is munaqada, or by the proponent establishing the corruption of his (i.e., the opponent's) evidence by the failure of the ruling or its entailing specific corruption, which is the summary counter-evidence, and the detail of the summary counter-evidence will come in the third article.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed example of mu'arada and explains how it is conducted and defended. The example: the proponent claims something is not human because it is a stone (using a categorical syllogism). The opponent can oppose by establishing the contradictory (it is human, because it is rational and every rational is human), the coextensive (it is a laugher potentially because it is astonished), or the more specific (it is black because it is from Abyssinia). The author then gives etiquette for the opponent: he should not say 'even if your evidence is established' because that would concede the evidence, leading to contradiction. Instead, he should say 'even if your evidence indicates what you claim, I have evidence that negates it.' The author cites al-Samarqandi: the opponent concedes the evidence only in the sense of not challenging it, not in believing it. Finally, the proponent can defend by denying a premise of the opponent's argument (munaqada) or by establishing the corruption of the opponent's evidence (summary counter-evidence). This is a practical guide for dialectical exchange.

نقض إجمالي
مساوى

Key Takeaway
Mu'arada uses three logical relationships—contradictory, coextensive, or more specific—to oppose a claim without attacking the proponent's evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the claim 'Wine is forbidden because it is an intoxicant,' construct three opposing arguments using: (1) contradictory (wine is not forbidden because...), (2) coextensive (wine is something that causes intoxication, and everything that causes intoxication is...), (3) more specific (wine is a grape drink, and every grape drink is...). Be sure each opposing argument forms a valid categorical syllogism.

Etiquette of Mu'arada and Proponent's Defense
Author explains etiquette for mu'arada and defense methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to correctly phrase a mu'arada and identify the two methods a proponent can use to defend: munaqada and naqd ijmālī.

Core Idea
In mu'arada, concede the evidence without belief; defend by denying premises or showing corruption.

The passage not only gives an example of mu'arada but also provides crucial etiquette: the respondent must not say 'your evidence is established' but rather 'your evidence indicates what you claim, but I have what negates it.' This avoids self-contradiction. The proponent, in turn, can defend by munaqada (denying a premise) or naqd ijmālī (showing the evidence is corrupt). Understanding these rules ensures a coherent and effective dialectical exchange.

Guided Path
1
Examine the respondent's required wording
The respondent must say: 'Your evidence, even if it indicates what you claimed... but I have what negates it.' This phrasing concedes the evidence only in the sense of not challenging its form, not in believing its truth.
2
Understand why forbidden wording causes contradiction
If the respondent says 'even if your evidence is established' and the entailment is certain, he would be believing the evidence's truth and thus believing its conclusion, making his opposition contradictory.
3
Analyze al-Samarqandi's definition of mu'arada
Al-Samarqandi states: mu'arada is conceding the evidence without believing it, then refuting the implicans and arguing for its opposite. This preserves consistency.
4
Learn the proponent's two defense strategies
The proponent can either deny a premise of the opponent's argument (munaqada) or establish the corruption of the opponent's evidence by showing the ruling fails or entails absurdity (naqd ijmālī).
Naqd ijmālī is explained later in the third article.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
