Mu'arada: Counter-Argument in Dialectic
Author explains the concept of mu'arada and the mudallil's defensive strategies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of mu'arada (contradictory, equal, specific) and distinguish the proponent's defenses: manāqaḍa and naqḍ ijmailī.

Core Idea
Mu'arada accepts the opponent's argument but counters with a new proof, without directly denying premises.

In dialectical debate, sometimes the most effective response is to accept the opponent's argument but provide a counter-argument that leads to the opposite conclusion. This is mu'arada. In this lesson, you will learn the structure of mu'arada, its types, and how the proponent can defend against it, as explained in this passage from the Waladiyya.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proponent's original argument
Find the claim the proponent is trying to prove and the evidence he uses. In the passage, the proponent argues that a thing is not a human because it is a stone.
The proponent's argument is a simple categorical syllogism.
2
Classify the objector's counter-argument type
The objector can counter by affirming a contradictory property (e.g., rational implies human), an equal property (e.g., laughter-potential), or a more specific property (e.g., being from Abyssinia implies black). Identify which type is used.
The passage gives three distinct examples.
3
Recognize the recommended phrasing
The objector should say 'Your argument, even if it indicates what you claimed, I have something that negates it.' Avoid saying 'even if it is established' in cases of certainty.
This phrasing avoids contradiction.
4
Identify the proponent's defense
The proponent can either deny a premise of the objector's argument (manāqaḍa) or show that the objector's argument leads to non-fulfillment or absurdity (naqḍ ijmailī).
These are the two main defensive strategies.
5
Assess the effectiveness of the counter-arguments
Consider whether the proponent's defense successfully repels the mu'arada. The passage notes that mu'arada by mere label is ineffective.
Reflect on the debate dynamic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واستدل على لا إنسانية ذلك الشيء بأن هذا الشيء لا إنسان لأنه حجر وكل حجر لا إنسان فهو لا إنسان. فعارضه باثبات إنسانيته بأن يقول إنه ناطق وكل ناطق إنسان فذلك الشيء إنسان. أو باثبات ضاحكيته وهو المساوى بأن قال إنه متعجب وكل متعجب ضاحك بالقوة. أو باثبات أنه زنجي وهو الأخص بأن قال إنه إنسان من بلاد الحبشة وكل إنسان من بلاد الحبشة فهو زنجي. فيستحب للعائل عند إرادة تقرير المعارضة أن يقول للمعلل: دليلك وإن دل على ما ادعيت لكن عندي ما ينفيه. ولا يجوز للسائل أن يقول وإن ثبت أو إن صدق بدل وإن دل فيما كان استلزامه قطعيا لئلا يلزم التناقض. ودفع المحلل المعارضة إما بمنع بعض مقدمات دليل المعارض وهو المناقضة، أو باثبات المعلل فساد دليله بتخلف الحكم أو باستلزامه الفساد وهو النقض الإجمالي. وسيأتي تفصيل النقض الإجمالي. ولا ينفع المعلل في المعارضة باللقب إذ دليل المعارض حينئذ عين دليل المعلل تأمل. أو بإثبات المعلل تلك الدعوى بدليل آخر وهو المعارضة على معارضة السائل وفي كون هذه المعارضة دافعة المعارضة السائل بحث.
And he argued for the non-humanity of that thing by saying: This thing is not a human because it is a stone, and every stone is not a human, so it is not a human. So he countered it by proving its humanity by saying: It is rational, and every rational is human, so that thing is human. Or by proving its laughter-potential, which is the equal, by saying: It is capable of wonder, and everything capable of wonder has laughter-potential. Or by proving that it is a black person, which is the more specific, by saying: It is a human from the land of Abyssinia, and every human from Abyssinia is black. So it is recommended for the objector, when intending to present the counter-argument, to say to the proponent: Your argument, even if it indicates what you claimed, nevertheless I have something that negates it. And it is not permissible for the objector to say 'even if it is established' or 'even if it is true' instead of 'even if it indicates' in cases where its implication is certain, to avoid contradiction. And the proponent's defense against the counter-argument is either by denying some premises of the objector's argument, which is contradiction (manāqaḍa), or by the proponent proving the corruption of his argument through the non-fulfillment of the judgment or its implication of corruption, which is the summary refutation (naqḍ ijālī). The details of the summary refutation will come later. And the proponent does not benefit from the counter-argument by label, for then the objector's argument is exactly the proponent's argument—reflect. Or by the proponent proving that claim with another argument, which is a counter-argument against the objector's counter-argument, and whether this counter-argument repels the objector's counter-argument is subject to discussion.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exposition on the technique of muʿāraḍa (counter-argument) in debate. It opens with an example: someone argues that a thing is not human because it is a stone. The objector counters this by proving its humanity either directly (through rationality), or by an equal attribute (potential to laugh), or by a more specific attribute (being black from Abyssinia). The author then prescribes the correct phrasing for the objector: affirm the proponent's argument's validity but assert that one has a counter-argument that negates the claim. He warns against saying 'even if it is established' in cases of certainty, as that would cause contradiction. Next, the author lists the proponent's defenses: 1) manāqaḍa (denying some premises of the objector's argument), and 2) naqḍ ijālī (summary refutation) by showing the objector's argument fails in judgment or leads to absurdity. He notes that muʿāraḍa by mere label is ineffective since the objector's argument would be identical to the proponent's. Finally, he mentions that the proponent can introduce a new argument to counter the objector's counter-argument, but there is debate whether this truly repels the objection. The passage uses the example of a stone being non-human, and the counter-arguments use rational, laughing, and blackness—terms from classical logic for essential and accidental predicates.

Mu'arada is a powerful dialectical tool: it grants the opponent's argument yet overturns the conclusion via a new proof without directly denying premises.

Responding to Mu'arada with a Second Proof
Discussion on whether a second proof can avoid being countered by mu'arada.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a dialectical exchange where a second proof is used to counter a mu'arada and evaluate the strength of the response.

Core Idea
A second proof can rebut mu'arada if it is stronger or cumulatively stronger with the first.

In dialectical debate, when your evidence is countered by a mu'arada (counter-argument), you may produce a second proof to support your claim. This passage from the Waladiyya examines whether that is beneficial. It presents an objection that the second proof can be countered just like the first, and then gives an answer that the second proof might be stronger or the combination stronger. We will walk through this logical move.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dialectical positions
The reason-giver (mu'allil) has a claim and an initial proof. The objector (sa'il) presents a mu'arada that counters the proof. The reason-giver then produces a second proof.
2
Understand the objection
The objection says the second proof is also subject to the same mu'arada, so it is of no benefit.
This is a typical critical move in jadal.
3
Analyze the answer
The answer refuses to concede that the second proof is of no benefit, because it may be stronger in some respect, or the cumulative force of two proofs may be greater than one.
This shows the dialectical principle of strengthening one's position.
4
Apply the principle to jadal
Recognize that in dialectics, the proponent can defend his position by providing multiple lines of evidence; the opponent cannot simply dismiss the second proof by repeating the same counter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فى المعارضة باللقب إذ دليل المعارض حينئذ عين دليل المعلل تأمل فلا ينفعه حينئذ إلا للمعارضة على المعارضة على تقدير كونها دافعة وإنما قال على تقدير كونها دافعة المعارضة السائل لأن في دفعها إياها اختلافا حيث قال بعض الأفاضل المعارضة لا تعارض لأن المعارضة تعارض ما يعارضها أيضا وسيجيء ما يتعلق به أو لأنها إنما يدفعها إذا كان موردها الدليل وأما إذا كان موردها المدعى فلا تدفعها إن للعلل إذا سلم دليلية دليل المعارض فيعارض الدليل الثاني كما يعارض الدليل الأول ( أو بإثبات ) المعلل تلك ( الدعوى بدليل آخر ) أى بدليل غير الدليل الأول ) وهو المعارضة على معارضة السائل ) قبل وتغيير المدعى وتحريره إثبات حكما ولا يخفى مافيه ) وفي كون هذه المعارضة دافعة المعارضة السائل بحث وتقريره أن الدليل الثاني للمعلل هنا يعارضه دليل السائل المعارض كما يعارض دليله الأول وذلك ظاهر فلا فائدة فى إثبات الدعوى بدليل آخر عند معارضة السائل والجواب عنه أن يقال لا نسلم أنه لا فائدة فيه إذ يجوز أن يكون الدليل الثاني للمعلل أقوى من دليل المعارض بوجه من الوجوه ولو سلم أنه بأقوى منه فيجوز أن يكون مجموع الدليلين أقوى من دليل واحد كذا قاله أبو الفتح كذا نقل عنه.
Regarding the counter-argument by way of the description: since the evidence of the objector then is exactly the same as the evidence of the reason-giver, reflect; so then it only benefits him to counter the counter-argument on the assumption that it is repelling. And he only said 'on the assumption that it is repelling the counter-argument of the questioner' because there is disagreement in its repelling of it—where some of the eminent scholars said: the counter-argument is not countered, because the counter-argument counters that which counters it also, and its answer will come; or because it only repels it if its locus is the evidence, but if its locus is the claim, it does not repel it—since the reason-giver, if he accepts the evidentiality of the objector's evidence, the second evidence is countered just like the first evidence. Or by the reason-giver proving that claim with another evidence—i.e., an evidence other than the first—which is the counter-argument against the questioner's counter-argument. And changing the claim and its reformulation is proving a ruling, and what is in it is not hidden. And there is investigation into whether this counter-argument is repelling the questioner's counter-argument. Its articulation: the second evidence of the reason-giver here is countered by the evidence of the objector-counter as his first evidence was countered, and that is obvious; so there is no benefit in proving the claim with another evidence in the face of the questioner's counter-argument. The answer to it is to say: we do not accept that there is no benefit in it, because it is possible that the second evidence of the reason-giver is stronger than the objector's evidence in some respect; and even if it is conceded that it is not stronger than it, it is possible that the sum of the two evidences is stronger than one evidence. Thus said Abu al-Fath; thus it is transmitted from him.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This is a dialectical passage discussing the nature of mu'arada (counter-argument) in debate. The author begins by considering a specific type of mu'arada—argument by description (al-laqlab). He notes that if the objector's evidence is identical to the reason-giver's, then the only benefit is to counter the counter-argument itself, assuming the mu'arada is repelling. However, there is disagreement on whether mu'arada is repelling: some say it is not countered because it counters what counters it; others say it only repels if aimed at the evidence, not the claim. The author then presents the objection: if the reason-giver provides a second proof to support his claim, the objector's evidence counters that second proof just as it countered the first, so there is no benefit. The answer is a refusal: we do not concede the lack of benefit, because the second proof may be stronger than the objector's evidence, or the combination of two proofs may be stronger than one. This is a classic dialectical move—the reason-giver defends his second proof by claiming superior strength or cumulative force. The commentary follows the logical flow of objection (naqd) and answer (jawab), typical of jadal (dialectical) texts.

اللقب
دليل المعلل

Key Takeaway
In jadal, producing a second proof can be beneficial if it is stronger than the opponent's mu'arada or if the combination of proofs creates cumulative strength.

Two Types of Mu'arada in Jadal
Author divides counter-arguments into those against the claim and those against a premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mu'arada in the claim and mu'arada in the premise, and identify when each applies in a dialectical exchange.

Core Idea
Counter-arguments target either the claim or the premise – knowing which is key to effective dialectic.

In the discipline of jadal, after a proponent establishes a claim, the objector may respond by attacking either the claim itself or one of the premises that support it. This passage from the author provides a clear division of mu'arada into these two types, with a crucial timing condition for the first type.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Locate the two categories named in the Arabic: mu'arada fi al-mudda'a and mu'arada fi al-muqaddima. Notice that the first is directly defined, while the second is given an alternative name (munaqada) with a qualification.
The text uses 'ila' to indicate the division.
2
Understand the timing condition
For mu'arada in the claim, the author explicitly states it occurs 'after the reason-giver has proven his claim'. If the objection comes before proof, it is considered ghasb (usurpation). Reflect on why timing matters in dialectical fairness.
3
Examine the second type
Mu'arada in the premise is called munaqada 'ala tariq al-mu'arada. Parse the phrase: 'in the manner of mu'arada'. This indicates it is not genuine munaqada (contradiction) but resembles it because it targets a premise, not the conclusion.
The author uses 'tushabbihu' (resembles) in the original.
4
Distinguish from genuine munaqada
The author emphasizes that calling premise-targeting mu'arada 'munaqada' is only metaphorical. Genuine munaqada would directly contradict a claim; here the attack is indirectly on a premise. Note how this distinction prevents confusion in debate rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن المعارضة مطلقا تنقسم إلى المعارضة في المدعى (أى المعارضة المتعلقة بالمدعى) وهو أن يثبت السائل المعارض خلاف مدعى المعلل بعد إثبات المعلل مدعاه إذ قبل الاثبات يكون غصبا وإلى المعارضة في المقدمة وتسمى هذه مناقضة على طريق المعارضة يعنى أنها مشابهة للمناقضة في كون موردها مقدمة من مقدمات الدليل ويؤيده قوله على طريق المعارضة لا أنها مناقضة حقيقة.
Then the counter-argument (mu'arada) in absolute terms is divided into counter-argument in the claim (i.e., the counter-argument related to the claim)—which is that the objector-counter proves the opposite of the reason-giver's claim after the reason-giver has proven his claim, because before proof it would be usurpation (ghasb)—and counter-argument in the premise. And this second is called contradictory argument (munaqada) in the manner of counter-argument, meaning it resembles contradictory argument in that its locus is a premise from the premises of the evidence, and this is supported by his saying 'in the manner of counter-argument', not that it is truly a contradictory argument.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is a definitional classification of the types of mu'arada (counter-argument) in jadal. The author divides mu'arada into two categories: the first is mu'arada in the claim (al-mudda'a). This occurs when the objector proves the opposite of the proponent's claim after the proponent has already established it. If the objector objects before the claim is proven, it is not mu'arada but rather ghasb (usurpation), meaning an invalid intervention. The second type is mu'arada in the premise (al-muqaddima). This is called munaqada (contradictory argument) 'in the manner of mu'arada' (ala tariq al-mu'arada). This means it resembles actual munaqada because its target is a premise of the proof, but it is not fully munaqada; it is a form of mu'arada. The author emphasizes the distinction by noting 'on the way of mu'arada', indicating it is not genuine munaqada. This division is crucial for understanding how objections are structured in dialectical debate: one can attack the conclusion directly by providing a counter-evidence, or attack a premise that supports the conclusion.

Key Terms
المعارضة في المدعى
المعارضة في المقدمة
المناقضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Mu'arada is divided into two: countering the claim (after proof) and countering a premise (resembling munaqada).

Responding to Mu'arida: Mu'arada vs Naqd
Author explains how to counter a mu'arid and when mu'arada is appropriate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether to use mu'arada or naqd as a response in a dialectical exchange based on the proponent's goal.

Core Idea
The response to an objection depends on the proponent's aim: mu'arada for proof, naqd for doubt.

In the previous lesson, you learned what mu'arada is and how it functions. Now, the author discusses how to actually respond when a mu'arid presents a counterargument. He distinguishes two scenarios: when the proponent is trying to prove a claim, and when he is merely casting doubt. Understanding this distinction is crucial for effective dialectical engagement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two response strategies
Read the passage and locate the two types of responses: mu'arada (counterargument) and naqd (refutation). Note that the author also mentions munaqada (contradiction) as a stronger form of naqd.
2
Extract the condition for each response
The author states that mu'arada is appropriate when the proponent's apparent state is proof. If the proponent's goal is to cast doubt (like al-Razi), then mu'arada is not satisfactory; instead, use naqd or munaqada.
The key is the proponent's intent: proof vs. doubt.
3
Understand the rationale against rejecting mu'arada based on weakness
The author warns that simply dismissing the mu'arida because it is weak is not the practice of verifiers. He implies that one must engage with the argument substantively, showing why it leads to corruption (tarjih al-marjuh).
This prevents superficial refutations.
4
Apply to al-Razi's example
Consider al-Razi's argument for denying entailment. Since his aim was to cast doubt, mu'arada would be ineffective. The author recommends naqd or munaqada instead.
Also note that if al-Razi's apparent state was proof, mu'arada would be permissible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا عارض معارض فللخصم أن يدفعها بإثبات قوة دليله عن دليل المعارض، وفي الحقيقة باستلزام الفساد وهو رجحان المرجوح فاعرف. وأما منع كون المعارض في معرض المعارضة مستندا بضعفه فلم تجر به عادة المحققين. ومما ينبغي أن يعلم أنه إذا كان غرض المستدل إيقاع الشك لا الإثبات كاستدلال الرازي على نفي اللزوم فالمعارضة غير مرضية فالأولى دفعه بالنقض بل بالمناقضة لأن المعارضة لا تدفع الشبهة لكن لما كان ظاهر حاله الإثبات جاز الدفع بالمعارضة أيضا.
So if a mu'arid (opponent) objects, then the opponent has the option to repel it by proving the strength of his evidence over the evidence of the mu'arid, and in reality by necessitating corruption, which is the preponderance of the preponderated [i.e., preferring the weaker], so know. As for preventing the mu'arid from being in the position of opposition based on its weakness, it is not the practice of the verifiers. And among what must be known is that if the goal of the proponent is to cast doubt, not to prove, like al-Razi's argument for denying entailment, then mu'arada is not satisfactory; rather it is better to repel it by naqd (refutation) or even by munaqada (contradiction) because mu'arada does not remove the doubt. But since his apparent state is proof, it is permissible to repel with mu'arada as well.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage discusses the proper response when an opponent (mu'arid) presents a counterargument (mu'arada). The author explains that the original proponent (khasm) can rebut the mu'arida by demonstrating that his own evidence is stronger than the mu'arid's evidence. In reality, this amounts to showing that accepting the mu'arida would lead to corruption (fasad), namely the preponderance of the weaker over the stronger (tarjih al-marjuh). The author warns that one should not simply dismiss the mu'arida by claiming it is weak, as this is not the method of the verifiers (muhaqqiqun). He then makes an important distinction: if the original proponent's aim is to create doubt (shakk) rather than to prove a claim—as in al-Razi's arguments about negating entailment—then mu'arada is not appropriate; instead, one should use naqd (refutation) or munaqada (contradiction) because mu'arada does not remove the doubt. However, since the proponent's outward appearance is that of proof, replying with mu'arada is also permissible.

Key Terms
معارضة (mu'arada): A counterargument presented by an opponent against the proponent's evidence. [Note: In jadal, mu'arada is a technical form of rebuttal distinct from naqd.]
نقض (naqd): Refutation that directly invalidates the opponent's evidence or claim. [Note: Often used interchangeably with mu'arada in non-technical contexts, but here distinguished.]
رجحان المرجوح (tarjih al-marjuh): Preferring the weaker over the stronger, a logical corruption that arises from accepting a weaker counterargument. [Note: A concept in jadal to indicate that the mu'arada leads to an absurd result.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When should a proponent use naqd instead of mu'arada in a dialectical exchange?
A) When the opponent's evidence is weak
B) When the proponent's aim is to prove, not create doubt
C) When the opponent intends to cast doubt
D) When the proponent's evidence is stronger

Key Takeaway
The correct response in a dialectical exchange depends on the proponent's intent: use mu'arada when aiming to prove, and naqd when aiming to cast doubt.

The Three Types of Mu'arada: Qalb, Mithl, Ghayr
Author divides each of the two mu'arada types into three: qalb, mithl, ghayr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the three types of mu'arada (qalb, mithl, ghayr) based on the relationship of the mu'arid's evidence to the original evidence.

Core Idea
Counterarguments in jadal are classified by how the opponent's evidence relates to yours: same, similar, or different.

The author now subdivides each of the two main types of mu'arada into three subtypes: qalb (reversal), mithl (similarity), and ghayr (other). This classification depends on whether the counterargument uses evidence that is identical in matter (same middle term or excepted premise), analogous, or completely different. Understanding this division helps you recognize the strategy of your opponent in debate.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the two main types of mu'arada
Recall that there are two main types: mu'arada fi al-mudda'a (counterargument against the claim) and mu'arada fi al-muqaddima (counterargument against the premise).
2
Note the three subtypes for each main type
Each main type divides into three: mu'arada bi al-qalb (by reversal), mu'arada bi al-mithl (by similarity), and mu'arada bi al-ghayr (by other). This yields six total types.
3
Understand the basis of division
The division depends on whether the mu'arid's evidence (dalil) is identical in matter (maddatan) to the proponent's evidence. 'Identical in matter' means that the madār al-istilzām (basis of entailment) is the same: the middle term in conjunctive syllogisms, or the excepted premise in disjunctive syllogisms.
If the basis is the same, the mu'arada falls into one of the three; if different, it is mu'arada bi al-ghayr.
4
Identify the three subtypes
Qalb reverses the opponent's argument (e.g., turning the middle term into the predicate). Mithl uses an analogous proof similar in structure. Ghayr uses a completely different proof that is not identical in matter.
The author does not elaborate further here, but these are the standard definitions.
5
Apply to a real debate example
In a debate about the validity of qiyas, if the proponent argues using a syllogism with middle term 'causing intoxication', a mu'arid using qalb might reverse the middle term to the predicate, while a mu'arid using mithl might construct a parallel syllogism with a similar middle term, and ghayr would introduce a completely different argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيان أقسام المعارضة. وكل واحدة منهما (أي من المعارضة في المدعى والمعارضة في المقدمة) تنقسم إلى ثلاثة أقسام: وهي المعارضة بالقلب، والمعارضة بالمثل، والمعارضة بالغير. فالأقسام ستة. لأن دليل المعارض مطلقا إن كان عين دليل المعلل مادة، وذلك بأن يكون مدار الاستلزام واحدا وهو الحد الأوسط في الاقتراني والمقدمة الاستثنائية في الاستثنائي، إذ لا يتصور الاتحاد بينهما في غير ذلك.
Chapter on explaining the divisions of mu'arada. Each one of them (i.e., mu'arada in the claim and mu'arada in the premise) divides into three types: mu'arada by reversal (qalb), mu'arada by similarity (mithl), and mu'arada by other (ghayr). So the divisions are six. This is because the mu'arid's evidence, absolutely, if it is the same in matter as the proponent's evidence, meaning that the basis of entailment is one—the middle term in the conjunctive [syllogism] and the excepted premise in the disjunctive [syllogism]—since it is inconceivable that there be unity between them in other than that.

Commentary
This passage is a further classification of mu'arada. The author states that each of the two main types (mu'arada fi al-mudda'a and mu'arada fi al-muqaddima) divides into three subtypes: mu'arada bi al-qalb (counterargument by reversal), mu'arada bi al-mithl (counterargument by similarity), and mu'arada bi al-ghayr (counterargument by other). Thus, there are six total types. The author explains that this division is based on the relationship between the evidence of the mu'arid and the original evidence of the proponent (mu'allil). Specifically, if the mu'arid's evidence is identical in matter (maddatan) to the proponent's—meaning that the basis of entailment (madār al-istilzām) is the same: the middle term in a conjunctive syllogism (qiyas iqtirani) or the excepted premise in a disjunctive syllogism (qiyas istithna'i)—then it belongs to one of these types. The author notes that unity of matter is inconceivable in other than that. In other words, the three categories correspond to whether the mu'arid's evidence is the same (qalb? actually qalb is reversal, mithl is same in form? This needs careful reading), but the text only gives the condition for when the evidence is identical in matter. Further distinction of the three subtypes is not elaborated in this passage, but the classification sets up the discussion.

?

?

Mu'arada as Naqd: The Water Example
Defines mu'arada containing meaning of naqd with the water example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a mu'arada constitutes a naqd by recognizing that a single proof cannot yield two contradictory rulings, using the Hanafi-Shafi'i water debate as an example.

Core Idea
A single sound proof cannot establish two contradictories.

In usul al-fiqh, opponents often cite the same textual evidence to argue opposite positions. This lesson introduces the concept of mu'arada that carries the meaning of naqd (refutation), illustrated by the classic Hanafi and Shafi'i debate on water purity. Understanding this will help you distinguish genuine refutations from mere disagreements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contradictory rulings
The Hanafi says water reaching two qullahs becomes impure; the Shafi'i says it does not. Both rely on the same hadith.
2
Recognize the shared proof
Both use the same hadith: 'When water reaches two qullahs, it does not carry impurity.' The disagreement is in its interpretation.
The hadith is the single proof in question.
3
Understand the logical consequence
If the same proof yields two contradictory rulings, then the proof cannot be sound, because a sound proof does not produce contradictions.
This is the essence of naqd.
4
Distinguish the two types of naqd
The passage explains naqd by testimony of specific corruption (the proof yields contradictions) and by testimony of non-application (the proof applies to the opposite claim but the rule fails).
Both types show the proof's unsoundness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدليلين في جميع المقدمات فضلا بين الدليلين المتعارضين مدلولا . قبل هذه العينية عند التفصيل : وأما عند الأصوليين فالمقصود منها اتحادهما في اللفظ فقط. وأما المعنى المختلف فيه بين الخصمين لا يحمل أحدهما على ما يحمل عليه الآخر وإلا لما أفاد الدليل الواحد النقيضين كما قال الحنفي الماء البالغ القلتين يتنجس بملاقاة النجس لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام : ( إذا بلغ الماء القلتين لم يتحمل الخبث ) أي يضعف عن حمله فيكون مغلوبا، ويعارض الشافعي رحمه الله بأن يقول الماء البالغ القلتين لا يتنجس بملاقاة النجس لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: «الماء البالغ القلتين لم يتحمل الخبث» أى يرده ولا يقبله ولا ينقلب إلى نجس فلا يتنجس . وصورة بأن يكونا متحدين شكلا وضربا في الاقتراني ، وقيل يكفي الاتحاد شكلا ووضعا ورفعا في الاستثنائي . وحاصل هذه المعارضة إبطال دليل المعلل لأن الدليل الصحيح لا يقوم على النقيضين لاستحالة اجتماع النقيضين ففيها معنى النقض : وأما في غيرها من المعارضات فلا يتعين فيها بطلان دليل المعلل بل يعلم إجمالا أن أحد الدليلين باطل إما دليل المعلل أو دليل المعارض إلا في القسم الأخير من المعارضة بالغير، ومعنى كون هذه المعارضة في معنى النقض الإجمالي هو إما بمعنى النقض بشهادة خصوص الفساد ، بأن يقال دليلك هذا يقوم على النقيضين ، والدليل الصحيح لا يقوم على النقيضين فدليلك هذا ليس بصحيح ، وإما بمعنى النقض بشهادة التخلف بأن يقال دليلك هذا جار في نقيض مدعاك مع تخلف الحكم عنه ، وكل دليل هذا شأنه ليس بصحيح . والجواب من طرف المعلل منع الكبرى مستندا بأن دليلي ظني وتخلف الحكم عنه غير مضر ، إذ الدليل الظني غير ملزوم للمدعى ، وهذا النقض إنما يفيد له إذا كان المطلب ظنيا . وأما إذا كان يقينيا فلا مجال لمنع الكبرى . قبل قد عرفت أن دليل المعارض وإن كان عين دليل المعلل صورة ، لكنه ليس عينه في جميع المادة حتى يلزم من قيامه على الآخر قيامه على النقيضين ، بل عينه في بعض المادة وغيره في البعض الآخر ، فلا يتعين بطلان دليل المعلل ، بل يحتمل أن يكون البطلان في دليل المعارض كما إذا قلنا العالم حادث لأنه أثر القديم وكل أثر القديم حادث فعارض فلسفي بأنه أثر القديم وكل أثر القديم قديم فإن هذه المعارضة بالقلب مع أن البطلان في كبرى دليل المعارض فللمعلل أن ينقضه وأن يمنع كبراه ، فإذا لم يتعين بطلان دليل المعلل عند اتحاد الصورة فلا يتعين عند الاختلاف بالطريق الأولى ، إذ يحتمل حينئذ أن البطلان في صورة دليل المعارض ومادته .
Regarding the two proofs in all premises, a distinction between the two contradictory proofs in terms of meaning. Before this 'ayniyya at the detail: while according to the usuliyyun, the intended meaning is their unity in wording only. As for the meaning that is disputed between the two opponents, one of them does not apply to the other what he applies to the other, otherwise the single proof would not yield two contradictories, as the Hanafi said: 'Water that has reached two qullahs becomes impure by contact with impurity due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'When water reaches two qullahs, it does not carry impurity,' meaning it weakens from carrying it, thus it is overcome.' And the Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, opposes by saying: 'Water that has reached two qullahs does not become impure by contact with impurity due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'Water that has reached two qullahs does not carry impurity,' meaning it repels it and does not accept it and does not turn into impurity, so it does not become impure.' And in form, by being united in figure and mood in the conjunctive syllogism, and it is said it suffices to be united in figure, position, and mode in the conditional. The summary of this opposition is the invalidation of the opponent's proof because a sound proof does not establish two contradictories due to the impossibility of the conjunction of two contradictories, so there is in it the meaning of refutation. As for other types of opposition, the invalidity of the opponent's proof is not determined, rather it is known in general that one of the two proofs is false, either the opponent's proof or the objector's proof, except in the last section of opposition by other. And the meaning of this opposition being in the meaning of general refutation is either by way of refutation by testimony of specific corruption, by saying: 'Your proof establishes two contradictories, and a sound proof does not establish two contradictories, so your proof is not sound'; or by way of refutation by testimony of non-application, by saying: 'Your proof applies to the opposite of your claim with the rule not applying from it, and every proof with this property is not sound.' And the answer from the opponent's side is to deny the major premise, basing that on his proof being probable and the non-application of the rule from it not being harmful, for a probable proof is not necessitating for the claim. And this refutation is only beneficial for him if the matter is probable. But if it is certain, there is no scope for denying the major premise. It was said: You already know that the objector's proof, even if it is identical in form to the opponent's proof, it is not identical in all matter such that its establishment of the other necessitates its establishment of the two contradictories. Rather, it is identical in some matter and different in other parts, so the invalidity of the opponent's proof is not determined. Rather, it is possible that the falsity is in the objector's proof, as when we say: The world is originated because it is an effect of the Eternal, and every effect of the Eternal is originated. Then a philosopher objects: It is an effect of the Eternal, and every effect of the Eternal is eternal. For this opposition is by inversion, while the falsity is in the major premise of the objector's proof, so the opponent can refute it and deny its major. So if the invalidity of the opponent's proof is not determined when there is unity of form, then it is a fortiori not determined when there is difference, because then it is possible that the falsity is in the form and matter of the objector's proof.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exposition of a type of mu'arada (opposition) in usul al-fiqh that carries the meaning of naqd (refutation). It begins by clarifying that the usuliyyun (jurisprudents) often mean by 'ayn (identity) only verbal identity in the wording of two proofs, not in their meaning. Then it gives a classic example from Hanafi-Shafi'i debate on water reaching two qullahs: both cite the same hadith but differ in interpretation, leading to contradictory rulings (impurity vs. non-impurity). The author explains that such opposition (where the same proof is used for both positions) invalidates the opponent's proof because a sound proof cannot yield two contradictory conclusions—this contains the meaning of naqd. Then he distinguishes other types of mu'arada (like opposition by like or by other) where the invalidity is not specifically assigned to the opponent's proof; rather, it is known that one of the two proofs is false. He then breaks down how this type of opposition constitutes naqd in two ways: (1) by testifying to specific corruption (the proof yields contradictions), and (2) by testifying to non-application (the proof applies to the opposite of the claim with the rule not applying). The answer from the opponent (mualil) is to deny the major premise, arguing that his proof is probable (zanni), and failure of the rule to apply in the opposite case does not harm the probable proof. But if the claim is certain (yaqini), this answer is not available. The passage then refines the point: even if the objector's proof is identical in form, it may differ in matter, so the opponent's proof may not be invalid; rather the falsity might be in the objector's proof. The example of the philosopher's objection ('the world is an effect of the Eternal, and every effect of the Eternal is eternal') illustrates an inversion (qalb) of the original argument. Thus, the opponent can himself refute and deny the major. The conclusion: if invalidity is not fixed when form is identical, it is even less so when form differs, because then the falsity could be in the objector's form and matter. The passage uses fiqhi and kalami examples throughout, grounding dialectical theory in Islamic sciences.

Key Terms
معارضة
نقض
دليل ظني

Key Takeaway
When a single proof is used to derive two contradictory conclusions, it constitutes a naqd, invalidating the opponent's argument.

?

In a debate, the opponent claims your proof is invalid because it leads to a contradiction. Using the water example, determine whether you can respond by denying the major premise based on the probability of your proof.

Denying the Major Premise: Probable vs Certain Proofs
Explains the response by denying the major premise and its condition of probability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when denying the major premise (man' al-kubra) is a valid response to a mu'arada that is a naqd, based on whether the proof is probable (zanni) or certain (yaqini).

Core Idea
A probable proof can be defended by denying the major premise; a certain proof cannot.

In the heat of dialectical debate, an opponent may try to refute your argument by showing it leads to a contradiction. This lesson explores the specific defense of denying the major premise, which is only available when your proof is probable (zanni), not certain (yaqini). Understanding this distinction is crucial for constructing robust arguments in Islamic legal theory.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two types of naqd
The naqd may be by testimony of specific corruption (contradiction) or by testimony of non-application (rule fails in a specific case).
2
Identify the opponent's response
The opponent (mualil) responds by denying the major premise of the objector's argument.
The major premise is the universal statement in the objector's syllogism.
3
Understand the basis for denial
The opponent claims his proof is zanni (probable), so the failure of the rule to apply in a specific case does not harm the argument, because a probable proof does not necessitate its conclusion.
A zanni proof is not 'lazim' (necessitating) for the claim.
4
Apply the condition
If the matter (the claim) is yaqini (certain), there is no scope for denying the major premise; the naqd stands.
This limitation protects certain proofs from being undermined by probabilistic defenses.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Then a philosopher objects: It is an effect of the Eternal, and every effect of the Eternal is eternal. For this opposition is by inversion, while the falsity is in the major premise of the objector's proof, so the opponent can refute it and deny its major. So if the invalidity of the opponent's proof is not determined when there is unity of form, then it is a fortiori not determined when there is difference, because then it is possible that the falsity is in the form and matter of the objector's proof.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exposition of a type of mu'arada (opposition) in usul al-fiqh that carries the meaning of naqd (refutation). It begins by clarifying that the usuliyyun (jurisprudents) often mean by 'ayn (identity) only verbal identity in the wording of two proofs, not in their meaning. Then it gives a classic example from Hanafi-Shafi'i debate on water reaching two qullahs: both cite the same hadith but differ in interpretation, leading to contradictory rulings (impurity vs. non-impurity). The author explains that such opposition (where the same proof is used for both positions) invalidates the opponent's proof because a sound proof cannot yield two contradictory conclusions—this contains the meaning of naqd. Then he distinguishes other types of mu'arada (like opposition by like or by other) where the invalidity is not specifically assigned to the opponent's proof; rather, it is known that one of the two proofs is false. He then breaks down how this type of opposition constitutes naqd in two ways: (1) by testifying to specific corruption (the proof yields contradictions), and (2) by testifying to non-application (the proof applies to the opposite of the claim with the rule not applying). The answer from the opponent (mualil) is to deny the major premise, arguing that his proof is probable (zanni), and failure of the rule to apply in the opposite case does not harm the probable proof. But if the claim is certain (yaqini), this answer is not available. The passage then refines the point: even if the objector's proof is identical in form, it may differ in matter, so the opponent's proof may not be invalid; rather the falsity might be in the objector's proof. The example of the philosopher's objection ('the world is an effect of the Eternal, and every effect of the Eternal is eternal') illustrates an inversion (qalb) of the original argument. Thus, the opponent can himself refute and deny the major. The conclusion: if invalidity is not fixed when form is identical, it is even less so when form differs, because then the falsity could be in the objector's form and matter. The passage uses fiqhi and kalami examples throughout, grounding dialectical theory in Islamic sciences.

منع الكبرى

According to the passage, when is denying the major premise a valid response to a naqd?

B) Only when the proof is probable (zanni).
C) Only when the proof is certain (yaqini).
D) Only when the naqd is by non-application.

Key Takeaway
The validity of denying the major premise depends on the proof being probable; certain proofs cannot use this defense.

Form vs Matter: The Qalb Example and Its Implications
Shows the refinement that identity in form does not entail identity in matter, using the qalb example from kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a mu'arada constitutes a naqd by analyzing whether the opposing proof shares both form and matter, using the kalam cosmological argument and its inversion (qalb) as an example.

Core Idea
Identity in form does not guarantee identity in matter; a mu'arada may be invalid if the objector's proof differs in matter.

Even when two arguments share the same logical form (figure and mood), they may differ in their material content (matter). This nuance is critical in determining whether a mu'arada amounts to a naqd. The passage illustrates this with the philosopher's inversion (qalb) of the kalam cosmological argument, showing that the falsity may lie in the objector's proof, not the original.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original argument
The original proof: 'The world is an effect of the Eternal; every effect of the Eternal is originated; therefore, the world is originated.'
This is a standard first-figure categorical syllogism.
2
Identify the objector's inversion (qalb)
The philosopher counters: 'The world is an effect of the Eternal; every effect of the Eternal is eternal; therefore, the world is eternal.'
The form is identical (same minor premise, same structure), but the major premise differs in matter.
3
Analyze where the falsity lies
The passage notes that the falsity is in the objector's major premise ('every effect of the Eternal is eternal'), not in the original proof. Thus, the mu'arada does not invalidate the original.
If the objector's proof is unsound, the original remains intact.
4
Generalize the principle
If invalidity is not determined when form is identical, it is even less determined when form differs (a fortiori). The falsity could be in the objector's form or matter.
This protects the original argument from being refuted by a superficially similar but materially flawed counterargument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدليلين في جميع المقدمات فضلا بين الدليلين المتعارضين مدلولا . قبل هذه العينية عند التفصيل : وأما عند الأصوليين فالمقصود منها اتحادهما في اللفظ فقط. وأما المعنى المختلف فيه بين الخصمين لا يحمل أحدهما على ما يحمل عليه الآخر وإلا لما أفاد الدليل الواحد النقيضين كما قال الحنفي الماء البالغ القلتين يتنجس بملاقاة النجس لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام : ( إذا بلغ الماء القلتين لم يتحمل الخبث ) أي يضعف عن حمله فيكون مغلوبا، ويعارض الشافعي رحمه الله بأن يقول الماء البالغ القلتين لا يتنجس بملاقاة النجس لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: «الماء البالغ القلتين لم يتحمل الخبث» أى يرده ولا يقبله ولا ينقلب إلى نجس فلا يتنجس . وصورة بأن يكونا متحدين شكلا وضربا في الاقتراني ، وقيل يكفي الاتحاد شكلا ووضعا ورفعا في الاستثنائي . وحاصل هذه المعارضة إبطال دليل المعلل لأن الدليل الصحيح لا يقوم على النقيضين لاستحالة اجتماع النقيضين ففيها معنى النقض : وأما في غيرها من المعارضات فلا يتعين فيها بطلان دليل المعلل بل يعلم إجمالا أن أحد الدليلين باطل إما دليل المعلل أو دليل المعارض إلا في القسم الأخير من المعارضة بالغير، ومعنى كون هذه المعارضة في معنى النقض الإجمالي هو إما بمعنى النقض بشهادة خصوص الفساد ، بأن يقال دليلك هذا يقوم على النقيضين ، والدليل الصحيح لا يقوم على النقيضين فدليلك هذا ليس بصحيح ، وإما بمعنى النقض بشهادة التخلف بأن يقال دليلك هذا جار في نقيض مدعاك مع تخلف الحكم عنه ، وكل دليل هذا شأنه ليس بصحيح . والجواب من طرف المعلل منع الكبرى مستندا بأن دليلي ظني وتخلف الحكم عنه غير مضر ، إذ الدليل الظني غير ملزوم للمدعى ، وهذا النقض إنما يفيد له إذا كان المطلب ظنيا . وأما إذا كان يقينيا فلا مجال لمنع الكبرى . قبل قد عرفت أن دليل المعارض وإن كان عين دليل المعلل صورة ، لكنه ليس عينه في جميع المادة حتى يلزم من قيامه على الآخر قيامه على النقيضين ، بل عينه في بعض المادة وغيره في البعض الآخر ، فلا يتعين بطلان دليل المعلل ، بل يحتمل أن يكون البطلان في دليل المعارض كما إذا قلنا العالم حادث لأنه أثر القديم وكل أثر القديم حادث فعارض فلسفي بأنه أثر القديم وكل أثر القديم قديم فإن هذه المعارضة بالقلب مع أن البطلان في كبرى دليل المعارض فللمعلل أن ينقضه وأن يمنع كبراه ، فإذا لم يتعين بطلان دليل المعلل عند اتحاد الصورة فلا يتعين عند الاختلاف بالطريق الأولى ، إذ يحتمل حينئذ أن البطلان في صورة دليل المعارض ومادته .
Regarding the two proofs in all premises, a distinction between the two contradictory proofs in terms of meaning. Before this 'ayniyya at the detail: while according to the usuliyyun, the intended meaning is their unity in wording only. As for the meaning that is disputed between the two opponents, one of them does not apply to the other what he applies to the other, otherwise the single proof would not yield two contradictories, as the Hanafi said: 'Water that has reached two qullahs becomes impure by contact with impurity due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'When water reaches two qullahs, it does not carry impurity,' meaning it weakens from carrying it, thus it is overcome.' And the Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, opposes by saying: 'Water that has reached two qullahs does not become impure by contact with impurity due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'Water that has reached two qullahs does not carry impurity,' meaning it repels it and does not accept it and does not turn into impurity, so it does not become impure.' And in form, by being united in figure and mood in the conjunctive syllogism, and it is said it suffices to be united in figure, position, and mode in the conditional. The summary of this opposition is the invalidation of the opponent's proof because a sound proof does not establish two contradictories due to the impossibility of the conjunction of two contradictories, so there is in it the meaning of refutation. As for other types of opposition, the invalidity of the opponent's proof is not determined, rather it is known in general that one of the two proofs is false, either the opponent's proof or the objector's proof, except in the last section of opposition by other. And the meaning of this opposition being in the meaning of general refutation is either by way of refutation by testimony of specific corruption, by saying: 'Your proof establishes two contradictories, and a sound proof does not establish two contradictories, so your proof is not sound'; or by way of refutation by testimony of non-application, by saying: 'Your proof applies to the opposite of your claim with the rule not applying from it, and every proof with this property is not sound.' And the answer from the opponent's side is to deny the major premise, basing that on his proof being probable and the non-application of the rule from it not being harmful, for a probable proof is not necessitating for the claim. And this refutation is only beneficial for him if the matter is probable. But if it is certain, there is no scope for denying the major premise. It was said: You already know that the objector's proof, even if it is identical in form to the opponent's proof, it is not identical in all matter such that its establishment of the other necessitates its establishment of the two contradictories. Rather, it is identical in some matter and different in other parts, so the invalidity of the opponent's proof is not determined. Rather, it is possible that the falsity is in the objector's proof, as when we say: The world is originated because it is an effect of the Eternal, and every effect of the Eternal is originated. Then a philosopher objects: It is an effect of the Eternal, and every effect of the Eternal is eternal. For this opposition is by inversion, while the falsity is in the major premise of the objector's proof, so the opponent can refute it and deny its major. So if the invalidity of the opponent's proof is not determined when there is unity of form, then it is a fortiori not determined when there is difference, because then it is possible that the falsity is in the form and matter of the objector's proof.

8 min
Every Opposition Contains a Refutation
Argues that every opposition contains a meaning of refutation and introduces inversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical mechanism by which an opposition (mu'arada) implies a refutation (naqd) and distinguish qalb from other types.

Core Idea
Every opposition either specifically refutes the opponent's proof or shows one proof is false.

In this lesson, we examine the central claim that every opposition (mu'arada) carries a meaning of refutation (naqd). The passage explains why: the objector essentially says 'your proof is invalid because I have a proof that negates your claim.' We will learn how the form and matter of proofs determine whether the refutation is specific or general.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key claim
Find the statement 'في كل معارضة معنى النقض' and its reasoning: opposition declares the opponent's proof invalid because a counterproof negates the claim.
2
Distinguish types of invalidity
The invalidity is either essential (for certain proofs) or descriptive (for probable proofs). Understand the difference.
Essential invalidity: the proof itself is flawed; descriptive: only its reliability is weakened.
3
Understand form and matter
The objector can convert opposition to a specific naqd if the proofs are materially identical (same premises in essence). If not, only general falsity is known.
Material identity means the same underlying facts even if wording differs.
4
Examine the example of qalb
Inversion (qalb) is a special opposition where the objector uses the opponent's own proof to derive the opposite conclusion, often in universally occurring sophisms.
The addition of explanatory premises does not break the inversion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال والتحقيق أن في كل معارضة معنى النقض، لأن المعارضة بمنزلة أن يقال دليلك هذا باطل لأنه جار في مدعاك مع تخلف الحكم عنه لأن عندي دليلا ينفي مدعاك، وكل دليل شأنه كذا فهو باطل إما ذاتا أو صفة، الأول في الدليل اليقيني، والثاني في الدليل الظني: إذ يبطل دليليته بالاعتقاد نقيض مدلوله بل بالشك، فإذا بدل المعارض معارضته إلى النقض فليس للمعلل إلا منع التخلف مستندا الدليلين في جميع المقدمات فضلا بين الدليلين المتعارضين مدلولا، فالقيد الكاشف في أحدهما والتغاير من تغاير المدلولين لا يمنع اتحادهما مادة عرفا. وصورة بأن يتحدا شكلا وضربا في الاقتراني، وقيل يكفي الاتحاد شكلا ووضعا ورفعا في الاستثنائي . بجواز بطلان دليل المعارض سواء كان دليل المعارض ظنيا أو يقينيا خذ هذا وكن من الشاكرين أقول: ونعم ما آتاه لو كان من عند نفسه. قال الشارح الحنفي اللزوم معتبر في مطلق الدليل أو للتناول لهما، فكيف يكون العقلي ملزوما والنقلي غير ملزوم: وبالجملة الفرق ليس على ما ينبغي. كما أي كالمعارضة الواقعة في المغالطات العامة الورود على جميع الأشياء من للمطالب التصديقية النظرية تسمى تلك المعارضة قلبا ومعارضة على سبيل القلب لقلب المعارض ذلك الدليل على المعلل بأن يقسم على نقيض مدعاه أو ما يستلزمه وزيادة دليل المعارض بما يفيد تقريرا وتفسيرا لا تبديلا ولا تغييرا لا تقدح في كون معارضته قلبا كذا في التلويح. والمغالطة هي قياس فاسد إما من جهة الصورة بأن لا يكون على هيئة منتجة لعدم شرط من شروطه المعتبرة بحسب الكيف والكم والجهة، وإما من جهة المادة بأن يكون المطلوب وبعض المقدمات شيئا واحدا أو بأن يكون بعض المقدمات كاذبة شبهة بالصادقة لفظا أو معنى؛ ويكفيك هذا الإجمال. كما في المغالطات العامة الورود، تسمى تلك المعارضة قلبا ومعارضة على سبيل القلب لقلب المعارض ذلك الدليل على المعلل. والمغالطة هي قياس فاسد إما من جهة الصورة بأن لا يكون على هيئة منتجة لعدم شرط من الشروط المعتبرة بحسب الكيف والكم والجهة، وإما من جهة المادة بأن يكون المطلوب وبعض المقدمات شيئا واحدا، أو بأن يكون بعض المقدمات كاذبة شبهة بالصادقة لفظا أو معنى: قال في الحاشية وحاصل هذه المعارضة إبطال دليل المعلل، لأن الدليل الصحيح لا يقوم على النقيضين لاستحالة اجتماع النقيضين: وأما في غيرها من المعارضات فلا يتعين فيها بطلان دليل المعلل، بل يعلم إجمالا أن أحد الدليلين باطل إما دليل المعلل أو دليل المعارض إلا في القسم الأخير من المعارضة بالغير تأمل انتهى: يعني أن حاصلها إبطال دليل المعلل بقيامه على النقيضين: أما قيامه على أحد النقيضين فمسلم: وأما قيامه على الآخر فلأنه عين دليل المعارض وهو قائم على الآخر، والدليل الصحيح لا يقوم على النقيضين لاستحالة اجتماع النقيضين، ففيها معنى النقيض: أي نقض دليل المعلل باستلزامه الفساد، وهو اجتماع النقيضين، ولذا سماها الأصوليون معارضة فيها معنى المناقضة، إذ المناقضة في عرفهم هو النقض الإجمالي وفي غيرها من المعارضات وهي المعارضة بالمثل والمعارضة بالغير، فلا يتعين فيها بطلان دليل المعلل لكونه غير دليل المعارض بلى يعلم إجمالا أن أحد الدليلين باطل إما دليل المعلل أو دليل المعارض لكون مدلولها متناقضين إلا في القسم الأخير من المناقضة بالغير، وهو ما كان غير دليل المعلل صورة وعينه مادة فانه يتعين فهم بطلان دليله أيضا لكون مدار الاستلزام فيه مستلزما للنقيضين. أما استلزامه أحدهما فمسلم، وأما استلزامه الآخر فلأنه عين مدار الاستلزام في دليل المعارض وهو مستلزم للآخر: أقول قد عرفت أن دليل المعارض عند القلب وإن كان عين دليل المعلل صورة لكنه ليس عينه في جميع المادة حتى يلزم من قيامه على الآخر قيامه على النقيضين، بل عينه في بعض المادة وغيره في بعض آخر فلا يتعين بطلان دليل المعلل. بل يحتمل أن يكون البطلان في دليل المعارض كما إذا قلنا العالم حادث لأنه أثر القديم وكل أثر قديم حادث فعارض الفلسفي بأنه قديم لأنه أثر القديم وكل أثر القديم قديم فان هذه
Then he said: And the verification is that every opposition contains the meaning of refutation, because opposition is like saying: This proof of yours is invalid because it applies to your claim while the rule is absent from it, because I have a proof that negates your claim, and every proof with this property is invalid either essentially or descriptively, the first in certain proofs and the second in probable proofs: since its being a proof is invalidated by believing the opposite of its content, indeed by doubt. So if the objector converts his opposition into a refutation, the opponent has nothing except to deny the non-application, basing on the two proofs in all the premises, a distinction between the two contradictory proofs in meaning. So the restrictive qualifier in one of them and the difference due to the difference of the two meanings do not prevent their material unity customarily. And in form, by being united in figure and mood in the conjunctive, and it is said it suffices to be united in figure, position, and mode in the conditional. With the permissibility of the invalidity of the objector's proof, whether the objector's proof is probable or certain, take this and be among the thankful. I say: And excellent is what he gave if it were from himself. The Hanafi commentator said: Necessity is considered in absolute proof or for encompassing both, so how can the rational be necessitating and the transmitted not necessitating? In summary, the difference is not as it should be. Like, i.e., like the opposition occurring in the universally occurring sophisms upon all things from among the theoretical assertoric claims; that opposition is called inversion and opposition by way of inversion, for the objector inverts that proof against the opponent by dividing upon the opposite of his claim or what necessitates it, and the addition of the objector's proof that provides affirmation and interpretation, not substitution nor change, does not detract from its being an inversion; so in al-Talwih. And the sophism is a faulty syllogism, either in terms of form, by not being in a productive figure due to the absence of a condition from its considered conditions according to quality, quantity, and modality; or in terms of matter, by the desired conclusion and some premises being one thing, or by some premises being false resembling the true literally or in meaning; and this summary suffices you. As in the universally occurring sophisms, that opposition is called inversion and opposition by way of inversion for the objector inverts that proof against the opponent. And the sophism is a faulty syllogism... [repeated]. He said in the supercommentary: The summary of this opposition is the invalidation of the opponent's proof, because a sound proof does not establish the two contradictories due to the impossibility of the conjunction of two contradictories. As for other oppositions, the invalidity of the opponent's proof is not determined, rather it is known in general that one of the two proofs is false, either the opponent's proof or the objector's proof, except in the last section of opposition by other; ponder, end. Meaning: its summary is the invalidation of the opponent's proof by its establishing the two contradictories: as for its establishing one of the two contradictories, it is conceded; and as for its establishing the other, it is because it is identical to the objector's proof and it establishes the other, and a sound proof does not establish the two contradictories due to the impossibility of the conjunction of two contradictories, so there is in it the meaning of contradiction, i.e., refutation of the opponent's proof by its necessitating corruption, which is the conjunction of the two contradictories. That is why the usuliyyun called it an opposition containing the meaning of contradiction, for contradiction in their idiom is the general refutation. And in other oppositions, which are opposition by like and opposition by other, the invalidity of the opponent's proof is not determined because it is not the objector's proof; rather, it is known in general that one of the two proofs is false, either the opponent's proof or the objector's proof, because their implications are contradictory, except in the last section of contradiction by other, which is what is other than the opponent's proof in form but identical in matter, for there the invalidity of his proof is determined as well, because the ground of necessitation in it necessitates the two contradictories. As for its necessitating one of them, it is conceded; and as for its necessitating the other, it is because it is the very ground of necessitation in the objector's proof and it necessitates the other. I say: You already know that the objector's proof in inversion, even if it is identical in form to the opponent's proof, it is not identical in all matter such that its establishment of the other necessitates its establishment of the two contradictories. Rather, it is identical in some matter and different in other parts, so the invalidity of the opponent's proof is not determined. Rather, it is possible that the falsity is in the objector's proof, as when we say: The world is originated because it is an effect of the Eternal, and every effect of the Eternal is originated. Then the philosopher objects: It is eternal because it is an effect of the Eternal, and every effect of the Eternal is eternal. For this...
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a dense commentary that brings together several points from the earlier discussion. It begins by asserting a strong claim: every mu'arada (opposition) contains a meaning of naqd (refutation). The reasoning is that opposition essentially says: 'Your proof is invalid because it applies to your claim while the rule is absent, since I have a proof that negates your claim. Any proof with this property is invalid.' This invalidity is either essential (for certain proofs) or descriptive (for probable proofs). The objector can convert his opposition into a direct naqd, leaving the opponent only the option to deny the non-application by showing a distinction between the two proofs in their premises and meaning. Even if the form is identical (same figure and mood in categorical, or same figure, position, mode in conditional), the matter may be different. The passage then discusses the permissibility of the invalidity of the objector's proof itself. A Hanafi commentator is quoted questioning the distinction between rational and transmitted proofs regarding necessity. Then the text defines a specific type of opposition—the inversion (qalb)—that occurs in universally occurring sophisms. This inversion is when the objector takes the opponent's proof and uses it to derive the opposite conclusion. The addition of explanation does not change its being an inversion. Then the definition of sophism (mughalata) is given: a faulty syllogism, either formally (missing a condition for productivity in quality, quantity, or modality) or materially (the conclusion or premises are identical, or premises are false but resemble truth). This definition is repeated, possibly due to scribal duplication. After that, a supercommentary (hashiya) rephrases the original point: the summary of this opposition is the invalidation of the opponent's proof because a sound proof cannot yield contradictories. In other types of opposition (by like or by other), the invalidity is not specific to the opponent's proof; only in the last type (opposition by other where form differs but matter is identical) is the opponent's proof clearly invalid. The passage ends with a restatement of the earlier point about inversion: the objector's proof may not be identical in all matter, so the opponent's proof might not be invalid; the falsity could be in the objector's proof, as in the example of the world's origination vs. eternity. This passage integrates earlier discussions and reinforces the need to examine both form and matter in dialectical exchanges. It also defines sophism explicitly, linking it to faulty syllogisms in the logical tradition.

Key Terms

نقض
قلب
مناقضة

Dialectic -- Jadal
intermediate

Definition of Sophism (Mughalata)
Defines sophism as a faulty syllogism with formal or material defects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given syllogism is a sophism (mughalata) by checking its form (conditions for productivity) and matter (truth of premises and circularity).

Core Idea
A sophism is a faulty syllogism due to formal invalidity or material unsoundness.

This lesson presents the standard definition of sophism (mughalata) in Islamic logic. The definition covers two dimensions: formal defects (violations of syllogistic rules) and material defects (false premises or circular reasoning). Mastering this definition is crucial for detecting fallacies in theological and legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Understand the genus
The genus of sophism is 'faulty syllogism' (قياس فاسد). It is a syllogism, not a single proposition or judgment.
2
Identify formal defects
A syllogism is formally faulty if it fails to meet conditions for productivity in quality, quantity, and modality (e.g., invalid figure or mood).
Conditions include correct distribution of terms, proper order, etc.
3
Identify material defects
A syllogism is materially faulty if the conclusion or some premises are identical (circular) or if premises are false but resemble truth (subterfuge).
False premises that appear true can be due to ambiguity or false analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

المقدمات كاذبة شبهة بالصادقة لفظا أو معنى؛ ويكفيك هذا الإجمال. كما في المغالطات العامة الورود، تسمى تلك المعارضة قلبا ومعارضة على سبيل القلب لقلب المعارض ذلك الدليل على المعلل. والمغالطة هي قياس فاسد إما من جهة الصورة بأن لا يكون على هيئة منتجة لعدم شرط من الشروط المعتبرة بحسب الكيف والكم والجهة، وإما من جهة المادة بأن يكون المطلوب وبعض المقدمات شيئا واحدا، أو بأن يكون بعض المقدمات كاذبة شبهة بالصادقة لفظا أو معنى: قال في الحاشية وحاصل هذه المعارضة إبطال دليل المعلل، لأن الدليل الصحيح لا يقوم على النقيضين لاستحالة اجتماع النقيضين: وأما في غيرها من المعارضات فلا يتعين فيها بطلان دليل وهو اجتماع النقيضين، ولذا سماها الأصوليون معارضة فيها معنى المناقضة، إذ المناقضة في عرفهم هو النقض الإجمالي وفي غيرها من المعارضات وهي المعارضة بالمثل والمعارضة بالغير، فلا يتعين فيها بطلان دليل المعلل لكونه غير دليل المعارض بلى يعلم إجمالا أن أحد الدليلين باطل إما دليل المعلل أو دليل المعارض لكون مدلولها متناقضين إلا في القسم الأخير من المناقضة بالغير، وهو ما كان غير دليل المعلل صورة وعينه Then he said: And the verification is that every opposition contains the meaning of refutation, because opposition is like saying: This proof of yours is invalid because it applies to your claim while the rule is absent from it, because I have a proof that negates your claim, and every proof with this property is invalid either essentially or descriptively, the first in certain proofs and the second in probable proofs: since its being a proof is invalidated by believing the opposite of its content, indeed by doubt. So if the objector converts his opposition into a refutation, the opponent has nothing except to deny the non-application, basing on the two proofs in all the premises, a distinction between the two contradictory proofs in meaning. So the restrictive qualifier in one of them and the difference due to the difference of the two meanings do not prevent their material unity customarily. Commentary
This passage is a dense commentary that brings together several points from the earlier discussion. It begins by asserting a strong claim: every mu'arada (opposition) contains a meaning of naqd (refutation). The reasoning is that opposition essentially says: 'Your proof is invalid because it applies to your claim while the rule is absent, since I have a proof that negates your claim. Analyzing the General Fallacy and Its Reversal
Author provides an example of a general fallacy and the dialectical exchange using reversal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a general fallacy (mu'arada 'amma) and analyze a dialectical exchange involving reversal (qalb).

Core Idea
A general fallacy forces a conclusion from a disjunction; reversal exposes its weakness by proving the opposite.

In dialectic, a proponent may attempt to prove a claim using a disjunctive argument where both alternatives lead to the same conclusion, making it seem inescapable. However, the opponent can counter by using the same logical form to derive the opposite conclusion, demonstrating the argument's invalidity. This passage provides a concrete example and the proper dialectical response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proponent's argument structure
Recognize that the argument uses a disjunctive premise: the subject is either existent or non-existent, and in both cases the desired conclusion follows. This is a form of constructive dilemma.
The structure is (p ∨ q), (p → r), (q → r) ⊢ r.
2
Understand the opponent's reversal
The opponent constructs a parallel argument using a different subject (tree) to prove the opposite conclusion (non-animal), showing the argument form can be used to support contradictory claims.
This is an example of mu'arada bi al-qalb.
3
Analyze the proponent's response
The proponent can either choose one disjunct (non-existence) and deny the conclusion follows from it, or challenge the opponent's major premise. This shows that the reversal does not automatically refute the original argument.
The passage says: 'We choose that it is non-existent and do not admit the establishment of the desired conclusion.'
4
Locate the defect
The passage states the defect is in the opponent's major premise, meaning the opponent's argument is invalid; the proponent can deny its major premise.
The major premise is the conditional linking the disjuncts to the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مثال المغالطة العامة الورود مثل أن يقال الشيء الذي يكون وجوده وعدمه مستلزما للمطلوب إما موجود وإما معدوم وأيا ما كان يلزم ثبوت المطلوب. فيقول السائل المعارض هذا الدليل إن دل على ما ادعيته لكن عندي ما يدل على خلافه وهو أن اللاحيوان ثابت لأنه إذا كان الشجر الذي يستلزم وجوده وعدمه اللاحيوان ثابتا كان اللاحيوان ثابتا لكن أحدهما ثابت فاللاحيوان ثابت. ويجاب عنه بأنا نختار أنه معدوم ولا نسلم ثبوت المطلوب لأنا نختار أنه معدوم ذاته وصفته التي هي استلزام عدمه المطلوب. المعارضة معارضة بالقلب مع أن البطلان في كبرى دليل المعارض، فللمعلل أن ينقضه وأن يمنع كبراه.
An example of a general fallacy is to say: The thing whose existence and non-existence both entail the desired conclusion: it is either existent or non-existent, and whichever it is, the desired conclusion follows. The opposing objector says: This proof, if it proves what you claim, but I have something that proves the opposite, which is that non-animal is established, because if the tree whose existence and non-existence both entail non-animal is established, then non-animal is established; but one of them is established, so non-animal is established. The response to him is: We choose that it is non-existent, and we do not admit the establishment of the desired conclusion, because we choose that it is non-existent in itself and its attribute which is that its non-existence entails the desired conclusion. The counter-argument is a counter-argument by reversal, and the defect is in the major premise of the opponent's proof, so the proponent can refute it and deny its major premise.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage presents a concrete example of a general fallacy and the dialectical exchange it produces. The fallacy is based on a disjunctive conditional proof where both existence and non-existence of the subject entail the same conclusion, making the conclusion inescapable. The opponent then uses the same logical form to prove the opposite conclusion, showing the proof is invalid because it can be reversed. The proponent's response is to choose one of the two disjuncts (non-existence) and deny that the conclusion follows from it, or alternatively to challenge the opponent's major premise. This illustrates the nature of reversal (qalb) in dialectic.

Key Terms
معارضة
قلب
نقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the opponent's counter-argument, what is the subject that leads to the conclusion 'non-animal is established'?
A) Human
B) Tree
C) Animal
D) Non-animal

Key Takeaway
A general fallacy can be countered by reversal, but the proponent can defend by denying the implication of a disjunct or by attacking the opponent's premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a study circle, a student argues: 'Either the Quran is created or uncreated. If it is created, it is a contingent thing. If it is uncreated, it is God's attribute. Both lead to the conclusion that the Quran is not the same as God. Therefore, the Quran is distinct from God.' Construct a reversal using the same logical form to argue that the Quran is identical to God (or at least not distinct). Then, as the original proponent, respond to your own reversal by choosing one disjunct and denying the implication.
?
Identify the major premise in the opponent's counter-argument in the passage (the conditional 'if the tree's existence entails non-animal...'). Is it plausibly true? How would you deny it? Discuss in a dialectical exchange.

Recognizing a Conditional Argument in Dialectical Exchange
Author shows dialectical exchange using a fallacious argument for the world's eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a conditional syllogism used in a kalam debate and recognize when an argument is turned against itself.

Core Idea
A conditional argument can be mirrored to reach the opposite conclusion.

In kalam debates, both sides often use the same logical form. This passage shows a philosopher arguing for the world's eternity using a conditional syllogism, and the opponent retorting with the same form for temporal origination. You need to see the pattern and evaluate whether the counterargument refutes the original.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
Look for the 'if...then...' structure. The philosopher says: 'If the thing whose existence and non-existence entail the eternity of the world is established, then the world is eternal.' This is a conditional statement.
The antecedent is a claim about the 'thing' being established.
2
Extract the antecedent and consequent
Antecedent: 'The thing whose existence and non-existence entail the eternity of the world is established.' Consequent: 'The world is eternal.'
The argument then affirms the antecedent: 'But one of them is established.'
3
Note the opponent's substitution
The opponent replaces 'eternity' with 'temporal origination' and uses the identical form: 'If the thing... entail temporal origination... then the world is temporal.'
This shows the argument is reversible if the 'thing' is ambiguous.
4
See the dialectical move
The opponent does not directly refute; rather, they show that the same form yields a contradictory conclusion. This is a common dialectical tactic.
The passage later resolves by identifying the 'thing' as non-existent, but here the focus is on the structure.
5
Evaluate whether this refutes the original
The counterargument does not prove the world is temporal; it only shows the original argument can be used for both. A valid refutation would require showing a flaw in the premises or form.
This highlights the need for careful analysis of the terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول فاذا استدل به (أى بذلك الدليل) الفلسفى على قدم العالم بأن قال إذا كان الشيء الذي يستلزم وجوده وعدمه قدم العالم ثابتا كان العالم قديما لكن أحدهما ثابت فالعالم قديم فتعارضه (أى الفلسفى) بالاستدلال به على حدوثه (أى على حدوث العالم) بأن يقال العالم حادث لأنه إذا كان الشيء الذي يستلزم وجوده وعدمه حدوث العالم ثابتا كان العالم حادثا لكن أحدهما ثابت فهو حادث.
I say: So if the philosopher argues by it (i.e., by that argument) for the eternity of the world, saying: If the thing whose existence and non-existence entail the eternity of the world is established, then the world is eternal. But one of them is established, so the world is eternal. Then you (i.e., the philosopher) counter it by arguing with it for its temporal origination (i.e., for the temporal origination of the world), saying: The world is temporally originated because if the thing whose existence and non-existence entail the temporal origination of the world is established, then the world is temporally originated. But one of them is established, so it is temporally originated.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage describes a dialectical exchange where the philosopher uses a conditional argument for the eternity of the world. The opponent counters by using the very same argument form but substituting 'temporal origination' (huduth) for 'eternity' (qidam), thereby claiming the world is temporally originated. This illustrates the use of a fallacious argument that can be turned against itself. The text later notes that such counterarguments can be answered by choosing that the 'thing' is non-existent, but here the focus is on the dialectical move.

A conditional argument can be mirrored by switching the conclusion, highlighting the need for precise terms in dialectic.
